### `2026-04-06T20:42:54.497Z`

> ---
>   Process adjustments from this session (read these)
> 
>   Three new feedback memories were created. They change how you operate:
> 
>   1. feedback_accept_as_is — When the roundtable recommends "accept as-is" for a design property, do NOT treat it
>    as resolved. Present it to Lane as a recommendation with the counter-argument. Lane's standard is
>   correct-by-construction; "the routing is correct so this is fine" is not acceptable.
>   2. feedback_stress_test_freshness — After ANY production change to wire format, codec, or dispatch, run cargo
>   test -- --ignored and verify stress test assertions match the NEW behavior. Tests written against old behavior
>   can pass with loose assertions while their comments and intent are wrong.
>   3. feedback_per_pane_threading — pane IS per-pane threading (same as Be's per-window model). Blocking within a
>   pane's looper is legitimate backpressure, not an I2 violation. I2 prevents cross-pane interference, which
>   per-pane threading already provides. Don't recommend try_send where blocking send is correct.
> 
>   EOS workflow is now formalized in PLAN.md Session End Checklist (11 items in Verify/Update/Handoff/Process
>   sections). Follow it at the end of your session.
> 
>   ---
>   That's the complete memo. Ready to relay.

>   Design decisions to know
> 
>   - RequestProtocol is a supertrait, not an associated type with default. Three rounds, unanimous after
>   evaluating A (default), A-twist (marker trait), and B (supertrait). B won because stable Rust has no associated
>    type defaults, and the supertrait IS the marker — fewer concepts.
>   - ReplyPort is closure-erased. One type, not two. Backend (in-process mpsc, wire serialization) captured in
>   closure at construction. Handler never knows which backend. T: Serialize bound on wire constructor only.
>   - Wire format is u16 session discriminant. Monotonic, no recycling. 65534 limit resolved by Phase 2
>   reconnect-on-exhaustion (session composition S₁;S₂).
>   - Bounded write channel (128). Backpressure via SyncSender. Blocking send is legitimate within a pane —
>   per-pane threading isolates. ServiceHandle::Drop uses try_send (non-blocking best-effort).
>   - Protocol tag in service payloads. XOR-fold of ServiceId UUID bytes to 1 byte. Checked by
>   make_service_receiver before deserialization. Defense-in-depth against routing bugs.
> 
>   Process adjustments from this session — READ THESE
> 
>   Three feedback memories were created that change how you operate:
> 
>   1. feedback_accept_as_is — When the roundtable recommends "accept as-is" for a design property, do NOT treat it
>    as resolved. Present it to Lane as a recommendation with the counter-argument. Lane's standard is
>   correct-by-construction; "the routing is correct so this is fine" is not acceptable.
>   2. feedback_stress_test_freshness — After ANY production change to wire format, codec, or dispatch, run cargo
>   test -- --ignored and verify stress test assertions match the NEW behavior. Tests written against old behavior
>   can pass with loose assertions while their comments and intent are wrong.
>   3. feedback_per_pane_threading — pane IS per-pane threading (same as Be's per-window model). Blocking within a
>   pane's looper is legitimate backpressure, not an I2 violation. I2 prevents cross-pane interference, which
>   per-pane threading already provides. Don't recommend try_send where blocking send is correct.

>   sections). Follow it at the end of your session.

>   What to do next — Tasks 3-6
> 
>   These complete request/reply dispatch. The four-agent roundtable already designed the full architecture across
>   3 rounds. The consensus is documented in serena and in this memo. Implement one task at a time, review between.
> 
>   Task 3: ServiceDispatch request closure slot
>   - Add RequestReceiver<H> type (closure that takes handler + messenger + payload bytes + wire reply port)
>   - make_request_receiver<H, P>() factory — deserializes P::Message, constructs typed ReplyPort<P::Reply> via
>   wire_reply_port, calls HandlesRequest::receive_request
>   - ServiceDispatch gains a second HashMap<u16, RequestReceiver> for request closures
>   - PaneBuilder::open_service registers the request closure when H: HandlesRequest
> 
>   Task 4: LooperCore Request dispatch (provider-side A1)
>   - dispatch_service Request branch: extract token from ServiceFrame::Request, construct wire ReplyPort via
>   wire_reply_port(write_tx.clone(), session_id, token), call request closure in ServiceDispatch
>   - Wrap in catch_unwind like Notification path
> 
>   Task 5: Consumer-side send_request wiring (A2 part 1)
>   - ServiceHandle::send_request gets token from Dispatch::insert (delete global NEXT_TOKEN)
>   - Messenger carries &mut Dispatch during handler dispatch (roundtable consensus: co-locate insert + token
>   alloc, no lost-reply race)
>   - send_request installs Dispatch entry BEFORE frame hits wire
> 
>   Task 6: LooperCore Reply/Failed routing (A2 part 2)
>   - dispatch_service Reply branch: deserialize payload, call dispatch.fire_reply(connection, token, handler,
>   messenger, payload)
>   - dispatch_service Failed branch: call dispatch.fire_failed(connection, token, handler, messenger)

> 
> 1. df47b17 — Known issues from previous session: token comments, EAct citation, pane-hello migrated to

>    deliberation chose option B: separate trait RequestProtocol: Protocol { type Reply: Message; } over single
>    trait with default. HandlesRequest receives ReplyPort<P::Reply>.
> 6. 8a7fc2d — 17 stress tests + found/fixed bug: dispatch_service missing catch_unwind (malformed inner payload
>    panicked through run())
> 7. 6734f50 — S3 fix: codec self-poison (AtomicBool, Poisoned variant). S1 fix: Dispatch::fail_session + server
>    echoes ServiceTeardown to revoker + LooperCore handles ServiceTeardown
> 8. 7f2c9a2 — S9: u8→u16 session discriminant (65534 sessions, monotonic). Wire format change.
> 9. cc247c6 — S8: protocol tag (XOR-fold ServiceId UUID, 1 byte) prepended to service payloads, checked before
>    deserialization
> 10. f167231 — Updated S3 stress tests for poison fix + u16 wire format
> 11. 3f8dac6 — 11 hardened stress tests: proptest FrameCodec fuzzing (4), randomized
>     DeclareInterest/RevokeInterest race, UnixStream transport tests (4), sustained load, backpressure documentation
> 12. 10050b7 — Created TODO.md for small items and flaky test tracking
> 13. bbcc837 — Backpressure fix: bounded write channel (sync_channel(128)), SyncSender throughout, try_send in
>     Drop
> 14. adcf949 — Unified ReplyPort: closure-erased backend, one type for in-process + wire. wire_reply_port
>     constructor.
> 15. 2266475 — Documented all intentional type distinctions across codebase
> 16. 9324397 — Updated PLAN.md for session 2
> 17. e6b339b — Formalized EOS workflow, captured process feedback
> 
> Design decisions to know
> 
> - RequestProtocol is a supertrait, not an associated type with default. Three rounds, unanimous after
>   evaluating A (default), A-twist (marker trait), and B (supertrait). B won because stable Rust has no associated
>   type defaults, and the supertrait IS the marker — fewer concepts.
> - ReplyPort is closure-erased. One type, not two. Backend (in-process mpsc, wire serialization) captured in
>   closure at construction. Handler never knows which backend. T: Serialize bound on wire constructor only.
> - Wire format is u16 session discriminant. Monotonic, no recycling. 65534 limit resolved by Phase 2
>   reconnect-on-exhaustion (session composition S₁;S₂).
> - Bounded write channel (128). Backpressure via SyncSender. Blocking send is legitimate within a pane —
>   per-pane threading isolates. ServiceHandle::Drop uses try_send (non-blocking best-effort).
> - Protocol tag in service payloads. XOR-fold of ServiceId UUID bytes to 1 byte. Checked by
>   make_service_receiver before deserialization. Defense-in-depth against routing bugs.
> 
> Process adjustments from this session — READ THESE
> 
> Three feedback memories were created that change how you operate:
> 
> 1. feedback_accept_as_is — When the roundtable recommends "accept as-is" for a design property, do NOT treat it
>    as resolved. Present it to Lane as a recommendation with the counter-argument. Lane's standard is
>    correct-by-construction; "the routing is correct so this is fine" is not acceptable.
> 2. feedback_stress_test_freshness — After ANY production change to wire format, codec, or dispatch, run cargo
>    test -- --ignored and verify stress test assertions match the NEW behavior. Tests written against old behavior
>    can pass with loose assertions while their comments and intent are wrong.
> 3. feedback_per_pane_threading — pane IS per-pane threading (same as Be's per-window model). Blocking within a
>    pane's looper is legitimate backpressure, not an I2 violation. I2 prevents cross-pane interference, which
>    per-pane threading already provides. Don't recommend try_send where blocking send is correct.
> 
> EOS workflow is now formalized in PLAN.md Session End Checklist (11 items in Verify/Update/Handoff/Process
> sections). Follow it at the end of your session.
> 
> What to do next — Tasks 3-6
> 
> These complete request/reply dispatch. The four-agent roundtable already designed the full architecture across
> 3 rounds. The consensus is documented in serena and in this memo. Implement one task at a time, review between.
> 
> Task 3: ServiceDispatch request closure slot
> 
> - Add RequestReceiver<H> type (closure that takes handler + messenger + payload bytes + wire reply port)
> - make_request_receiver<H, P>() factory — deserializes P::Message, constructs typed ReplyPort<P::Reply> via
>   wire_reply_port, calls HandlesRequest::receive_request
> - ServiceDispatch gains a second HashMap<u16, RequestReceiver> for request closures
> - PaneBuilder::open_service registers the request closure when H: HandlesRequest
> 
> Task 4: LooperCore Request dispatch (provider-side A1)
> 
> - dispatch_service Request branch: extract token from ServiceFrame::Request, construct wire ReplyPort via
>   wire_reply_port(write_tx.clone(), session_id, token), call request closure in ServiceDispatch
> - Wrap in catch_unwind like Notification path
> 
> Task 5: Consumer-side send_request wiring (A2 part 1)
> 
> - ServiceHandle::send_request gets token from Dispatch::insert (delete global NEXT_TOKEN)
> - Messenger carries &mut Dispatch during handler dispatch (roundtable consensus: co-locate insert + token
>   alloc, no lost-reply race)
> - send_request installs Dispatch entry BEFORE frame hits wire
> 
> Task 6: LooperCore Reply/Failed routing (A2 part 2)
> 
> - dispatch_service Reply branch: deserialize payload, call dispatch.fire_reply(connection, token, handler,
>   messenger, payload)
> - dispatch_service Failed branch: call dispatch.fire_failed(connection, token, handler, messenger)
> 
> After Tasks 3-6: formal-verifier audit, PLAN.md update, serena freshness check.
> 
> Known issues (TODO.md)
> 
> - unix_stream_rapid_connect_disconnect flaky (timing-sensitive real socket test, passed on retry)
> - Dead code warnings in pane-app (scaffolding for Tasks 3-6, will resolve)
> - Comprehensive warning audit needed
> 
> Build
>

> cargo test -- --ignored # 28 stress tests

### `2026-04-06T21:10:03.014Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a2714362847d8cd28</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01LiEvpDWEfc3ACJorTvjK3p</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a2714362847d8cd28.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: Task 3 review (Plan 9)" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I now have a thorough picture of the existing code. Let me deliver the review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Review: Task 3 — ServiceDispatch Request Closure Slot
> 
> The plan is sound. No critical issues. Three moderate points and two minor ones.
> 
> ### 1. Token correlation maps correctly to 9P tags
> 
> **Verdict: correct.**
> 
> In 9P, the tag is a client-chosen u16 in every T-message, echoed in the corresponding R-message. The kernel's `devmnt.c` dispatches replies via `mountmux()`, which walks the outstanding request list matching tags. Here, the token serves the same purpose — chosen by the sender, carried in the frame, matched on reply. The u64 width is fine; 9P's u16 tag space was a practical limitation, not a design virtue, and it forced tag recycling logic that caused real bugs in concurrent workloads.
> 
> The scope question: 9P tags were per-connection (per mount channel fd). The plan says tokens are per-Dispatch-instance, which is per-looper. Since a looper has one connection, these scopes currently coincide. If pane ever supports multiple connections per looper, the token space needs to become per-connection or include the connection discriminant. **Moderate** — not blocking for Task 3, but document the invariant now so it doesn't become a silent assumption.
> 
> ### 2. Passing write_tx into the closure is the right structure
> 
> **Verdict: correct, with a note on the 6-parameter signature.**
> 
> In Plan 9, the server's reply path was the same fd it read from — `mntio()` in `devmnt.c` writes the R-message back on the mount channel. The server doesn't get a separate "reply object"; it constructs an `Fcall` with the same tag and writes it to the fd. Here, the closure receives `write_tx` + `session_id` + `token` and internally constructs a `wire_reply_port`. This is functionally identical: the closure has the channel, the addressing info, and the correlation token.
> 
> However, the 6-parameter closure is doing two jobs: it's both the generic deserialization trampoline AND the reply-port constructor. The existing `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` has 3 params (`handler`, `messenger`, `payload`). The proposed `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` adds `write_tx`, `session_id`, `token` — and the closure itself calls `wire_reply_port()` to build the `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;`.
> 
> That's fine structurally, but consider: `wire_reply_port` already exists in `service_handle.rs`. The closure factory `make_request_receiver` will call it. The LooperCore `dispatch_service` method already has `session_id` from the frame routing. Instead of passing `write_tx`, `session_id`, and `token` into the closure, the LooperCore could construct the `ReplyPort` *before* calling the closure. This would reduce the closure to 4 params: `handler`, `messenger`, `payload`, `reply_port_factory` — or even just pass a pre-built `ReplyPort&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;&gt;` (untyped) that the closure re-wraps.
> 
> But actually, the closure needs the concrete `P::Reply` type to build a typed `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;`. LooperCore doesn't know `P`. So the closure must do the construction. The 6-param signature follows from this type erasure boundary. **This is correct.** The closure captures `P::Reply` at registration time; LooperCore supplies the runtime values (`write_tx`, `session_id`, `token`) at dispatch time. Same pattern as how Plan 9's kernel passed the mount channel to the file server but the file server chose how to format the reply.
> 
> **Minor**: clone semantics on `write_tx`. The plan says "SyncSender is cloned by caller." Currently, `LooperCore` doesn't hold `write_tx` — it comes from `PaneBuilder`. For Task 3, LooperCore needs access to `write_tx` to pass it into the request closure. That means either LooperCore stores it, or the closure captures a clone at registration time. If the closure captures a clone at registration time, you don't need `write_tx` as a parameter at all — you get a 4-param closure (`handler`, `messenger`, `payload`, `token`). Check which approach the plan intends. Capturing at registration time is simpler and is what I'd recommend.
> 
> ### 3. Notification/Request split maps to 9P's T/R pairing — but not exactly
> 
> **Verdict: partially correct, needs clarification.**
> 
> 9P doesn't have a "notification" concept. Every T-message expects an R-message. Even `Tclunk` gets `Rclunk`. There is no fire-and-forget in 9P.
> 
> The Notification/Request split is pane's design, mapping more to BeOS's distinction between `BMessenger::SendMessage` (with reply port) vs `PostMessage` (fire-and-forget). From 9P's perspective, Notification is actually the deviation — it's a message with no tag and no reply expectation, which has no 9P analogue.
> 
> This isn't a problem. Notification is legitimate and useful. But the design heritage comment in `service_frame.rs` should be precise: the Request/Reply/Failed triple maps to 9P's Tmsg/Rmsg/Rerror triple. Notification is BeOS heritage, not Plan 9. **Minor** — documentation accuracy only.
> 
> ### 4. Token lifetime and Failed semantics
> 
> **Verdict: sound.**
> 
> The `ReplyPort::Drop` sending `ServiceFrame::Failed` when the handler drops without replying — this is strictly better than 9P. In 9P, a hung server that forgot to reply was a hung client. Period. There was no timeout, no automatic failure, no cleanup. The client's process would block in `mntio()` until someone killed it. The `#[must_use]` + Drop compensation pattern in `ReplyPort` eliminates this failure mode entirely. This is one of the genuine improvements over Plan 9's design.
> 
> One thing to verify: when `dispatch_service` calls the request closure inside `catch_unwind`, and the handler panics, the `ReplyPort` that was passed into `receive_request` will be dropped by the unwind. Its `Drop` impl should fire `Failed`. Confirm that `catch_unwind` + `AssertUnwindSafe` around the request dispatch doesn't suppress the `ReplyPort` Drop. I expect it doesn't — Rust's unwind drops locals — but this is a correctness invariant worth a test. **Moderate.**
> 
> ### 5. open_request_service requiring both Handles&lt;P&gt; and HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;
> 
> The plan says `open_request_service&lt;P&gt;` requires `H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`. This means the handler must implement both notification receive and request receive for the same protocol.
> 
> In 9P, there's no such distinction — every message type either has a reply or it doesn't, and this is fixed by the protocol spec, not the handler. But pane's protocol model is different: a `RequestProtocol` can carry both notification-style messages (via `Handles&lt;P&gt;`) and request-style messages (via `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`). Whether this is desirable depends on whether the same `P::Message` type is used for both notification and request payloads. Looking at the traits, `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` takes `P::Message` and `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` also takes `P::Message` — same message type, different dispatch paths. The ServiceFrame variant (Notification vs Request) determines which handler fires.
> 
> This is coherent. A single protocol might receive both "here's an update, no reply needed" and "I need an answer to this." The wire frame determines which path. The handler implements both. **No issue.**
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> | # | Issue | Severity | Action |
> |---|-------|----------|--------|
> | 1 | Document that token scope = per-connection. If multi-connection per looper ever lands, revisit. | Moderate | Add comment on NEXT_TOKEN / token scope |
> | 2 | Consider capturing `write_tx` clone at registration time instead of passing as closure param. Reduces RequestReceiver to 4 params. | Moderate | Evaluate before implementing |
> | 3 | ReplyPort Drop under catch_unwind: add a test proving panicking handler still sends Failed. | Moderate | Add test in Task 3 |
> | 4 | Design heritage comment: Notification is BeOS, not 9P. Request/Reply/Failed maps to 9P. | Minor | Documentation |
> | 5 | Clone semantics of SyncSender need to be specified in the plan. | Minor | Clarify |
> 
> The plan is implementable as-is. Issue #2 (closure param count) is the one I'd push hardest on — if `make_request_receiver` captures a `write_tx` clone at registration time, you eliminate three closure parameters and the LooperCore doesn't need to know about `write_tx` at all. The closure becomes `Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], u64) -&gt; Flow` — handler, messenger, payload, token. The `wire_reply_port` call happens inside the closure using the captured `write_tx` and the captured `session_id` (also known at registration time). Only `token` varies per-request and must be passed at dispatch time.</result>

> ---
> 
> ## Review: Task 3 Implementation Plan
> 
> **Overall verdict: The plan is sound.** The structural decisions are right, and they match the spirit of Be's dispatch model while properly exploiting Rust's type system. Three items to address, none critical.
> 
> ### 1. Notification/Request split vs Be's dispatch model
> 
> **Verdict: Correct departure from Be. No issue.**
> 
> In BeOS, `BHandler::MessageReceived(BMessage*)` was a single dispatch surface. Whether the message was a notification or a request-expecting-reply was a runtime property of the message — the `REPLY_REQUIRED` flag in the message header, the `reply_port`/`reply_target` fields. The handler had to *know* it should call `SendReply()`. If it forgot, the sender hung. I saw this bug approximately ten thousand times.
> 
> The split into `open_service` (notification only) and `open_request_service` (notification + request) is the right translation. It makes the reply obligation visible at the registration site, not just at the handler impl. The `H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` bound on `open_request_service` means the compiler proves both dispatch paths exist before the looper starts. This is strictly better than Be's approach.
> 
> The design note about Rust's inability to conditionally register based on trait bounds within a single method is accurate. Two methods is the right answer. Be could get away with one `AddHandler()` because everything went through one `MessageReceived` — but that was the source of the bugs, not a virtue.
> 
> ### 2. RequestReceiver type — 6-parameter closure
> 
> **Severity: Moderate (ergonomics, not correctness)**
> 
> The proposed signature:
> ```rust
> Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;, u16, u64) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;
> ```
> 
> Six parameters is a lot. Compare `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` which takes 3: `(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8])`. The extra 3 (`write_tx`, `session_id`, `token`) exist because `wire_reply_port` needs them to construct the reply channel.
> 
> The concern isn't correctness — it works. The concern is that this closure is a LooperCore-internal implementation detail, and 6 positional parameters with two bare `u16` and `u64` invite transposition bugs. The types don't help you: `session_id: u16` and `token: u64` could be swapped and the compiler wouldn't care if the token happened to fit in a u16.
> 
> **Recommendation:** Consider having `make_request_receiver` take `(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], ReplyPort&lt;...&gt;)` — 4 parameters, same as Notification + 1. Move the `wire_reply_port` construction *out* of the closure and into the LooperCore dispatch site (which already has `write_tx`, `session_id`, and `token` from the `ServiceFrame::Request` destructure). The closure receives a constructed `ReplyPort`, not the raw ingredients.
> 
> This would make `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` look like:
> ```rust
> pub type RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], ReplyPort&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;&gt;) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;;
> ```
> 
> Wait — but the closure captures `P::Reply` at construction time, and `wire_reply_port&lt;T&gt;` needs the concrete `T` for serialization. If you construct a `ReplyPort&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;&gt;` (pre-serialized) at the LooperCore site, the `make_request_receiver` closure would need to wrap it in a `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;` that serializes before forwarding. That's a `ReplyPort::map` operation.
> 
> Actually, looking at `wire_reply_port&lt;T&gt;` — the `T: Serialize` bound is on the constructor, and serialization happens inside the closure that `ReplyPort::new` captures. So the concrete type knowledge must be available when constructing the ReplyPort. This means the current plan's approach of constructing `wire_reply_port::&lt;P::Reply&gt;` *inside* the type-erased closure (where `P` is captured) is sound. The 6-parameter version works because the closure is the only place where both the concrete reply type and the wire ingredients coexist.
> 
> **Revised recommendation:** The 6-parameter version is justified by the type erasure boundary. But wrap the last three parameters in a small struct:
> 
> ```rust
> pub(crate) struct RequestContext {
>     pub write_tx: SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;,
>     pub session_id: u16,
>     pub token: u64,
> }
> ```
> 
> Then `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` becomes:
> ```rust
> Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], RequestContext) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;
> ```
> 
> Four parameters, no transposition risk on the primitives. LooperCore constructs `RequestContext` from the `ServiceFrame::Request` fields plus its cloned `write_tx`. Minor change, but it's the difference between a function that invites bugs and one that doesn't.
> 
> **Severity stays moderate** because the current plan works — this is about making the wrong thing harder, which is a Be API principle worth honoring even in internal code.
> 
> ### 3. Naming
> 
> **Minor issues:**
> 
> - `open_request_service` — the verb "open" matches `open_service`, which is good. But the name suggests you're opening a *request service*, when what you're doing is opening a service *that handles both notifications and requests*. In Be terms, this is closer to "add a handler that can do both PostMessage and SendMessage-with-reply."
> 
>   The asymmetry is: `open_service` opens notification-only; `open_request_service` opens notification+request. A developer who calls `open_request_service` might think they only get request dispatch and not notification dispatch. The plan says it "registers both notification and request receivers," which is right, but the name doesn't say that.
> 
>   Consider: `open_service_with_requests` — makes clear it's `open_service` plus request handling. Or keep `open_request_service` and document clearly. The divergence tracker should note whichever you pick.
> 
>   **Severity: Minor.** The name is not wrong, just slightly misleading. Documentation fixes it.
> 
> - `register_request` / `dispatch_request` on ServiceDispatch — these are internal, parallel to `register` / `dispatch_notification`. Fine. Consistent with existing naming.
> 
> ### 4. `open_service_inner` factoring (builder step 4)
> 
> **Verdict: Good call.** The DeclareInterest/blocking loop is identical for notification-only and request+notification paths. Factoring it into `open_service_inner&lt;P&gt;` that returns `Option&lt;(u16, SyncSender)&gt;` is exactly right — one place for the handshake logic, two call sites that differ only in what they register.
> 
> **No issue here.**
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> | # | Item | Severity | Action |
> |---|------|----------|--------|
> | 1 | Notification/Request dispatch split | None | Plan is correct |
> | 2 | 6-param RequestReceiver closure | Moderate | Wrap `(write_tx, session_id, token)` in `RequestContext` struct |
> | 3 | `open_request_service` naming | Minor | Consider `open_service_with_requests`, or document that it covers both |
> | 4 | `open_service_inner` factoring | None | Good as proposed |
> 
> The plan correctly identifies that the type erasure boundary forces the wire ReplyPort construction inside the closure. The `open_service_inner` refactoring is clean. The parallel HashMap approach in ServiceDispatch is straightforward and matches the existing pattern.
> 
> One thing I want to flag that isn't in the plan: the `catch_unwind` boundary in LooperCore's Notification dispatch path (lines 257-266 of looper_core.rs) needs to be replicated for the Request dispatch path. A panicking `HandlesRequest::receive_request` should trigger `run_destruction(ExitReason::Failed)`, not propagate. The existing `dispatch_service` has the pattern — just make sure it's applied to the new Request branch too when Task 4 wires it up. Not a Task 3 concern per se, but worth noting now so Task 4 doesn't miss it.</result>

> 
> ## Review: Task 3 Implementation Plan (Request/Reply Dispatch Wiring)
> 
> ### 1. Two HashMaps vs Unified Dispatch — **Two maps is correct. Minor suggestion.**
> 
> **Severity: no issue (the plan is right)**
> 
> The two maps reflect genuinely different categorical structures, not an arbitrary split:
> 
> - `receivers: HashMap&lt;u16, ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;&gt;` — coproduct eliminator for **notification** dispatch. The closure has shape `(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8]) → Flow`. Pure labeled case elimination on session_id.
> 
> - `request_receivers: HashMap&lt;u16, RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;&gt;` — coproduct eliminator for **request** dispatch. The closure has shape `(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], SyncSender&lt;...&gt;, u16, u64) → Flow`. It carries *extra structure* — the reply channel, session_id, and token — because the closure must construct a `ReplyPort` obligation internally.
> 
> These are not two cases of the same eliminator. They are eliminator families with different arities (different exponential objects in the categorical reading). The notification receiver is `[u8] → (H × Messenger ⊸ Flow)` while the request receiver is `[u8] × WriteTx × u16 × u64 → (H × Messenger ⊸ Flow)`, where the extra parameters provide the reply capability.
> 
> Merging them into `Either&lt;ServiceReceiver, RequestReceiver&gt;` would be a *dependent* sum — the tag determines the payload shape. That's strictly less clear than two maps indexed by the same key type, and it forces a runtime match at every dispatch that already knows which variant it wants (the caller already matched `ServiceFrame::Notification` vs `ServiceFrame::Request` in `dispatch_service`).
> 
> The product alternative `(Option&lt;ServiceReceiver&gt;, Option&lt;RequestReceiver&gt;)` is theoretically defensible — it says "each session_id has an optional notification handler and an optional request handler." But it introduces partiality (two `Option`s) where the current design has totality (if the key exists, the receiver exists). Two maps with non-overlapping keys is cleaner than one map with a pair of optionals.
> 
> **One minor point:** The plan should specify the invariant that a session_id appearing in `request_receivers` must *also* appear in `receivers` (since `RequestProtocol: Protocol`, and `open_request_service` registers both). If they're ever out of sync, that's a bug. The registration method should enforce this atomically. I'd document this as a representational invariant in the `ServiceDispatch` struct comment.
> 
> ### 2. Polarity at the Boundary — **Correct. No issues.**
> 
> **Severity: no issue**
> 
> The polarity analysis in the plan is faithful to the duploid structure. Walking through it:
> 
> 1. **Wire side (positive):** `ServiceFrame::Request { token, payload }` arrives as serialized positive data. The looper deserializes the outer frame — this is positive-to-positive (Kleisli, associative).
> 
> 2. **Closure boundary (polarity crossing):** The `RequestReceiver` closure is negative (it's a handler awaiting invocation). The looper calls it with positive arguments (payload bytes, write_tx, session_id, token). This is a single cross-polarity step, serialized by the actor's single-threaded dispatch. Correct per duploid principle 4.
> 
> 3. **Inside the closure (negative):** `postcard::from_bytes(&amp;payload)` is ↓P (deserialize = force the thunked wire data). Constructing `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;` via `wire_reply_port` is ↑(continuation) — wrapping the write channel in a positive obligation handle. Calling `handler.receive_request(msg, reply_port)` is negative-to-negative (co-Kleisli, associative). All correct.
> 
> 4. **Reply path:** `reply_port.reply(value)` is the ↓ force operator on the ↑(continuation). Serializes `P::Reply` (positive), sends through `write_tx` (positive wire). Single polarity crossing at the `.reply()` call, which is fine because it's initiated from within the handler (negative context forcing a positive value onto the wire).
> 
> The key insight: the closure captures `P::Reply` type knowledge *negatively* (it knows how to serialize the reply type), while the `ReplyPort` presents the obligation *positively* (it's a move-only value the handler must consume). This is exactly `↑(continuation)` as documented in the duploid analysis. Sound.
> 
> ### 3. Separate `open_service` vs `open_request_service` — **The split is justified, but needs a constraint.**
> 
> **Severity: moderate (design, not correctness)**
> 
> The split reflects a real type-level distinction. `open_service` requires `H: Handles&lt;P&gt;` where `P: Protocol`. `open_request_service` requires `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` where `P: RequestProtocol`. These are different bounds — you can't unify them without something like `open_service_full&lt;P: RequestProtocol&gt;() where H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`, which would force every request-capable service to also implement the notification handler, and vice versa.
> 
> The split correctly models that a protocol can be consumed for:
> - Notifications only (`Handles&lt;P&gt;`)
> - Requests only (`HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`)
> - Both (`Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`)
> 
> This is a product of capabilities, and the API should reflect it as independent registrations. That's what the plan does.
> 
> **The constraint that needs enforcement:** `open_request_service` must register a notification receiver *in addition to* the request receiver, because `RequestProtocol: Protocol` — any `RequestProtocol` can also receive notifications. Looking at the current `dispatch_service`, a `ServiceFrame::Notification` on a session_id with only a request receiver would silently drop. The plan says "registers both notification and request receivers" — good, but this means `open_request_service` must require `H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`, not just `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`.
> 
> If the intent is to allow request-only services (no notification handling), then `dispatch_service` must handle the case where `ServiceFrame::Notification` arrives on a session_id that only has a request receiver. Currently notification dispatch goes through `self.service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(session_id, ...)` — if no entry exists, it returns `None` and the looper treats it as unknown session_id. That's silent drop, which is acceptable, but should be documented as intentional rather than a gap.
> 
> **Recommendation:** Make the bound `H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` on `open_request_service`. This is the natural thing — if you're providing a request-capable service, you should handle both notifications and requests for that protocol. A protocol that truly has no notification messages will have a trivial `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impl (the macro can generate it). This avoids the silent-drop ambiguity entirely and is categorically cleaner: the protocol is a single entity with both dispatch surfaces, not two independent capabilities that happen to share a session_id.
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> The plan is sound. The two-HashMap structure, the polarity crossing, and the `open_service` / `open_request_service` split are all well-motivated. Two items for the implementation:
> 
> 1. **(Minor)** Document the invariant that `request_receivers` keys are a subset of `receivers` keys, or that they're independent namespaces — whichever is intended. Enforce at registration time.
> 
> 2. **(Moderate)** Pin down the trait bound on `open_request_service`: `H: Handles&lt;P&gt; + HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` (recommended) or `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` alone (requires documenting the silent-drop behavior for notifications on request-only sessions).</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>55994</total_tokens><tool_uses>21</tool_uses><duration_ms>92683</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-06T21:10:35.457Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a2f060cc3851dec22</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0135Q8bKi5x2X8hvYTGnALXv</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a2f060cc3851dec22.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: Task 3 review (session types)" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. Now I have the full picture. Here is my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Conditionally sound.** Two issues require changes before implementation. Three are clean.
> 
> ## 1. Obligation safety — SOUND
> 
> The proposed closure constructs `wire_reply_port::&lt;P::Reply&gt;(write_tx, session_id, token)` inside the type-erased `RequestReceiver` and passes it to `receive_request`. Three paths:
> 
> **Normal path.** Handler calls `reply.reply(value)`, consuming the port. The closure inside `wire_reply_port` serializes the value and sends `ServiceFrame::Reply { token, payload }` on the write channel. Port is consumed, obligation discharged.
> 
> **Handler panics.** `catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(|| ...))` in `dispatch_service` catches the panic. During stack unwinding, the `ReplyPort` is dropped. `Drop::drop` calls the closure with `Err(ReplyFailed)`, which sends `ServiceFrame::Failed { token }` on the write channel via `try_send`. Obligation discharged. This is tested in `obligation.rs` line 345-358 (`reply_port_drop_fires_during_panic_unwind`).
> 
> **Handler forgets to reply.** The `ReplyPort` is dropped at end of `receive_request` scope. Same Drop path. The `#[must_use]` annotation produces a compiler warning, but does not prevent it. This is the affine gap: Rust permits drop without use. The compensation (Drop sends `ServiceFrame::Failed`) converts a liveness failure (requester hangs forever) into a safety-preserving failure signal. This matches the design principle in the linearity gap analysis (path (a): accept affinity + runtime recovery).
> 
> The key property: the `ReplyPort` is constructed *inside* the `catch_unwind` boundary. If it were constructed *outside* and the closure panicked before entering `receive_request`, the port would be dropped in the catch_unwind cleanup, which is correct. But in the proposed design it's constructed *inside* the closure that `catch_unwind` wraps, so the unwinding path catches it correctly either way. No leak path exists.
> 
> **Severity: None.** Sound as proposed.
> 
> ## 2. Tag mismatch path — MODERATE, requires change
> 
> If `payload[0] != expected_tag`, the closure returns `Flow::Continue` without constructing a `ReplyPort`. No reply is sent. No `ServiceFrame::Failed` is sent. The requester's `Dispatch` entry remains forever pending.
> 
> This is a violation of the reply obligation at the protocol level. The token was allocated by the requester and a `Dispatch` entry was installed. Without a `Failed` frame, that entry is never consumed. It will only be cleaned up by `fail_connection` (on disconnect) or `fail_session` (on service teardown). Until then, it is a resource leak and, more importantly, the requester's `on_failed` callback never fires — the application-level failure signal is lost.
> 
> For notifications this is fine (no obligation). For requests it is not.
> 
> **Concrete scenario:** A server bug or S8 tag collision (truncated hash) causes a tag mismatch on a valid request. The consumer called `send_request`, installed a `Dispatch` entry, and is waiting for a reply or failure. Neither arrives. The consumer's application logic hangs at the conversation level (not the thread level — the looper continues, but the specific request is orphaned).
> 
> **Fix:** On tag mismatch, construct a `wire_reply_port` and immediately drop it (which sends `ServiceFrame::Failed`), or send `ServiceFrame::Failed` directly on the write channel:
> 
> ```rust
> if payload.is_empty() || payload[0] != expected_tag {
>     // Tag mismatch — cannot deserialize. Send Failed so the
>     // requester's Dispatch entry is consumed.
>     let frame = pane_proto::ServiceFrame::Failed { token };
>     let bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;frame)
>         .expect("service frame serialization failed");
>     let _ = write_tx.try_send((session_id, bytes));
>     return Flow::Continue;
> }
> ```
> 
> This preserves the invariant that every `ServiceFrame::Request` with a token produces exactly one `ServiceFrame::Reply` or `ServiceFrame::Failed`. In session-type terms, this is the dual obligation: the server-side endpoint of a request session must produce exactly one response. Dropping it silently breaks the dual (Ferrite, Chen/Balzer/Toninho ECOOP 2022, section 4.2 — protocol fidelity requires that both endpoints adhere to the session specification; a missing response violates the server's obligation in the `Recv&lt;Req, Send&lt;Resp, End&gt;&gt;` session).
> 
> **Severity: Moderate.** Silent failure path that orphans consumer-side dispatch entries.
> 
> ## 3. Deserialization failure — MODERATE, requires change
> 
> Same issue as #2, different trigger. The `.expect()` on `postcard::from_bytes` panics, which is caught by `catch_unwind` in `dispatch_service`. But the `ReplyPort` was never constructed (the panic happens before `wire_reply_port` is called), so no `ServiceFrame::Failed` is sent.
> 
> The `catch_unwind` path in `dispatch_service` currently runs `self.run_destruction(ExitReason::Failed)` — this is the full looper destruction sequence. That seems disproportionate for a single malformed message on one service. A deserialization failure is not a handler panic — it is a framing error on one request.
> 
> **Two sub-issues:**
> 
> **(a)** The requester's token is orphaned, same as #2. The requester never gets a `Failed` frame.
> 
> **(b)** The entire pane dies because `catch_unwind` treats the `.expect()` panic identically to a handler panic. For notifications, this is already the behavior (line 263-266 in looper_core.rs). For requests, it should be reconsidered: the request arrived with a token, and the caller expects a reply. Killing the pane is one valid response (the `fail_connection` in the destruction sequence will fire `on_failed` for all pending entries), but it kills all conversations, not just the malformed one.
> 
> **Fix:** Replace `.expect()` with a match. On deserialization failure, send `ServiceFrame::Failed` on the wire and return `Flow::Continue`. Do not panic, do not kill the pane.
> 
> ```rust
> let msg: P::Message = match postcard::from_bytes(&amp;payload[1..]) {
>     Ok(m) =&gt; m,
>     Err(_) =&gt; {
>         let frame = pane_proto::ServiceFrame::Failed { token };
>         let bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;frame)
>             .expect("service frame serialization failed");
>         let _ = write_tx.try_send((session_id, bytes));
>         return Flow::Continue;
>     }
> };
> ```
> 
> This is consistent with the existing behavior for malformed `ServiceFrame` outer envelopes (line 245: `Err(_) =&gt; return DispatchOutcome::Continue`), which are silently dropped. The difference is that for requests, "silently dropped" must include sending `Failed` to honor the token obligation.
> 
> **Severity: Moderate.** Deserialization failures kill the pane unnecessarily, and orphan the requester's dispatch entry.
> 
> ## 4. Type erasure soundness — SOUND
> 
> The closure captures `P::Reply` at `make_request_receiver` construction time via the turbofish on `wire_reply_port::&lt;P::Reply&gt;`. The type parameter flows:
> 
> 1. `make_request_receiver&lt;H, P&gt;()` where `P: RequestProtocol` — `P::Reply` is known.
> 2. Inside the closure, `wire_reply_port::&lt;P::Reply&gt;(write_tx, session_id, token)` constructs a `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;`.
> 3. The handler's `receive_request` has signature `fn receive_request(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;) -&gt; Flow` — the types match.
> 
> The type erasure boundary is at the `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` type alias: `Box&lt;dyn Fn(...)&gt;`. Above this boundary (the caller in `dispatch_service`), `P::Reply` is not known — only raw bytes and token. Below the boundary (inside the closure), `P::Reply` is captured and used for both deserialization of the message and construction of the reply port.
> 
> This is the correct polarity arrangement per the service_dispatch module's own comment: "positive at serialize (sender), negative at deserialize (inside the closure)." The type information is preserved across the erasure boundary by closure capture, not by runtime type tags. The `P::Reply` is monomorphized at closure construction time and baked into the closure's code. No `Any` downcast, no type tag check — the Rust type system guarantees the reply port's type parameter matches the handler's expected reply type.
> 
> **Severity: None.** Sound by construction.
> 
> ## 5. Fn vs FnOnce — SOUND
> 
> `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` uses `Fn` (not `FnOnce`) because:
> 
> - The same session_id can receive multiple requests over its lifetime.
> - Each invocation constructs a fresh `ReplyPort` from the raw components passed in.
> - No per-invocation state is consumed — the only captured state is `expected_tag` (a `u8`, `Copy`).
> 
> The `Fn` bound is correct. `FnOnce` would mean the closure is consumed after one request, which contradicts the design (a service session handles many requests). The linearity requirement is on the `ReplyPort`, not on the receiver closure. Each `ReplyPort` is created fresh per call, used exactly once (reply or drop), and is not shared across calls. The `Fn` closure is a factory for linear obligations, not itself a linear resource.
> 
> In session-type terms, the receiver closure is the server-side session endpoint for a persistent service: `rec X. Recv&lt;Request, Send&lt;Response, X&gt;&gt;`. Each unfolding of the recursive type produces a fresh `Recv&lt;Request, Send&lt;Response, ...&gt;&gt;` — that is the `ReplyPort`. The `Fn` bound corresponds to the recursive unfolding being repeatable.
> 
> **Severity: None.** Sound as proposed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> | Item | Verdict | Severity | Action |
> |------|---------|----------|--------|
> | 1. Obligation safety | Sound | None | None |
> | 2. Tag mismatch | Unsound for requests | Moderate | Send `Failed` frame on mismatch |
> | 3. Deserialization failure | Unsound for requests | Moderate | Replace `.expect()` with match + `Failed` frame |
> | 4. Type erasure | Sound | None | None |
> | 5. Fn vs FnOnce | Sound | None | None |
> 
> **Runtime invariant for conditional soundness:** Every `ServiceFrame::Request { token, .. }` received by the provider must produce exactly one `ServiceFrame::Reply { token, .. }` or `ServiceFrame::Failed { token }` in response. The current proposal violates this invariant on two paths (tag mismatch, deserialization failure). With the fixes above, the invariant holds on all paths: normal reply, handler panic (Drop compensation), handler forgets (Drop compensation), tag mismatch (explicit Failed), deserialization failure (explicit Failed).
> 
> This invariant is the server-side dual of the consumer's dispatch entry lifecycle: every `Dispatch::insert` must be matched by exactly one `Dispatch::fire_reply`, `Dispatch::fire_failed`, `Dispatch::cancel`, or `Dispatch::fail_connection`/`Dispatch::fail_session`. The two fixes close the gap where the server silently swallows requests without producing the response frame that would trigger the consumer-side cleanup.</result>

> <result>Now I have everything I need. Here's the analysis:
> 
> ---
> 
> **1. Should LooperCore hold write_tx?**
> 
> Yes. This is sound and aligns with Be's design.
> 
> BLooper held `fMsgPort` — the port was the looper's bidirectional connection to the system. It read messages from it (task_looper/MessageFromPort) and could write to it (PostMessage writes through `_PostMessage` -&gt; `BMessenger::SendMessage`, which ultimately writes to a port). The looper always had direct access to its own communication channel. It wasn't indirected through a handler or dispatch table for system-level framing concerns.
> 
> The key distinction here: sending `ServiceFrame::Failed` for an unregistered session_id is **framework plumbing**, not application-level messaging. It's the looper taking responsibility for protocol correctness — every Request with a token must produce exactly one Reply or Failed. This is the same category as BLooper handling `_QUIT_` internally or dispatching `B_REPLY` — the looper itself needs to participate in the wire protocol, not just delegate to handlers.
> 
> **Severity: non-issue.** This is the right place for it.
> 
> **2. Is `Option` the right wrapping?**
> 
> Yes. Tests construct LooperCore without a real connection. `Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;...&gt;&gt;` makes that explicit. The alternative — a no-op channel that silently drops — would hide the distinction between "no connection" and "channel closed," which are semantically different failure modes. If you try to send Failed and `write_tx` is `None`, that's a test/headless scenario where there's nobody on the other end anyway. If it's `Some` and `try_send` fails, that's connection teardown. Both are correctly handled by best-effort semantics (which `send_failed` already uses via `try_send`).
> 
> **Severity: non-issue.** Option is correct here.
> 
> **3. Is there a better alternative to sending Failed?**
> 
> No. The three options:
> 
> - **Silent drop**: Violates the token invariant. The consumer sent a Request with token T and installed a Dispatch entry. If you drop the frame, that entry is orphaned forever — it leaks, and if there's a timeout or cleanup sweep later, the consumer gets a confusing failure mode divorced from cause. This is the wrong answer.
> 
> - **Connection error / tear down the pane**: Disproportionate. An unregistered session_id for a Request is an error (the provider doesn't handle requests on that protocol), but it's a protocol-level error, not a connection-level one. Tearing down the pane for this is like BLooper calling `Quit()` because it got an unknown `what` code.
> 
> - **Send Failed**: Correct. It honors the token invariant (every Request produces exactly one Reply or Failed), it tells the consumer something actionable, and it's the same mechanism already used for tag mismatches and deserialization failures inside `make_request_receiver`. Consistency matters.
> 
> **Severity: this is the only correct choice.**
> 
> **4. Concerns about LooperCore having write access?**
> 
> None. LooperCore already owns `exit_tx` which is write access to a different channel. The threading model guarantees LooperCore runs single-threaded — there's no contention risk. The write_tx it would hold is the same one already cloned into the RequestReceiver closures in ServiceDispatch. Adding it to LooperCore just means the looper can respond to protocol-level situations (unregistered session, malformed frame) without needing a registered handler to do it for it.
> 
> If anything, the current design where write_tx is scattered across closures but absent from the core is the less clean state. The looper should be able to participate in the wire protocol directly, same as BLooper could always PostMessage to its own port.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Summary**: Add `write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;` to LooperCore. Thread it through both constructors (`new` gets None, `with_service_dispatch` gets Some). In `dispatch_service`'s Request branch, if `dispatch_request` returns None, use it to send Failed. No design concerns.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: write_tx on LooperCore" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. Now I have the complete picture. Here's the evaluation.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Does LooperCore holding write_tx match Plan 9 server semantics?
> 
> Yes. This is structurally correct.
> 
> In Plan 9, the kernel's `devmnt` (or any 9P server like `exportfs`) reads T-messages from the mount channel fd and writes R-messages back on the same fd. The server *always* holds the write side. There's no scenario where a 9P server receives a Trequest and lacks the ability to send Rerror. The write fd is established at `mount(2)` time and belongs to the server process for the lifetime of the connection.
> 
> Your `LooperCore` is the dispatch loop — it's the server's `main()`. The fact that it currently doesn't hold `write_tx` is the anomaly, not the proposal. The request receivers already capture `write_tx` at closure construction time (line 179 of `service_dispatch.rs`), so write capability is already inside LooperCore's dispatch path — it's just threaded through closure captures rather than held structurally. Adding it as a field makes the actual authority explicit.
> 
> **Severity of not doing this: critical.** A Request arriving on a session_id with no request receiver currently hits the TODO stub at line 273 of `looper_core.rs` and returns `DispatchOutcome::Continue`. That orphans the consumer's token permanently. This is the 9P equivalent of a server reading a Trequest and then doing nothing — the client's `msgrpc()` in `devmnt.c` would block forever on `Sleep(&amp;r-&gt;r, ...)` waiting for the reply to arrive. In Plan 9 this was considered a server bug, not a protocol ambiguity. Every T-message gets exactly one R-message. No exceptions.
> 
> ## 2. Could the "no receiver" case be detected earlier?
> 
> Two options:
> 
> **A. Reject at registration time** — if a session_id is registered for notifications only (via `open_service` without `_with_requests`), the server could refuse to route Requests to that session_id entirely during the handshake (e.g., in the Hello/Welcome exchange you have planned as "Eager Hello.interests" in PLAN.md). This is like Plan 9's `open(2)` mode bits — if a file is opened `OREAD`, the server can reject `Twrite` immediately with `Rerror "permission denied"` without ever dispatching it.
> 
> This is the cleaner long-term answer. But it requires the handshake to advertise "this provider handles requests" vs "notifications only", and the *server* (ProtocolServer) to enforce it before the frame reaches the pane. You don't have that plumbing yet.
> 
> **B. Detect at dispatch time (the proposal)** — when `dispatch_request` returns `None`, LooperCore sends Failed with the token. This is the `Rerror` path — the server received a valid T-message for an operation it can't perform, so it replies with an error.
> 
> Both are valid. Plan 9 did both: `open()` mode enforcement was early rejection; `Rerror` was the runtime fallback for operations that made it past the mode check. **Do B now, plan for A in the Hello.interests work.** They're complementary, not exclusive.
> 
> ## 3. Token orphaning in 9P
> 
> In the Plan 9 kernel (`devmnt.c`), every `Trequest` generated a `Mntrpc` struct with a unique tag, and `msgrpc()` put the calling process to sleep on `r-&gt;r` until a reply arrived. If the server never responded:
> 
> - The process slept forever. There was no timeout.
> - If the connection fd was closed (e.g., server crashed), `mountmux()` would wake all sleeping processes with `mntgate(r)` and set `r-&gt;error` to `Ehungup`. That was the only cleanup path.
> - There was no per-tag timeout. This was a known limitation. A misbehaving server could hang a client process indefinitely as long as the connection stayed alive.
> 
> Plan 9 relied on the connection itself dying as the timeout mechanism. This was pragmatic for a controlled LAN environment but would be a real problem for pane over unreliable networks.
> 
> **What this means for you:** Sending Failed immediately on "no receiver" is the right move — it's strictly better than the Plan 9 approach of hoping the connection dies. For in-flight tokens where the provider *has* a request receiver but simply never calls `reply_port.reply()`, you'll eventually need a token timeout or liveness mechanism. That's a separate problem from what you're solving here, but worth noting: the ReplyPort's Drop impl sending Failed (which you already have at line 166 of `service_handle.rs`) handles the "handler panics or forgets" case. The "handler runs forever" case remains open, same as Plan 9.
> 
> ## Recommendation
> 
> Add `write_tx` to LooperCore. It's the right field in the right struct for the right reason. Concretely:
> 
> - `Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;...&gt;&gt;` is fine — it's None only if the pane has no connection, which is a valid state (local-only pane).
> - When `dispatch_request` returns `None`, send Failed with the token, return `DispatchOutcome::Continue`. Same pattern as the existing `send_failed` helper.
> - Wrap the dispatch call in `catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(..))` like you did for notifications — a panicking request handler must send Failed, not crash the loop.
> 
> Confidence: high. This is standard 9P server hygiene. The only thing I'd flag is that `write_tx` is now held in two places: LooperCore (for the "no receiver" error path) and inside each RequestReceiver closure (for normal reply routing). That's fine — it's a `SyncSender`, cloning is cheap, and the two uses are for different failure domains.</result>

> <result>Good. Now I have the full picture. Let me look at the Request arm in `dispatch_service` more carefully — it's the TODO at line 269-275.
> 
> Here's the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Is adding `write_tx` to LooperCore a category error?
> 
> **Severity: moderate (design smell, not a bug).**
> 
> No and yes. LooperCore is not "purely a consumer" — it already holds `exit_tx: mpsc::Sender&lt;ExitReason&gt;`, which is an output channel. So the claim that it's unidirectional is already false. But `exit_tx` is *lifecycle* output (a terminal signal to the server), whereas `write_tx` is *data-plane* output (service frames flowing back to the peer). These are categorically different:
> 
> - `exit_tx` is part of the destruction comonad — it fires once, at termination. It's a counit, not a data channel.
> - `write_tx` is a persistent output capability that makes LooperCore an active bidirectional participant in the service protocol.
> 
> Adding `write_tx` to LooperCore means the dispatch loop becomes a *dialog* in the duploid sense (your header comment at lines 28-39 explicitly says the active phase is a "plain (non-dialogue) duploid"). A dialog requires managing polarity crossings. The reason the current design works cleanly is that it avoids this — `make_request_receiver` closures capture `write_tx` at registration time, keeping the output capability *inside the closure*, not on the dispatch core. That's the right factoring.
> 
> Putting `write_tx` on LooperCore would be analogous to giving a labeled coproduct eliminator access to the default case's continuation — it muddies the eliminator's job. The eliminator dispatches; it doesn't produce output on the coproduct's channel.
> 
> ## 2. Default request receiver — categorically cleaner?
> 
> **Severity: this is the right move.**
> 
> Yes, and the reason is structural. Look at what already exists:
> 
> - `make_request_receiver` (line 178) captures `write_tx` and `session_id` at construction time. The `send_failed` helper (line 218) is already factored out.
> - `ServiceDispatch::dispatch_request` returns `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` — `None` means "no receiver for this session_id."
> 
> The natural completion is: register a **default request receiver** that sends `Failed` for unrecognized session_ids. This keeps `write_tx` inside the closure system where it already lives. Concretely, this could be:
> 
> - A `default_request_handler: Option&lt;Box&lt;dyn Fn(u16, u64) + Send&gt;&gt;` field on `ServiceDispatch`, set by `PaneBuilder` with a closure that captures `write_tx`.
> - `dispatch_request` falls through to this default when the session_id lookup returns `None`.
> 
> This preserves the invariant that LooperCore never touches `write_tx`. The dispatch table is the right place for this because it already owns the routing decision. The "send Failed" behavior is a *property of the dispatch table's completeness*, not of the dispatch loop.
> 
> An even simpler variant: don't add a field at all. Just have `dispatch_service` in LooperCore handle the `None` return from `dispatch_request` by calling a method on `ServiceDispatch` that sends the failure — `ServiceDispatch` already holds closures that captured `write_tx`. But this requires `ServiceDispatch` to hold a shared `write_tx` separately from the per-session closures, which is the same structural move, just located differently.
> 
> **Recommendation:** a `default_request_handler` on `ServiceDispatch`, set during `PaneBuilder` setup. It keeps write_tx out of LooperCore and makes the dispatch table total (every Request produces a response), which is the right algebraic property — you want the coproduct eliminator to be *exhaustive*.
> 
> ## 3. `Option&lt;SyncSender&gt;` partiality
> 
> **Severity: minor, but get it right now.**
> 
> You're correct that `Option` here encodes test-vs-production partiality, which is a code smell. In production, the field is always `Some`. The `Option` exists only because tests don't want to wire up a channel.
> 
> If write_tx lives on `ServiceDispatch` (per recommendation above), the problem dissolves — `ServiceDispatch::new()` has no default handler, tests that don't register one get no Failed sent (which is the current behavior: `dispatch_request` returns `None` and LooperCore drops it). Tests that care about Failed behavior create a channel and register the default handler explicitly. No `Option` needed anywhere.
> 
> If you did put it on LooperCore despite the above, then yes: tests should provide a dummy `sync_channel(1)` and drop the receiver. `Option` on an always-present production field is a lie in the type system — it forces every use site to handle a case that can't happen.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Summary:** Default request handler on `ServiceDispatch` is the clean path. It makes the dispatch table exhaustive (every Request yields exactly one response), keeps `write_tx` inside the closure system where it already lives, avoids `Option` partiality, and preserves LooperCore's non-dialogue character. The `write_tx`-on-LooperCore proposal works but breaks the factoring that's already correct.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a9bf8dd6767f003b4</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_015UJqyom3vfjZVrZBngAWCb</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a9bf8dd6767f003b4.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: write_tx on LooperCore" completed</summary>
> <result>I now have a complete picture of the code. Here's my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: The proposal is conditionally sound but suboptimal. There is a cleaner alternative. Severity: moderate (obligation leak in tests, unnecessary coupling).**
> 
> ## 1. Where should the invariant be enforced?
> 
> The invariant is: "every `ServiceFrame::Request` with a token produces exactly one Reply or Failed."
> 
> This is a **linear obligation** created at deserialization of `ServiceFrame::Request`. The obligation must be discharged in the same dispatch cycle (single-threaded actor, no deferral). The right place to enforce it is **the match arm that destructures `ServiceFrame::Request`** inside `dispatch_service` -- i.e., LooperCore. This is correct because:
> 
> - LooperCore is where `ServiceFrame` is parsed. The obligation is born here.
> - `ServiceDispatch` returns `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` -- the `None` case (no receiver registered) is visible only to the caller (LooperCore).
> - `make_request_receiver` already handles the *happy path* and *error-within-receiver* cases (tag mismatch, deser failure, handler panic via Drop). The gap is specifically the "no receiver exists" case.
> 
> So LooperCore is the correct layer. **Not** FrameCodec (too low -- it doesn't know about session registration), and **not** ProtocolServer (it routes frames by session_id without knowledge of request-vs-notification capability per session).
> 
> ## 2. The `Option&lt;SyncSender&gt;` problem
> 
> Adding `write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;` to LooperCore creates two problems:
> 
> **Problem A: Obligation leak in tests.** When `write_tx` is `None`, a `ServiceFrame::Request` arrives and LooperCore cannot send `Failed`. The token's obligation is silently leaked. This is not a safety violation in test scenarios (no consumer is waiting), but it makes the invariant **conditionally enforced** -- it holds iff `write_tx.is_some()`. Any test that exercises the Request path without providing a `write_tx` silently masks bugs in the obligation machinery.
> 
> **Severity: moderate.** The invariant is the system's primary session-type compensation for the affine gap. Making it conditional weakens the runtime compensation layer that substitutes for the linear guarantees Rust can't provide statically. In Ferrite terms (Chen/Balzer/Toninho, ECOOP 2022, Section 4.2), protocol fidelity requires that every send-receive pair is matched at runtime when the type system can't enforce it statically. An `Option`-gated enforcement path is a hole.
> 
> **Problem B: Responsibility diffusion.** `write_tx` is already captured inside each `RequestReceiver` closure (see `make_request_receiver` at line 178 of `service_dispatch.rs`). Adding a second copy to LooperCore means two components hold the write channel for different cases of the same frame type. This splits the obligation discharge logic across two files for one `ServiceFrame::Request` match arm -- error-prone during maintenance.
> 
> ## 3. Better alternative: fallback inside ServiceDispatch
> 
> The cleanest option from your list: **ServiceDispatch gets a fallback that always sends Failed.** Concretely:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn dispatch_request(
>     &amp;self,
>     session_id: u16,
>     handler: &amp;mut H,
>     messenger: &amp;Messenger,
>     payload: &amp;[u8],
>     token: u64,
>     write_tx: &amp;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;,  // passed by caller
> ) -&gt; Flow {
>     match self.request_receivers.get(&amp;session_id) {
>         Some(receiver) =&gt; receiver(handler, messenger, payload, token),
>         None =&gt; {
>             send_failed(write_tx, session_id, token);
>             Flow::Continue
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This keeps the obligation discharge colocated with the mechanism that already handles the other failure modes (tag mismatch, deser failure). The `write_tx` doesn't live in LooperCore -- it's threaded through from `PaneBuilder::run_with` as a parameter to `dispatch_service`. No `Option` wrapping. Tests that call `dispatch_service` must provide a channel -- which means the test *observes* the Failed frame, which is testable and correct.
> 
> The alternative you listed -- "ProtocolServer rejects requests to sessions that don't support them" -- is also sound but pushes the check to a layer that currently doesn't know about per-session capability (request vs notification-only). It would require the ProtocolServer to track which sessions registered request receivers, which is information that currently lives only in `ServiceDispatch`. This is a bigger change for the same guarantee.
> 
> The "protocol violation / disconnect" option is too aggressive. An unregistered session_id for requests is a framework wiring issue (PaneBuilder registered `open_service` but not `open_service_with_requests`), not a protocol violation by the peer. Disconnecting punishes the peer for the provider's incomplete setup.
> 
> ## 4. Summary
> 
> | Aspect | `write_tx: Option` on LooperCore | Fallback in `dispatch_request` |
> |--------|----------------------------------|-------------------------------|
> | Obligation complete | Conditional (None leaks) | Unconditional |
> | Test coverage | Tests can mask bugs | Tests must observe Failed |
> | Coupling | write_tx duplicated in two places | Single flow-through |
> | LooperCore growth | +1 field, +Option match | No new fields |
> 
> Recommendation: thread `write_tx` through as a parameter, handle the fallback in `ServiceDispatch::dispatch_request`, remove the return type from `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` to `Flow` (it always produces a result now). LooperCore's `dispatch_service` Request arm calls `service_dispatch.dispatch_request(session_id, handler, messenger, &amp;inner, token, &amp;write_tx)` and wraps in `catch_unwind` the same way notifications are wrapped.</result>

> </task-notification>

> 
> ---
> 
> ## Judgment
> 
> **Accept, with one clarification on Be precedent.**
> 
> Here's what the Haiku source actually shows about the split question:
> 
> ### 1. Where did BLooper handle unrecognized messages?
> 
> The answer is unambiguous: **BLooper did NOT handle the error response. The handler chain did.**
> 
> The dispatch path is:
> 
> - `BLooper::task_looper()` (Looper.cpp:1248-1253) resolves a target handler, runs it through `_TopLevelFilter`, then calls `DispatchMessage(fLastMessage, handler)`.
> - `BLooper::DispatchMessage()` (Looper.cpp:244-271) handles exactly two system messages (`_QUIT_` and `B_QUIT_REQUESTED`). Everything else falls through to `handler-&gt;MessageReceived(message)` at line 267.
> - `BHandler::MessageReceived()` (Handler.cpp:196-283) is where the terminal fallback lives. At the bottom, lines 277-281: if no handler in the chain could handle the message, and it's not already a `B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD`, BHandler itself calls `message-&gt;SendReply(B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD)`.
> 
> The critical structural point: BLooper's `DispatchMessage` does not send error replies. It delegates to the handler chain, and the **leaf of the handler chain** (BHandler base class) is where the "not understood" reply originates. The looper framework dispatches; the handler hierarchy resolves.
> 
> ### 2. What about fMsgPort?
> 
> `fMsgPort` (Looper.h:163) is a `port_id` -- always a non-Option field. BLooper creates it unconditionally in `_InitData` (Looper.cpp:1026-1028): either from a provided port or via `create_port()`. There is no scenario where a BLooper exists without a port. This maps directly to the synthesis's proposal: `write_tx` as a non-Option field on LooperCore, tests provide a dummy channel.
> 
> ### 3. Does putting fallback logic in ServiceDispatch conflict with Be?
> 
> No. It's actually the correct translation. Here's why:
> 
> In BeOS, the error response (`B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD`) was generated by the terminal handler in the dispatch chain, not by the looper framework. BLooper's role was structural: it owned the port, resolved the target handler, and called `DispatchMessage`. The handler hierarchy decided what to do with messages it didn't recognize.
> 
> The synthesis maps this correctly:
> - **LooperCore holds write_tx** = BLooper holds fMsgPort. Structural ownership. Non-optional.
> - **ServiceDispatch::dispatch_request produces the Failed response for unregistered sessions** = BHandler::MessageReceived sends B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD at the leaf. The dispatch layer handles exhaustiveness, not the framework infrastructure.
> - **LooperCore passes &amp;self.write_tx to dispatch_request** = BLooper passes the message and handler to DispatchMessage, which passes to MessageReceived. The framework provides the resources; the dispatch logic decides the outcome.
> 
> ### 4. The write_tx already lives in closures
> 
> One thing worth noting about the current code: `write_tx` is already captured inside `make_request_receiver` closures (service_dispatch.rs:179). The synthesis adds a second copy on LooperCore. That's fine -- it's the same pattern as BLooper owning fMsgPort while individual BHandlers could also hold `BMessenger` references to the same port. The structural copy on the framework object is for the fallback path (unregistered session sends Failed); the copies in closures are for the happy path (wiring ReplyPorts).
> 
> **Verdict: accept as proposed.** The synthesis correctly separates structural ownership (LooperCore) from dispatch exhaustiveness (ServiceDispatch), which matches the BLooper/BHandler separation in BeOS. The non-Option constraint on write_tx matches fMsgPort's unconditional initialization. And the fallback living in ServiceDispatch rather than LooperCore is actually more faithful to Be's design than the alternative would have been.</result>

### `2026-04-06T21:53:10.304Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8d25d882f1c39587</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01SEapwFJz75Ft5fkZesh8Wu</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a8d25d882f1c39587.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Synthesis judgment with references" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have everything I need. Let me give the verdict.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Accept, with one clarification.**
> 
> The synthesis is correct and I'm satisfied. Here's why, grounded in the source.
> 
> ## 1. Does it satisfy my original position?
> 
> Yes. My position was that write_tx is structural plumbing -- the write side of the connection is always present when the server is operating. The synthesis makes it a non-Option field on LooperCore, which matches that.
> 
> The key Plan 9 reference: in `exportfs/exportfs.c`, `netfd` (line 45: `int netfd;`) is a package-global -- not optional, not nullable. Every handler function in `exportsrv.c` calls `reply()`, which calls `write(netfd, data, n)` (exportfs.c:464). The write side is always there. The server doesn't ask "do I have a connection?" before responding. It has one or it isn't running.
> 
> In `lib9p/srv.c`, the same pattern: `respond()` (line 744) writes via `srv-&gt;outfd` (line 800: `m = write(srv-&gt;outfd, srv-&gt;wbuf, n)`). The `Srv` struct holds `outfd` as a plain `int`, not a pointer that might be nil. The main loop in `srv()` (line 686) reads until EOF, dispatches, and each handler calls `respond()` which writes the reply. No nil-check on the write channel anywhere.
> 
> So: non-Option write_tx on LooperCore is structurally correct. Tests providing a dummy sync_channel is the right cost.
> 
> ## 2. Where does Rerror construction live?
> 
> This is the critical question for the synthesis, and the Plan 9 source gives a clear answer: **the handler constructs the error, not the dispatch loop.**
> 
> In **exportfs** (`exportfs.c:387-401`), the main dispatch loop is:
> 
> ```c
> for(;;) {
>     r = getsbuf();
>     n = localread9pmsg(netfd, r-&gt;buf, messagesize, &amp;initial);
>     if(convM2S(r-&gt;buf, n, &amp;r-&gt;work) == 0)
>         fatal("convM2S format error");
>     (fcalls[r-&gt;work.type])(r);
> }
> ```
> 
> The dispatch table (`fcalls[]`, exportfs.c:21-36) maps T-message type to handler function. There is **no default case** in the loop. If `r-&gt;work.type` is an index with no entry in `fcalls[]`, you get a nil function pointer dereference -- a crash. The loop doesn't construct Rerror for unknown types. That's the handler's job (or a crash if there's no handler).
> 
> Each handler in `exportsrv.c` calls `reply(&amp;t-&gt;work, &amp;rhdr, "error string")` to construct Rerror. For example, `Xwalk()` (exportsrv.c:164): `reply(&amp;t-&gt;work, &amp;rhdr, Ebadfid)`. The handler decides the error, the handler sends it. The `reply()` function (exportfs.c:444) is just plumbing -- it sets `t-&gt;type = Rerror` if `err != nil`, serializes, writes.
> 
> In **lib9p/srv.c** (the library), the dispatch loop (line 705-727) **does** have a default:
> 
> ```c
> switch(r-&gt;ifcall.type){
> default:
>     respond(r, "unknown message");
>     break;
> case Tversion:  sversion(srv, r);  break;
> ...
> }
> ```
> 
> Here, `respond(r, "unknown message")` is called from the dispatch switch itself for unknown types. But for known types, each `sfoo()` handler calls `respond()` with the error string. The dispatch loop doesn't construct errors for known-but-failing operations.
> 
> There's also a pre-dispatch error path (srv.c:706): if `getreq()` detects a duplicate tag, it sets `r-&gt;error = Eduptag`, and the loop calls `respond(r, r-&gt;error)` before the switch. That's framing-level validation, not dispatch-level.
> 
> **The pattern:** The dispatch table/switch handles "I don't know what this message type is" (unknown message Rerror). Each handler handles "I know what you asked for but it failed" (bad fid, permission denied, etc.). The main loop never inspects the handler's result to decide whether to send Rerror -- the handler always sends its own response.
> 
> ## 3. Verdict on the synthesis
> 
> The synthesis maps correctly to both patterns:
> 
> - **LooperCore holds write_tx** (non-Option) = exportfs holds `netfd` as a plain int, lib9p holds `srv-&gt;outfd` as a plain int. Structural access, always present.
> 
> - **ServiceDispatch::dispatch_request takes &amp;SyncSender as parameter and returns Flow (not Option&lt;Flow&gt;)** = each handler in exportsrv.c receives the request, calls `reply()` with success or error, and returns. The handler is responsible for exhaustiveness. The dispatch loop's only job is routing.
> 
> - **"No receiver registered" fallback lives in dispatch_request, not LooperCore** = lib9p's `default: respond(r, "unknown message")` in the switch. The dispatch mechanism says "unknown," not the main loop logic. In the current code, `dispatch_service()` already does this at the LooperCore level for the `ServiceFrame::Request` branch (line 269-275, currently a TODO), but moving the fallback-Failed send into `dispatch_request` is cleaner -- it keeps LooperCore from knowing about ServiceFrame::Failed semantics.
> 
> One thing worth noting: in the proposed synthesis, `dispatch_request` takes `&amp;SyncSender` as a parameter rather than capturing it at closure-creation time. Currently, `make_request_receiver()` captures `write_tx` in the closure (service_dispatch.rs:179). The synthesis says LooperCore passes `&amp;self.write_tx` at call time. These are two different wiring strategies. The Plan 9 source is neutral on this -- `reply()` in exportfs writes to a global, `respond()` in lib9p writes through `r-&gt;srv-&gt;outfd`. Both work. The closure-capture approach (current) has the advantage that each request receiver is self-contained. Passing it as a parameter has the advantage that LooperCore owns the channel in one place.
> 
> If the synthesis means "LooperCore holds write_tx, passes it to dispatch_request, dispatch_request passes it to the registered closure which uses it for the fallback," then accept. If it means "remove write_tx from the closure capture in make_request_receiver and always pass it at dispatch time," that's a larger change but also fine -- either way, the write side is structurally available and every Request produces a response.
> 
> **Accept.**</result>
