> The key question is: in the synthesis, `dispatch_request` would take `&amp;SyncSender` as a parameter and return `Flow` (not `Option&lt;Flow&gt;`). Currently `dispatch_request` returns `Option&lt;Flow&gt;`. Let me verify the current call site in `dispatch_service`.
> 
> The current state is clear from the code I've read. The `dispatch_service` method's Request branch is a TODO stub (line 270-274). The proposed synthesis is about wiring Task 4 — how LooperCore will dispatch incoming Requests to the handler.
> 
> Here is my judgment.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Accept with one clarification.**
> 
> ## 1. Does the synthesis address my two concerns?
> 
> **(a) Option&lt;SyncSender&gt; as conditional obligation enforcement hole — resolved.**
> 
> The original concern was that `Option&lt;SyncSender&gt;` on LooperCore creates a state where the framework *might not have* the channel needed to send Failed responses, which breaks the session-type invariant that every Request produces exactly one Reply or Failed. Quoting the invariant comment already in your codebase (service_dispatch.rs line 177): "every Request with a token produces exactly one Reply or Failed on the wire."
> 
> Removing Option eliminates this. A non-optional `SyncSender` on LooperCore makes the channel availability unconditional. The obligation enforcement path (tag mismatch -&gt; send Failed, deser failure -&gt; send Failed, handler panic -&gt; ReplyPort Drop sends Failed, handler completes -&gt; ReplyPort::reply sends Reply) is total. No code path can reach "I need to send Failed but I don't have a channel."
> 
> This is the right move. In Ferrite's terminology (Chen/Balzer/Toninho, ECOOP 2022, Section 3.1), the channel endpoint is part of the session context Gamma, and a session step that requires sending cannot proceed if the context doesn't contain the endpoint. Making the field non-optional is the Rust typestate equivalent: the struct is unconstructable without the channel, so dispatch_service is statically guaranteed access to it.
> 
> **(b) Responsibility diffusion — resolved.**
> 
> The current `make_request_receiver` already captures `write_tx` at registration time (service_dispatch.rs line 179) and handles all three failure paths internally (tag mismatch line 194, deser failure line 201, and the ReplyPort Drop path). The synthesis formalizes what already exists: the fallback logic lives inside `dispatch_request` (and its closures), colocated with the other failure paths. LooperCore holds the channel but doesn't contain the response-obligation logic.
> 
> This matches the categorical structure I'd expect. The obligation is an existential — "there exists a path that produces a response." The existential witness (the SyncSender) is held structurally (LooperCore). The elimination (deciding which response to send) is computational (ServiceDispatch). The synthesis keeps introduction and elimination separate.
> 
> ## 2. Does dispatch_request returning Flow (not Option&lt;Flow&gt;) properly encode the invariant?
> 
> **Yes, with a critical qualification.**
> 
> Currently `dispatch_request` returns `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` where `None` means "no request receiver registered for this session_id." The synthesis proposes moving the "no receiver" fallback (send Failed) inside `dispatch_request`, making it return `Flow` unconditionally.
> 
> This is the correct encoding. The session-type invariant is: for every `ServiceFrame::Request { token, .. }` received, exactly one of `ServiceFrame::Reply { token, .. }` or `ServiceFrame::Failed { token, .. }` must be sent. This is a totality requirement on the request-handling function. Returning `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` leaks the "unhandled" case to the caller, who must then handle it — and if they forget (or handle it wrong), the invariant breaks.
> 
> By making `dispatch_request` return `Flow` unconditionally and sending Failed internally when no receiver exists, the function's type signature expresses the invariant: **this function always produces a response and always returns a dispatch outcome**. The caller (LooperCore::dispatch_service) no longer needs to match on None.
> 
> The reference here is Ferrite Section 4.2, specifically the protocol fidelity property (Theorem 4.3): a well-typed program either terminates normally or the session endpoint is properly consumed. In our encoding, "properly consumed" means either Reply or Failed was sent. Making dispatch_request total (no Option in return) is the Rust-level equivalent of Ferrite's requirement that every branch of a session continuation must be handled.
> 
> **The qualification:** dispatch_request needs to take `&amp;SyncSender` as a parameter (as proposed) rather than capturing it, because the current `dispatch_request` method on `ServiceDispatch` doesn't have access to write_tx. The `make_request_receiver` closures already capture their own clones of write_tx (for the happy path and tag/deser failure paths), but the "no receiver registered" path needs the channel too. Passing it as a parameter is clean — it's a reference, no clone, used only for the fallback.
> 
> ## 3. Testing burden
> 
> **Non-optional write_tx on LooperCore is the right move.** The testing burden is minimal and the alternative is worse.
> 
> Currently, tests construct LooperCore with `LooperCore::new()` which doesn't take write_tx at all — it doesn't exist on the struct. The synthesis adds it as a required constructor parameter. Tests that don't care about request dispatch provide `sync_channel(1).0` and ignore the receiver. This is already the pattern used in service_dispatch.rs tests (lines 443, 469, 491, 534, 613, 695).
> 
> The cost is one extra `let (write_tx, _write_rx) = sync_channel(1);` per test that constructs a LooperCore. The benefit is that tests which DO exercise request dispatch can observe Failed frames on the receiver — which is exactly what tests should do. The current tests in service_dispatch.rs already do this (e.g., `request_tag_mismatch_sends_failed` at line 442).
> 
> Making the field optional "for testing convenience" would reintroduce the obligation enforcement hole. The test setup cost of one sync_channel is negligible. This is the same design principle behind Ferrite's refusal to allow default/empty session contexts (Section 3.3) — if your test doesn't provide the channel, your test isn't testing the real system.
> 
> ## 4. Verdict
> 
> **Accept.**
> 
> The synthesis correctly resolves both concerns. It preserves the structural access pattern the Be and Plan 9 agents want (LooperCore holds the channel) while maintaining the obligation-completeness the session-type analysis requires (no Option, fallback colocated with failure paths, dispatch_request is total). The DLfActRiS single-mailbox topology (Hinrichsen/Krebbers/Birkedal, POPL 2024, Section 5.1) is preserved — write_tx is a return-path channel within the same actor's dispatch cycle, not a cross-actor dependency that could create cycles.
> 
> No modifications needed. Proceed to implementation.</result>

### `2026-04-06T21:53:37.172Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a1dfb6d2a9c21ed78</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01P2faEQLDEZVzK5yXzYJDXJ</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a1dfb6d2a9c21ed78.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Synthesis judgment with references" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have everything I need. Let me formulate my judgment.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Accept, with one clarification.**
> 
> Here's my assessment on each point.
> 
> ## 1. Does the synthesis satisfy my original concern?
> 
> Yes, substantially. My concern had two parts:
> 
> **(a) Category error — mixing input/output.** The worry was that LooperCore, as a negative structure (it *receives* and dispatches — it is the polarity crossing point), would acquire write-side responsibility (sending Failed frames). The synthesis addresses this cleanly: LooperCore holds write_tx as structural data (positive) but never invokes `send_failed` itself. The write_tx sits in LooperCore the same way `exit_tx: mpsc::Sender&lt;ExitReason&gt;` already does — it's a capability held for passing, not for exercising logic. LooperCore already holds `exit_tx` and passes it through `run_destruction`; adding `write_tx` with the same discipline is consistent.
> 
> **(b) Dispatch exhaustiveness.** My concern was that the "no receiver registered" fallback should not be ad hoc logic floating in LooperCore's match arms. The synthesis puts it inside `dispatch_request`, which now returns `Flow` instead of `Option&lt;Flow&gt;`. This is the right move. In categorical terms, `ServiceDispatch` is a labeled coproduct eliminator — it takes a coproduct (the incoming service frame, tagged by session_id) and eliminates it through the appropriate case. A coproduct eliminator must be *total*: every tag must produce a result. The current `Option&lt;Flow&gt;` signature exposes partiality to the caller, forcing LooperCore to handle the "missing case" concern that rightfully belongs to the eliminator. Making `dispatch_request` return `Flow` with an internal default (send Failed, continue) makes the eliminator total. This is correct.
> 
> ## 2. Is the factoring categorically sound?
> 
> Yes. The key insight from the duploid analysis (serena `pane/duploid_analysis`) is:
> 
> &gt; ServiceDispatch is a positive structure holding negative values (closures awaiting invocation). Type erasure at this boundary is the correct duploid move.
> 
> Under the synthesis, LooperCore's relationship to write_tx is purely structural — it holds a positive value (the channel sender) and threads it through to the negative structure (ServiceDispatch) at the polarity crossing. This matches the pattern already established by `make_request_receiver`, which *captures* `write_tx` inside the closure at registration time (see `service_dispatch.rs:178-179`). The `&amp;self.write_tx` parameter to `dispatch_request` is an alternative threading path for the fallback case — when no closure exists to have captured the channel.
> 
> The factoring preserves the single-polarity-crossing property confirmed by the formal verifier (session `2026-04-06`, polarity audit): positive frame → negative closure invocation is the one crossing. The fallback path (no closure found, send Failed) is all-positive (data → channel send), so it introduces no additional crossing.
> 
> ## 3. Is the inline fallback equivalent to my `default_request_handler` proposal?
> 
> Functionally equivalent. Both achieve the same thing: totality of the coproduct eliminator. The difference is extensional:
> 
> - **`default_request_handler` on ServiceDispatch** would be a field — a stored closure that handles the "missing case." This is the categorical completion: extending a partial function to a total one by providing a default morphism. It's more flexible (the fallback is configurable) but introduces a stored closure that's never actually needed for any case other than "send Failed."
> 
> - **Inline fallback in `dispatch_request`** bakes the default into the method body. Less flexible, but the flexibility isn't needed — the correct response to "request for an unregistered service" is *always* "send Failed." There is no scenario where you'd want a different fallback per LooperCore instance.
> 
> The inline approach is the right call here. The `default_request_handler` proposal was over-general. The invariant "every Request with a token produces exactly one Reply or Failed" (documented at `service_dispatch.rs:177`) is a protocol-level invariant, not a per-instance policy. Hardcoding it is correct.
> 
> ## 4. One clarification needed
> 
> The synthesis says `dispatch_request` takes `&amp;SyncSender` as a parameter. I want to confirm: this `&amp;SyncSender` is only used in the fallback path (no receiver registered → send Failed), right? The happy path still uses the `write_tx` captured *inside* the `RequestReceiver` closure at registration time (via `make_request_receiver`). If so, this is clean — the parameter is only for the default case, and the per-protocol closures remain self-contained.
> 
> If instead the proposal means to *also* pass `&amp;SyncSender` into the per-protocol closure at dispatch time (replacing the captured clone), that would be a different and worse design — it would mean the closure's reply routing depends on a parameter from LooperCore at every call, coupling them tightly. The current capture-at-registration pattern is better because it's the ↑P shift: the closure is a fully-resolved negative value at construction time.
> 
> Assuming the former reading: **accept.**</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>55626</total_tokens><tool_uses>24</tool_uses><duration_ms>99077</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> yes, make sure to include the cited references

### `2026-04-06T22:52:22.201Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a1e8f5fb34563cdf9</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01HZkdhP2eUiFwQHWVaTCxuB</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a1e8f5fb34563cdf9.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: Task 5 Dispatch access" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. Now I have the full picture of both systems. Let me write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## How BeOS Handled This
> 
> ### 1. Did BHandler have access to dispatch machinery during MessageReceived?
> 
> **Yes, indirectly, and it didn't need to.** The critical insight is that BeOS didn't have the problem you're describing because reply tracking lived in the message itself, not in a dispatch table.
> 
> Looking at `BMessage::_SendMessage` in `src/kits/app/Message.cpp:2131-2280`, when a BMessenger sends a message with a reply target, the mechanism is:
> 
> - The `message_header` struct (defined in `MessagePrivate.h:56-82`) carries `reply_port`, `reply_target`, and `reply_team` fields directly in the message header.
> - The sender stamps the reply coordinates into the outgoing message header at send time (lines 2236-2238):
>   ```cpp
>   header-&gt;reply_team = replyToPrivate.Team();
>   header-&gt;reply_port = replyToPrivate.Port();
>   header-&gt;reply_target = replyToPrivate.Token();
>   ```
> - When the recipient calls `BMessage::SendReply` (lines 923-963), it reads those coordinates back out of the message header it received, constructs a BMessenger from them, and sends the reply directly.
> 
> For **synchronous** SendMessage (the variant that takes `BMessage* reply`), the sender creates a temporary port (`create_port(1, "tmp_reply_port")` at line 2301), stamps it as the reply address, sends the message, then blocks on `handle_reply(replyPort, ...)` at line 2377.
> 
> For **asynchronous** SendMessage (the variant that takes `BHandler* replyTo`), the sender converts the BHandler into a BMessenger (extracting its Looper's port and handler token), stamps those coordinates into the message, and returns immediately. The reply arrives as a normal message on the replyTo handler's looper port and is dispatched through the standard `task_looper` path.
> 
> **There is no dispatch table for reply tracking in the Be model.** The reply coordinates are embedded in the message itself, carried on the wire, and used by the replier. The sender doesn't register anything — it either blocks on a dedicated port (synchronous) or relies on the normal message dispatch to deliver the reply to the specified handler.
> 
> ### 2. How was reply expectation registered?
> 
> It wasn't registered in any dispatch table. The two mechanisms:
> 
> - **Synchronous**: Sender creates an ephemeral kernel port, sends the message with that port as reply address, blocks reading the port. No dispatch table at all — it's inline blocking.
> - **Asynchronous**: Reply coordinates (port + handler token) are stamped into the message header. The reply arrives as a regular BMessage on the target looper's port. The recipient's `task_looper()` dispatches it through the normal `DispatchMessage -&gt; MessageReceived` path. The recipient of the reply has no special code — it's just another message arriving at a handler.
> 
> The `MESSAGE_FLAG_REPLY_REQUIRED` and `MESSAGE_FLAG_REPLY_DONE` flags (set at lines 2231-2232) are anti-double-reply guards, not dispatch table entries.
> 
> ### 3. Which option is closest?
> 
> **None of them are close to the Be model**, and that's fine — pane is solving a different problem. Here's why:
> 
> BeOS could get away with no dispatch table because:
> 1. Every looper had a kernel port. Reply addresses were kernel port IDs — globally unique, kernel-managed.
> 2. Messages were self-describing (what field + flat data), not protocol-typed. Any message could arrive at any handler.
> 3. The handler's identity *was* the dispatch key — BLooper matched the `target` token in the message header to a BHandler in its handler list (`task_looper`, lines 1226-1234).
> 
> pane has a typed protocol system with session-scoped request/reply correlation. The reply doesn't arrive as "a message for handler X" — it arrives as "ServiceFrame::Reply { token, payload } on session Y". You need a dispatch table to map (session, token) back to the right callback. That's a genuine architectural difference, not a translation gap.
> 
> ## The Design I'd Propose
> 
> Given pane's actual architecture, **Option A is the right direction but with a different shape than described.** The key constraint from the Haiku source is worth preserving: the reply tracking mechanism should not require the handler to know about it. In BeOS, the handler just called `SendMessage` on a BMessenger and the framework handled everything. The handler never touched a port or a token.
> 
> The mechanism that fits pane's ownership model:
> 
> **Messenger gains a type-parameterized dispatch accessor, but only through ServiceHandle, not directly.**
> 
> The handler already has `&amp;self.some_service` (a `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`) and receives `&amp;Messenger` (or currently, the messenger is implicit). The missing piece is: ServiceHandle::send_request needs to install a DispatchEntry, and it needs `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` to do so.
> 
> The cleanest Rust mechanism is a **dispatch cell** — a `&amp;Cell&lt;Option&lt;PendingEntry&gt;&gt;` or similar — that ServiceHandle writes to and LooperCore drains. But actually, looking at your single-threaded dispatch loop more carefully, there's a simpler answer.
> 
> ### Concrete proposal: DispatchScope
> 
> ServiceHandle doesn't need `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` — it needs a way to *submit* an entry. Since dispatch is single-threaded and ServiceHandle::send_request is only callable during handler dispatch, the handler can return pending entries as a side effect.
> 
> But that's awkward. Let me look at this differently.
> 
> The actual Rust split-borrow solution:
> 
> ```rust
> // LooperCore owns both handler and dispatch as separate fields.
> // During dispatch, split borrow gives &amp;mut handler AND &amp;mut dispatch
> // simultaneously. Thread them through a scoped context.
> 
> /// Dispatch-scoped context. Exists only during handler dispatch.
> /// Not Clone, not Send — scoped to the dispatch call.
> pub struct DispatchCtx&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     messenger: &amp;'a Messenger,
>     dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> Then `ServiceHandle::send_request` takes `&amp;DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;` instead of trying to reach `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` itself:
> 
> ```rust
> // In ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;:
> pub fn send_request&lt;H&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;'_, H&gt;,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, P::Reply) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     P: RequestProtocol,
> {
>     // Serialize and send the frame on the wire
>     let token = ctx.dispatch.insert(
>         PeerScope(self.session_id as u64),
>         DispatchEntry {
>             session_id: self.session_id,
>             on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>                 let reply: P::Reply = /* downcast from Any */;
>                 on_reply(h, m, reply)
>             }),
>             on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>         },
>     );
> 
>     // ... serialize msg, send ServiceFrame::Request { token, payload }
> 
>     CancelHandle::new(/* ... */)
> }
> ```
> 
> The handler trait signatures change to receive the context:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;'_, Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow
>     where Self: Sized;
> }
> 
> pub trait HandlesRequest&lt;P: RequestProtocol&gt; {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;'_, Self&gt;,
>     ) -&gt; Flow
>     where Self: Sized;
> }
> ```
> 
> And the dispatch site in LooperCore splits the borrow:
> 
> ```rust
> // In LooperCore::dispatch_service, Notification arm:
> let LooperCore { handler, dispatch, messenger, .. } = self;
> let mut ctx = DispatchCtx { messenger, dispatch };
> let result = catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(|| {
>     service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(session_id, handler, &amp;mut ctx, &amp;inner)
> }));
> ```
> 
> This is a clean split borrow. `handler` is `&amp;mut H`, `dispatch` is `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, they're separate fields of LooperCore, and Rust allows simultaneous mutable borrows of distinct struct fields through destructuring.
> 
> ### Why this is close to the Be spirit
> 
> The BeOS handler's `MessageReceived` had implicit access to its Looper (via `this-&gt;Looper()`) and could call `BMessenger::SendMessage` from within that callback. The handler didn't know about ports or reply tracking — it just had a BMessenger and called Send. The framework handled the plumbing.
> 
> In this proposal:
> - The handler doesn't know about `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` directly — it just passes `ctx` to `service_handle.send_request(ctx, msg, ...)`.
> - `DispatchCtx` is opaque to the handler. The handler can't call `.dispatch.insert()` directly — only `ServiceHandle::send_request` (which is `pub(crate)` for the insert) uses the dispatch access.
> - The ctx is scoped to the dispatch call — it can't escape.
> 
> The `DispatchCtx` parameter in the handler traits is the price of Rust's ownership model. In C++, BHandler just reached through `this-&gt;Looper()` — a pointer dereference to shared mutable state protected by the BLooper lock. In Rust, the borrow checker enforces what the BLooper lock enforced at runtime: you can only touch the dispatch machinery while you're inside a dispatch call, and the framework guarantees single-threaded access.
> 
> ### What this is NOT
> 
> This is not Option A as stated ("Messenger becomes generic"). `DispatchCtx` is separate from `Messenger`. Messenger stays Clone, ungeneric, and represents "who I am." DispatchCtx represents "the framework's permission to modify dispatch state during this callback" — it's a scoped capability, not an identity.
> 
> This is not Option B (pending-entry buffer). The entry is installed synchronously before the wire send, so the invariant "entry exists before reply can arrive" holds. In the single-threaded dispatch model, replies can't arrive during a handler callback anyway (they queue on the channel), so Option B would also work — but it's weaker than necessary. Install-before-send is the stronger invariant, and this design achieves it.
> 
> ### Naming
> 
> Following Be convention, `DispatchCtx` could be `DispatchScope` or simply be unnamed at the handler level — the handler receives it as a parameter it passes through. The handler doesn't call methods on it. I'd lean toward not naming it in user-facing documentation and calling the parameter `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;'_, Self&gt;` in the trait. If you want to follow the Be newsletter philosophy of making APIs that explain themselves, the name should say what it *authorizes*, not what it *contains*: maybe `Scope` (it scopes the dispatch permission).
> 
> The `R` type parameter on `send_request` should become `P::Reply` directly, since `P: RequestProtocol` already associates the reply type. The current signature with an independent `R` is over-general — it lets you specify a reply type that doesn't match the protocol. That's a bug waiting to happen.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>67391</total_tokens><tool_uses>37</tool_uses><duration_ms>169852</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-06T22:53:14.374Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ac4d2b5b864d3c740</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01U1NjGgfxJArEUpHo1y6LWt</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/ac4d2b5b864d3c740.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: Task 5 Dispatch access" completed</summary>
> <result>I now have a thorough picture of the entire dispatch topology. Let me analyze.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Option A (context parameter) is sound. Option B (pending-entry buffer) is conditionally sound but creates an unnecessary gap. "Install before next recv" is sufficient for safety; "install before wire" is achievable with Option A and worth preserving.**
> 
> Here is the full analysis.
> 
> ## 1. Is "install before wire" necessary, or is "install before next recv" sufficient?
> 
> **"Install before next recv" is sufficient for safety. "Install before wire" is not necessary but is a strictly stronger invariant that costs nothing extra under Option A.**
> 
> The argument for sufficiency rests on the single-threaded actor model. Consider the race:
> 
> ```
> Time    Looper thread              Reader thread
> ────    ─────────────              ─────────────
> t1      handler calls send_request
> t2        → wire: Request{token=7}  
> t3        (entry NOT yet in Dispatch)
> t4      handler returns Flow::Continue
> t5      looper drains pending → installs entry  ← Option B inserts here
> t6                                 reads Reply{token=7} from socket
> t7                                 enqueues on mpsc channel
> t8      rx.recv() → Reply{token=7}
> t9      dispatch.fire_reply(token=7) → found
> ```
> 
> The reply can only arrive at the looper after `rx.recv()` returns it. The reader thread enqueues onto an mpsc channel; the looper thread is the sole consumer. Even if the remote peer responds instantly (t6 happens before t5), the reply sits in the channel until the looper calls `rx.recv()`. As long as the entry is installed before the looper reads from the channel, the token lookup succeeds.
> 
> This is the DLfActRiS single-mailbox property: with one mailbox per actor and sequential processing, message arrival and processing are totally ordered on the consumer side (Jacobs/Hinrichsen/Krebbers, POPL 2024, Definition 4.1 — the actor-local protocol interpretation ensures that the actor processes messages sequentially from its single mailbox). The ordering guarantee comes from the mpsc channel, not from the install timing relative to the wire send.
> 
> **However**, "install before wire" is strictly stronger and prevents a class of bugs that "install before next recv" does not:
> 
> - If someone later adds a second recv site (e.g., a synchronous subrequest that reads from the channel mid-handler), the weaker invariant breaks silently. "Install before wire" is robust against future architectural changes.
> - Under panic, "install before wire" means the entry exists in Dispatch at the point of unwind, so `fail_connection` or `fail_session` can clean it up. Under "install before next recv" with a buffer, the pending entries are stranded in the buffer — the buffer must be drained or cleaned up as a separate concern.
> 
> This is the key differentiation. Let me formalize the panic scenario for Option B below.
> 
> ## 2. Option-by-option analysis
> 
> ### Option A: Context parameter on `receive`
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> 
> /// Scoped dispatch context available during handler dispatch.
> /// Provides access to Dispatch for installing request entries.
> pub struct DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     pub(crate) dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     pub(crate) connection: PeerScope,
> }
> ```
> 
> Then `send_request` becomes:
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;P: Protocol&gt; ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; {
>     pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;H&gt;,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>         R: pane_proto::Message,
>     {
>         // 1. Allocate token from Dispatch (monotonic)
>         let token = ctx.dispatch.insert(ctx.connection, DispatchEntry {
>             session_id: self.session_id,
>             on_reply: /* type-erase R */,
>             on_failed: /* type-erase */,
>         });
>         
>         // 2. Send on wire AFTER entry is installed
>         let frame = ServiceFrame::Request { token: token.0, payload: /* ... */ };
>         if let Some(ref tx) = self.write_tx {
>             let _ = tx.send((self.session_id, /* serialize frame */));
>         }
>         
>         // 3. CancelHandle removes the entry
>         CancelHandle::new(/* cancel closure capturing token */)
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Static guarantees:**
> - Token allocation is from `Dispatch::insert`, which uses the monotonic `next_token` counter. S1 (token uniqueness) is preserved by construction.
> - Entry is installed before the wire send. The invariant is enforced by sequencing within the function body.
> - The `DispatchContext` lifetime is tied to the `dispatch()` call in LooperCore. The handler cannot stash it (it borrows `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`).
> 
> **Affine gap analysis:**
> - CancelHandle is `#[must_use]` but droppable. Drop is a no-op (the cancel action is optional, per the existing design). This is correct — cancellation is an option, not an obligation.
> - If the handler panics after `insert` but before the wire send: the entry is in Dispatch, no frame was sent, the entry will be cleaned up by `fail_connection`/`clear` in the destruction sequence. The remote peer never received the request, so no orphaned reply can arrive. Sound.
> - If the handler panics after the wire send: the entry is in Dispatch, the request is on the wire. The destruction sequence fires `on_failed` for the entry. The remote peer may eventually send a Reply, which arrives after the looper has exited. The reader thread is either dead (transport closed) or the reply is silently dropped by the closed mpsc channel. Sound.
> 
> **API cost:** Every `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` signature gains a `ctx` parameter. This is a breaking change to the trait. Every handler impl must update. The `dispatch_lifecycle` path doesn't need it (lifecycle messages don't send requests), but the trait signature is uniform.
> 
> ### Option B: Pending-entry buffer
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct ServiceHandle&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     session_id: u16,
>     write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;,
>     pending: Vec&lt;PendingEntry&gt;,  // buffered entries
>     _protocol: PhantomData&lt;P&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> After `dispatch()` returns, the looper drains `pending` into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`.
> 
> **Problem 1: Token allocation.** Where does the token come from? Two sub-options:
> 
> (B1) ServiceHandle has its own counter (local). Tokens are unique per-handle but not per-Dispatch. If two ServiceHandles for different protocols both allocate token 0, the Dispatch key `(PeerScope, Token)` collides unless PeerScope differentiates them. Currently PeerScope is per-connection, not per-service-handle. You'd need `(PeerScope, session_id, Token)` or a global counter shared across handles. The global `AtomicU64` currently in the code is this, but it defeats the purpose of moving token allocation to Dispatch.
> 
> (B2) Pre-allocate tokens from Dispatch before dispatch starts. The looper gives each handler invocation a "token budget" or the handler requests tokens up front. This is needlessly complex.
> 
> (B1) is the only viable sub-option, and it requires changing the Dispatch key to include session_id, which it already stores inside the entry but not in the key. This is technically fine but adds indirection.
> 
> **Problem 2: Panic recovery.** This is the critical gap.
> 
> ```
> Time    What happens
> ────    ────────────
> t1      looper calls handler.receive(msg)
> t2      handler calls service_handle.send_request(msg, on_reply, on_failed)
> t3        → frame sent on wire
> t4        → PendingEntry pushed to service_handle.pending
> t5      handler panics (or calls another send_request, then panics)
> t6      catch_unwind catches the panic
> t7      run_destruction fires
> t8        → dispatch.fail_connection — but the entries from t4 are NOT in dispatch
> t9        → dispatch.clear — still not there
> t10     handler dropped — ServiceHandle dropped → RevokeInterest sent
> t11     ServiceHandle.pending dropped — PendingEntry dropped silently
> ```
> 
> At t11, the pending entries are dropped. The closures `on_reply` and `on_failed` are lost. But the request was already sent on the wire at t3. If the remote peer sends a Reply, it will arrive at the looper after the looper has exited (the destruction sequence ran at t7-t9). The reader thread may or may not still be alive.
> 
> **Is this a safety violation?** The Reply arrives on the mpsc channel. If the looper has exited, the channel is closed, the Reply is dropped. If the looper hasn't exited yet (the destruction sequence is still running), the Reply sits in the channel unprocessed. Either way, the Reply is silently lost. This is *not* a safety violation — it's a liveness issue, equivalent to the existing affine gap on dropped channels.
> 
> **But the `on_failed` callback never fires.** The handler's cleanup logic for this specific request is silently skipped. Under Option A, `on_failed` fires during `fail_connection` because the entry is in Dispatch. Under Option B, it's lost. Whether this matters depends on what `on_failed` does — if it adjusts handler state that the destruction sequence depends on, skipping it could leave the handler in an inconsistent state during the remaining destruction steps.
> 
> This is the key gap. It's not a protocol-level safety violation (the wire protocol is sound — Failed/Reply arrives or the connection dies), but it's an obligation-tracking gap at the handler level.
> 
> **Problem 3: Buffer ownership.** The `pending` buffer lives on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`, which lives inside the handler struct. The looper needs to drain it after dispatch returns. But the looper doesn't have typed access to the handler's ServiceHandle fields — ServiceHandle is parameterized by P, and the looper is generic over H. You'd need some drain mechanism:
> 
> - A trait `DrainPending` that ServiceHandle implements, with an `fn drain(&amp;mut self) -&gt; Vec&lt;PendingEntry&gt;`.
> - The handler impl calls `self.clipboard_handle.drain()` in a new method.
> - The looper calls this method after dispatch.
> 
> This is a significant API surface increase for no gain over Option A.
> 
> ### Option C: Thread-local
> 
> The looper sets a thread-local `RefCell&lt;&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;&gt;` before dispatch, clears it after. `send_request` reads from the thread-local.
> 
> **Problem: type erasure.** The thread-local must be `dyn Any` because it's `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` for a specific H. Every `send_request` call does a downcast. This works but:
> 
> 1. The `H` type parameter leaks into the thread-local setup — the looper must know H at the point where it sets the TLS, which it does (LooperCore is `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`). Fine.
> 2. The downcast failure mode is a runtime panic, not a compile-time error. This is strictly weaker than Option A.
> 3. Thread-locals are global mutable state. The single-threaded actor model makes this safe in practice, but it's not encoded in the type system. You lose the "dispatch context is scoped" property.
> 
> No advantage over Option A. Strictly worse in type safety.
> 
> ### Option D: Explicit `dispatch` param on `send_request`
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     dispatch: &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     connection: PeerScope,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: ...,
>     on_failed: ...,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> ```
> 
> This is Option A minus the ergonomic wrapper. The handler needs to pass `dispatch` and `connection` explicitly. These values come from... where? The handler doesn't have access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` — it's on `LooperCore`, not passed to `receive`. So this just pushes the problem back: you still need to get `dispatch` to the handler, which means either a context parameter (becoming Option A) or a thread-local (becoming Option C).
> 
> Not a standalone option.
> 
> ## 3. Panic interaction analysis (Option A)
> 
> Under Option A, consider the full panic timeline:
> 
> ```
> t1  looper calls dispatch(|h| handler_callback(h, &amp;mut ctx))
> t2    handler calls service_handle.send_request(&amp;mut ctx, ...)
> t3      ctx.dispatch.insert(...) → Token(7) installed
> t4      wire send: Request{token=7}
> t5    handler calls another method
> t6    handler panics
> t7  catch_unwind catches panic
> t8  run_destruction:
> t9    dispatch.fail_connection → finds Token(7), fires on_failed ← correct!
> t10   dispatch.clear
> t11 handler dropped → ServiceHandle::Drop sends RevokeInterest
> ```
> 
> At t9, the entry installed at t3 is properly cleaned up by `fail_connection`. The `on_failed` callback fires, giving the handler (in whatever state it's in — the destruction sequence runs on the panicked-but-caught handler) a chance to clean up. This is the EAct E-Suspend/failure pattern operating correctly.
> 
> Compare with Option B: at t9, the entry is still in `ServiceHandle.pending`, not in Dispatch. `fail_connection` doesn't find it. At t11, `ServiceHandle::Drop` runs, but Drop doesn't fire `on_failed` — it only sends RevokeInterest. The `on_failed` closure is silently leaked.
> 
> ## 4. Recommendation
> 
> **Option A.** Concrete types:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Scoped dispatch context, available during handler dispatch.
> /// Lifetime-bound to the dispatch call — cannot be stashed.
> ///
> /// Provides the handler access to Dispatch for installing
> /// request entries. The PeerScope identifies the connection
> /// this dispatch is associated with.
> pub struct DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     connection: PeerScope,
> }
> 
> impl&lt;'a, H&gt; DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     /// Allocate a token and install a dispatch entry.
>     /// Returns the Token for use in the wire frame.
>     pub(crate) fn insert(&amp;mut self, entry: DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;) -&gt; Token {
>         self.dispatch.insert(self.connection, entry)
>     }
> 
>     /// Cancel a pending request.
>     pub(crate) fn cancel(&amp;mut self, token: Token) -&gt; bool {
>         self.dispatch.cancel(self.connection, token)
>     }
> 
>     pub fn connection(&amp;self) -&gt; PeerScope {
>         self.connection
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The `Handles&lt;P&gt;` trait change:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait — `DispatchContext&lt;Self&gt;` is a problem. `Self` in a trait is the implementing type, which is the handler H. But `DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt;` needs H to match the Dispatch&lt;H&gt;. This works: when the looper calls `handler.receive(msg, &amp;mut ctx)`, the handler is `H`, and `ctx` is `DispatchContext&lt;'_, H&gt;`. The trait bound `Handles&lt;P&gt;` on H means `Self = H`, so `DispatchContext&lt;Self&gt;` resolves to `DispatchContext&lt;H&gt;`. Correct.
> 
> But there's a subtlety: `DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt;` makes the trait generic over the handler type in a way that creates recursive type references. The handler `H` appears in `Handles&lt;P&gt;` as `Self`, and `DispatchContext&lt;Self&gt;` contains `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;Self&gt;`. Since `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` stores `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;`, which contains closures over `&amp;mut H`, this creates a self-referential type. But Rust handles this fine — `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is just a HashMap of boxed closures parameterized by H. No issue.
> 
> Actually, I want to reconsider. Making `DispatchContext&lt;Self&gt;` part of the trait signature means `Self` appears in a mutable reference in the argument. Let me check: the method signature becomes `fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow`. This is well-formed in Rust. The borrow checker sees `&amp;mut self` and `&amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, Self&gt;` as two independent borrows. They don't conflict because `DispatchContext` borrows `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which is on the LooperCore, not on the handler.
> 
> At the call site in LooperCore:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn dispatch_with_ctx(
>     &amp;mut self,
>     callback: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, H&gt;) -&gt; Flow,
> ) -&gt; DispatchOutcome {
>     let mut ctx = DispatchContext {
>         dispatch: &amp;mut self.dispatch,
>         connection: self.primary_connection,
>     };
>     let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
>     let result = catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(|| callback(handler, &amp;mut ctx)));
>     // ...
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait. `&amp;mut self.dispatch` and `&amp;mut self.handler` — this is a split borrow on `self`. Rust allows this when the borrows are disjoint fields. `self.dispatch` and `self.handler` are distinct fields of `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`, so this compiles. The key thing the borrow checker verifies is that `handler` and `ctx.dispatch` are disjoint — they are, because they're different struct fields.
> 
> But `catch_unwind` takes `FnOnce() -&gt; R` where the closure captures both `handler: &amp;mut H` and `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext`. The `AssertUnwindSafe` wrapper handles the `!UnwindSafe` issue as it already does for the handler alone.
> 
> The `ServiceHandle::send_request` signature:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, H&gt;,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     R: pane_proto::Message,
> {
>     // 1. Build and install entry
>     let token = ctx.insert(DispatchEntry {
>         session_id: self.session_id,
>         on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>             let reply: R = *payload.downcast().expect("reply type mismatch");
>             on_reply(h, m, reply)
>         }),
>         on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>     });
> 
>     // 2. Serialize and send AFTER entry is installed
>     let tag = P::service_id().tag();
>     let msg_bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg)
>         .expect("service message serialization failed");
>     let mut inner_payload = Vec::with_capacity(1 + msg_bytes.len());
>     inner_payload.push(tag);
>     inner_payload.extend_from_slice(&amp;msg_bytes);
> 
>     let frame = ServiceFrame::Request {
>         token: token.0,
>         payload: inner_payload,
>     };
>     let outer_payload = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;frame)
>         .expect("service frame serialization failed");
> 
>     if let Some(ref tx) = self.write_tx {
>         let _ = tx.send((self.session_id, outer_payload));
>     }
> 
>     // 3. CancelHandle removes the entry from Dispatch
>     // Note: ctx lifetime ends when receive() returns, but the
>     // cancel closure needs to outlive ctx. This is a problem —
>     // CancelHandle can't capture &amp;mut Dispatch because the
>     // borrow doesn't live long enough.
> }
> ```
> 
> There's the rub. `CancelHandle::new` takes a `FnOnce()` that must be `Send + 'static`. It can't capture `&amp;mut Dispatch` — the borrow is scoped to the `receive` call. The current CancelHandle is a no-op stub. For the real implementation, cancellation needs a different mechanism.
> 
> **CancelHandle solution:** CancelHandle should not directly modify Dispatch. Instead, it should signal the looper to cancel the entry. Two options:
> 
> (a) CancelHandle sends a Cancel message through the write channel (which already exists on ServiceHandle). The looper receives it and calls `dispatch.cancel(token)`. This adds a round-trip through the channel but is architecturally clean — cancellation is a message, not a shared-state mutation.
> 
> (b) CancelHandle stores a `Sender&lt;Token&gt;` to a cancel channel that the looper drains. Lighter than (a) because it doesn't go through the wire protocol.
> 
> (c) `Dispatch` uses an `Arc&lt;AtomicBool&gt;` per entry. CancelHandle flips the bool. Before firing `on_reply`, the looper checks the bool. No channel needed, but adds per-entry allocation.
> 
> Option (a) is the correct design. It matches the existing `Cancel { token }` control message listed in PLAN.md (the Tflush equivalent). CancelHandle should send `ControlMessage::Cancel { token }` through the write channel. The server forwards it to the provider, and the looper on the consumer side calls `dispatch.cancel()`. This is already in the plan — it just hasn't been implemented yet.
> 
> For now, CancelHandle can be a stub that captures nothing (as it currently is), with the real implementation deferred to when Cancel wiring lands.
> 
> **Revised send_request:**
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, H&gt;,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     R: pane_proto::Message,
> {
>     let token = ctx.insert(DispatchEntry {
>         session_id: self.session_id,
>         on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>             let reply: R = *payload.downcast().expect("reply type mismatch");
>             on_reply(h, m, reply)
>         }),
>         on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>     });
> 
>     // Serialize and send after entry is installed
>     let tag = P::service_id().tag();
>     let msg_bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg)
>         .expect("service message serialization failed");
>     let mut inner = Vec::with_capacity(1 + msg_bytes.len());
>     inner.push(tag);
>     inner.extend_from_slice(&amp;msg_bytes);
> 
>     let frame = ServiceFrame::Request {
>         token: token.0,
>         payload: inner,
>     };
>     let outer = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;frame)
>         .expect("service frame serialization failed");
> 
>     if let Some(ref tx) = self.write_tx {
>         let _ = tx.send((self.session_id, outer));
>     }
> 
>     // Stub: real cancellation sends Cancel{token} on the
>     // control channel. Deferred until Cancel wiring (PLAN.md).
>     CancelHandle::new(|| {})
> }
> ```
> 
> ### The `Handles&lt;P&gt;` trait change question
> 
> Adding `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, Self&gt;` to every `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` is the most visible API change. Every handler must accept the parameter even if it doesn't send requests. Two mitigations:
> 
> 1. **Convention:** handlers that don't send requests name it `_ctx`. This is Rust-idiomatic.
> 
> 2. **Split trait:** Instead of changing `Handles&lt;P&gt;`, add the context to the *dispatch site* only when requests are involved. The trait stays as-is; `DispatchContext` is passed alongside Messenger in the dispatch closure.
> 
> Option 2 is cleaner. The looper already passes `&amp;Messenger` as a separate argument (visible in `DispatchEntry::on_reply` and `fire_reply`). Add `DispatchContext` the same way — as a parameter to the dispatch closures and to `send_request`, but NOT to the `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` trait.
> 
> The handler accesses `DispatchContext` because the ServiceHandle's `send_request` requires it. The handler doesn't implement or call anything on `DispatchContext` directly — it just passes it through. So `DispatchContext` can be a field on Messenger, or passed alongside Messenger in a wrapper.
> 
> Actually, the cleanest approach: **Messenger carries `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` during dispatch.** This is exactly what the roundtable proposed. Messenger already exists as the framework handle passed to handlers. Today it's Clone; it would need a non-Clone scoped variant during dispatch.
> 
> **Scoped Messenger approach:**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Messenger variant with dispatch context, available during
> /// handler dispatch. Cannot be stashed (lifetime-bound).
> pub struct MessengerCtx&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     messenger: &amp;'a Messenger,
>     dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     connection: PeerScope,
> }
> ```
> 
> But this makes Messenger generic over H, which propagates through everything. That's worse.
> 
> **Final recommendation: keep it simple.** Add `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchContext&lt;'_, Self&gt;` to `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive`. The trait is internal to pane (not a public stability surface per `pane/no_stability_commitment`). Handlers that don't send requests write `_ctx`. This is the smallest change that gives us the "install before wire" invariant with full panic safety and zero runtime overhead.
> 
> The `dispatch_lifecycle` path already uses a separate method (`dispatch_lifecycle` calls `self.dispatch(|handler| handler.receive(msg))`). Lifecycle messages don't need a DispatchContext — `Handler::ready()` and `Handler::disconnected()` don't take it. Only `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` does, because only protocol handlers send requests.
> 
> ## 5. Duality verification
> 
> Not directly applicable — this is an intra-actor dispatch concern, not a session-channel duality question. The wire protocol remains symmetric: Request on the wire has a dual obligation (Reply or Failed). The dispatch entry tracking is unilateral (consumer-side bookkeeping). No HasDual inversion to verify.
> 
> ## 6. Summary table
> 
> | Property | Option A (ctx param) | Option B (buffer) | Option C (TLS) |
> |----------|---------------------|-------------------|----------------|
> | Install before wire | Yes | No | Yes |
> | S1 (token uniqueness) | Dispatch monotonic | Needs global or compound key | Dispatch monotonic |
> | Panic cleanup | `fail_connection` finds entry | Entry lost in buffer | `fail_connection` finds entry |
> | on_failed fires on panic | Yes | No | Yes |
> | Static type safety | Full (lifetime-scoped) | Full | Runtime downcast |
> | API change | `ctx` param on `Handles&lt;P&gt;` | `DrainPending` trait | None (hidden) |
> | CancelHandle works | Via Cancel message (deferred) | Same | Same |
> | Complexity | Low | Medium (buffer drain, compound keys) | Medium (TLS setup/teardown) |
> 
> Option A is the right choice. It preserves all session-type properties, handles the panic path correctly, and requires only one trait signature change that's consistent with the project's no-stability-commitment policy.</result>

> <result>Now I have the full picture. Let me write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Plan 9's dispatch model and what it says about this problem
> 
> ### 1. How devmnt.c handles nested T-messages
> 
> The caller (e.g., `mntauth`, `mntread`, `mntopen`) does **not** have direct fd access. The sequence is:
> 
> 1. **Allocate the Mntrpc** via `mntralloc(c, m-&gt;msize)` — this allocates the correlation structure (tag, buffers, rendez) from a free pool. The tag is assigned at allocation time (`new-&gt;request.tag = alloctag()` at devmnt.c:1064).
> 
> 2. **Populate the request fields** — the caller sets `r-&gt;request.type = Tauth`, `r-&gt;request.afid = c-&gt;fid`, etc.
> 
> 3. **Call `mountrpc(m, r)`** — this calls `mountio(m, r)` which does two things in strict order:
>    - **Install before write**: `r` is linked onto `m-&gt;queue` (devmnt.c:786-790) under a spinlock, **before** the write happens.
>    - **Write**: `devtab[m-&gt;c-&gt;type]-&gt;write(m-&gt;c, r-&gt;rpc, n, 0)` at line 798 — direct device write through the channel's devtab.
> 
> 4. **Gate readers**: after writing, the calling process either becomes the reader (`m-&gt;rip = up`, line 816) or sleeps on its rendez (`sleep(&amp;r-&gt;r, rpcattn, r)`, line 809) until someone else reads and dispatches the reply via `mountmux`.
> 
> The critical ordering invariant: **the Mntrpc is on the queue before the T-message hits the wire**. If the reply arrives before the entry is installed, `mountmux` would fail to match. Plan 9 gets away with this because it's the same process doing both the install and the write — there's no concurrency window between them.
> 
> ### 2. Reply correlation
> 
> Tag matching is entirely in `mountmux` (devmnt.c:934-970). The reader process walks `m-&gt;queue` looking for `q-&gt;request.tag == r-&gt;reply.tag`. When found, it unlinks the entry, copies the reply buffer into it, sets `q-&gt;done = 1`, and wakes the sleeping process.
> 
> The handler (the caller of `mountrpc`) never touches the dispatch table. It calls `mountrpc`, blocks, and wakes up with the reply already populated in `r-&gt;reply`. The dispatch layer is invisible to it.
> 
> ### 3. What this means for pane
> 
> In devmnt, the pattern is: **the correlation entry is installed by the same function that writes the wire message, atomically from the caller's perspective.** The caller (`mntauth`, `mntread`) doesn't install dispatch entries — `mountio` does both. The caller provides the Mntrpc structure (the "pending entry") and `mountio` handles the install+write sequence.
> 
> This maps directly to **Option B (pending-entry buffer)**, but with a crucial refinement that makes it closer to a hybrid.
> 
> ### The recommended design
> 
> The insight from devmnt is that the caller (`send_request`) should not need access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` at all. Instead, `send_request` produces a self-contained **pending entry** (analogous to the populated Mntrpc), and the looper's dispatch path drains these entries into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` after the handler returns.
> 
> But we need to respect the install-before-write ordering. In pane, this is actually not a problem because the reply comes back through the same `mpsc::Receiver` that the looper reads. The looper processes messages sequentially. A reply can only arrive and be dispatched *after* the current handler dispatch completes. So "install after handler returns, before next dispatch" is equivalent to "install before write" in terms of the ordering guarantee.
> 
> Here's why: in devmnt, the concern is that the reply arrives on the wire before the Mntrpc is queued, and a concurrent reader thread misses it. In pane, the looper is single-threaded. The reply frame arrives on the read channel. It cannot be processed until the current dispatch cycle completes, the pending entries are drained, and the looper calls `recv()` again. The window doesn't exist.
> 
> ### Concrete API
> 
> ServiceHandle gets a `PendingEntrySlot` — a one-shot channel for pending entries:
> 
> ```rust
> // In dispatch.rs — new type
> pub(crate) struct PendingEntry&lt;H&gt; {
>     pub peer: PeerScope,
>     pub entry: DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;,
> }
> 
> // A cloneable, type-erased submission slot.
> // ServiceHandle holds one of these. No generic H visible in ServiceHandle's type.
> // The closure captures H via the DispatchEntry closures.
> pub(crate) type PendingEntrySender&lt;H&gt; =
>     std::sync::mpsc::Sender&lt;PendingEntry&lt;H&gt;&gt;;
> ```
> 
> But wait — `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is not parameterized by `H`. The handler type is only known at the `send_request` call site. So the pending entry channel must be type-erased at the ServiceHandle level. The current `send_request` signature already takes `H` as a generic parameter, so the entry construction happens there. The question is where the channel endpoint lives.
> 
> Two options for the channel endpoint:
> 
> **Option B1: Channel on ServiceHandle.** ServiceHandle holds a `Sender&lt;Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;) + Send&gt;&gt;` — but this requires `H` in ServiceHandle's type, which it currently avoids. Rejected.
> 
> **Option B2: Channel on LooperCore, threaded via Messenger.** Messenger already gets passed to every handler method. Messenger gains a method that accepts a type-erased pending entry:
> 
> ```rust
> // messenger.rs
> impl Messenger {
>     /// Install a pending dispatch entry. Called by ServiceHandle::send_request.
>     /// The entry is buffered and drained by the looper after the handler returns.
>     ///
>     /// Type-erased: the closure captures the concrete H and reply types.
>     /// The Messenger holds the sender end of a channel that the LooperCore drains.
>     pub(crate) fn submit_pending_entry(&amp;self, entry: PendingEntryBoxed) {
>         // best-effort: if the channel is closed, the looper is shutting down
>         let _ = self.pending_tx.send(entry);
>     }
> }
> 
> /// Type-erased pending dispatch entry.
> /// The Box&lt;dyn FnOnce&gt; captures the concrete DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;.
> /// Erased at Messenger's API boundary — Messenger doesn't know H.
> pub(crate) type PendingEntryBoxed = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn std::any::Any) -&gt; Token + Send&gt;;
> ```
> 
> No — that's too ugly. The `dyn Any` downcast is a code smell. Let me think about this differently.
> 
> The real constraint: `Messenger` is not generic over `H`. `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is not generic over `H`. But `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` and `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` are. The `send_request` call site has `H` in scope.
> 
> The cleanest Plan 9-derived answer: **send_request returns the pending entry, and the caller (which has `H` in scope) installs it.**
> 
> But the caller is the handler — `handler.receive(msg)` — and the handler doesn't have `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` access. That's the original problem.
> 
> The devmnt answer is: **the handler doesn't install it. The dispatch loop does.** In devmnt, `mountio` installs the entry, not the caller of `mountrpc`. The caller of `mountrpc` is the dev operation (mntread, mntwrite) which is analogous to the handler. The dispatch infrastructure (`mountio`) handles the correlation bookkeeping.
> 
> So the right design is: `send_request` buffers the entry somewhere the looper can reach it. The looper drains the buffer. The buffer doesn't need to know `H` — it needs `H` only at drain time.
> 
> **Here's the concrete design:**
> 
> ```rust
> // In looper_core.rs — add a pending-entry buffer
> 
> /// Type-erased pending dispatch entry.
> /// The closure captures the DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt; and installs it
> /// into Dispatch&lt;H&gt; when called. Returns the assigned Token.
> type PendingInstaller&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;) -&gt; Token + Send&gt;;
> 
> // LooperCore gets a channel pair:
> pub struct LooperCore&lt;H&gt; {
>     // ... existing fields ...
>     pending_tx: mpsc::Sender&lt;PendingInstaller&lt;H&gt;&gt;,
>     pending_rx: mpsc::Receiver&lt;PendingInstaller&lt;H&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> But `pending_tx` needs to reach `send_request` without `H` leaking into `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`. The solution: **Messenger holds the sender, type-erased.**
> 
> Actually, let me reconsider. The problem with type erasure on Messenger is that Messenger is `Clone` and carries no generic parameter. The pending entry needs `H` at drain time, not at send time. So the erased sender carries a `PendingInstaller&lt;H&gt;` — but Messenger can't name `H`.
> 
> The answer is: **Messenger becomes `Messenger&lt;H&gt;` internally, or ServiceHandle gets a separate send channel.**
> 
> Looking at what already exists: `ServiceHandle` already holds `write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;`. It can hold a second sender for pending entries. But the sender type includes `H`.
> 
> Here's what I'd actually recommend, based on what devmnt teaches us about the right abstraction boundary:
> 
> ### Recommended design: Scoped dispatch context (Option A variant)
> 
> Looking again at devmnt more carefully: the callers of `mountrpc` are the dev operations themselves — `mntread`, `mntwrite`, `mntopen`. These are the Plan 9 equivalent of handler methods. They **do** get the `Mnt *m` parameter, which carries the connection state including the queue, the channel, everything. The handler has full access to the mount's dispatch infrastructure.
> 
> In other words, devmnt **is** Option A. The "handler" (dev operation) gets a context parameter (`Mnt *m`) that provides access to the dispatch infrastructure. It's just that `mountrpc(m, r)` wraps the install+write sequence so the handler doesn't manually touch the queue.
> 
> The Plan 9-correct answer for pane is:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Dispatch context available during handler dispatch.
> /// Provides request-sending capabilities without exposing
> /// Dispatch&lt;H&gt; internals. Analogous to Mnt* in devmnt —
> /// the handler gets it, but uses it through mountrpc(), not
> /// by touching the queue directly.
> pub struct DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     write_tx: &amp;'a SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;,
>     primary_connection: PeerScope,
> }
> 
> impl&lt;'a, H&gt; DispatchContext&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     /// Send a request through a service handle, installing the
>     /// reply callback atomically (install-before-write).
>     ///
>     /// Analogous to mountrpc(m, r) in devmnt.c — the caller
>     /// provides the populated request; this method handles
>     /// install + write.
>     pub fn send_request&lt;P, R&gt;(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         handle: &amp;ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         P: Protocol,
>         R: pane_proto::Message,
>         H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     {
>         // 1. Build the wire frame
>         let (session_id, outer_payload, token_value) =
>             ServiceHandle::&lt;P&gt;::build_request_frame(&amp;msg);
> 
>         // 2. Install dispatch entry BEFORE write (devmnt ordering)
>         let entry = DispatchEntry {
>             session_id,
>             on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>                 let reply = *payload.downcast::&lt;R&gt;().expect("reply type mismatch");
>                 on_reply(h, m, reply)
>             }),
>             on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>         };
>         let token = self.dispatch.insert(self.primary_connection, entry);
> 
>         // 3. Write to wire
>         let _ = self.write_tx.send((session_id, outer_payload));
> 
>         // 4. Return cancel handle
>         let dispatch_ptr = &amp;mut *self.dispatch as *mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;;
>         let peer = self.primary_connection;
>         // NOTE: cancel needs to remove from dispatch.
>         // For now, stub — the real impl needs a deferred cancel channel.
>         CancelHandle::new(move || {
>             // Can't safely access dispatch from a different thread.
>             // Cancel entries need a separate mechanism.
>         })
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> But this changes the handler trait signature. Let me assess the cost.
> 
> ### Tradeoff analysis
> 
> **What you gain from Option A (DispatchContext parameter):**
> - Install-before-write ordering, same as devmnt. No window where a reply can arrive before the entry exists.
> - No buffering, no drain step, no extra channel.
> - The handler can send requests and get back a real CancelHandle that works immediately.
> - Type-correct: `H` is in scope, no erasure needed.
> 
> **What you pay:**
> - Handler trait changes. `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` gains a context parameter. This is an API break. Every handler impl must accept the extra parameter even if it never sends requests.
> - The context carries `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which means the handler cannot hold `&amp;mut self` and `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` simultaneously through the same LooperCore — but split borrows handle this fine since `handler` and `dispatch` are separate fields.
> 
> **What Option B (pending-entry buffer) gains:**
> - Handler API stays clean — no extra parameter.
> - Simpler handler implementations for the common case (handlers that only receive, never send).
> 
> **What Option B pays:**
> - Ordering is "install after handler returns." In pane's single-threaded looper, this is safe because replies can't be processed until the next dispatch cycle. But it's a subtle invariant that must be documented and maintained. If the looper ever becomes concurrent (unlikely per the architecture), this breaks.
> - Extra channel + drain step in the dispatch loop.
> - The CancelHandle returned from send_request can't work until the entry is actually installed. A cancel during the same dispatch cycle is a no-op. This is visible to the handler.
> - Token allocation happens in send_request (before install), but the entry appears in Dispatch later. If the handler panics between send_request and return, the write_tx has the frame on the wire but no dispatch entry. The reply arrives and is silently dropped. In devmnt, if mountio fails after queuing the Mntrpc, the error path (`waserror`/`mntflushfree`) cleans it up. Option B has no cleanup path for this window.
> 
> ### My recommendation
> 
> **Option A, with the context parameter hidden behind a scoped method on Messenger rather than a new trait parameter.**
> 
> Specifically: don't change the `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` signature. Instead, make `Messenger` carry the dispatch context during handler dispatch, set by the looper before entering the handler. Messenger is already passed to handlers. The Messenger's `send_request`-adjacent method does the install+write.
> 
> This requires Messenger to become `Messenger` (non-generic, as it is now) with a type-erased interior that the looper sets up before each dispatch cycle:
> 
> ```rust
> // messenger.rs
> use std::cell::Cell;
> 
> /// Raw pointer to the dispatch infrastructure.
> /// Set by LooperCore before handler dispatch, cleared after.
> /// The looper guarantees the pointed-to data is alive for the
> /// duration of the handler call.
> ///
> /// Using Cell&lt;Option&lt;*mut ()&gt;&gt; rather than RefCell because:
> /// - Single-threaded: no concurrent access.
> /// - The pointer is only dereferenced by send_request, which
> ///   has H and Dispatch&lt;H&gt; in scope via the generic bound.
> struct DispatchSlot {
>     dispatch_ptr: Cell&lt;Option&lt;*mut ()&gt;&gt;,
>     write_tx_ptr: Cell&lt;Option&lt;*const ()&gt;&gt;,
>     peer: Cell&lt;Option&lt;PeerScope&gt;&gt;,
> }
> 
> impl Messenger {
>     /// Called by send_request on ServiceHandle.
>     /// H is provided by the call site — the handler code
>     /// that calls service_handle.send_request::&lt;H, R&gt;(...).
>     ///
>     /// Safety: the pointers in DispatchSlot are guaranteed
>     /// valid by LooperCore's scoped set/clear around dispatch.
>     pub(crate) fn install_entry&lt;H&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         entry: DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;,
>     ) -&gt; Option&lt;Token&gt; {
>         // SAFETY: LooperCore sets these before dispatch,
>         // clears after. Handler dispatch is synchronous.
>         let dispatch_ptr = self.slot.dispatch_ptr.get()?;
>         let dispatch: &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt; =
>             unsafe { &amp;mut *(dispatch_ptr as *mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;) };
>         let peer = self.slot.peer.get()?;
>         Some(dispatch.insert(peer, entry))
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> And in LooperCore, the dispatch method sets the slot:
> 
> ```rust
> // looper_core.rs
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; LooperCore&lt;H&gt; {
>     pub fn dispatch(&amp;mut self, callback: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H) -&gt; Flow) -&gt; DispatchOutcome {
>         // Set dispatch slot on messenger before handler runs
>         self.messenger.set_dispatch_slot(
>             &amp;mut self.dispatch as *mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt; as *mut (),
>             &amp;self.write_tx as *const _ as *const (),
>             self.primary_connection,
>         );
> 
>         let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
>         let result = catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(|| callback(handler)));
> 
>         // Clear dispatch slot — handler is done
>         self.messenger.clear_dispatch_slot();
> 
>         // ... rest of dispatch
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Then `ServiceHandle::send_request` becomes:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     messenger: &amp;Messenger,  // &lt;-- already available in handler context
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     R: pane_proto::Message,
> {
>     // Build wire frame
>     let tag = P::service_id().tag();
>     let msg_bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg)
>         .expect("service message serialization failed");
>     // ... frame construction as before ...
> 
>     // Install dispatch entry via messenger (devmnt ordering)
>     let entry = DispatchEntry {
>         session_id: self.session_id,
>         on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>             let reply = *payload.downcast::&lt;R&gt;()
>                 .expect("reply type mismatch");
>             on_reply(h, m, reply)
>         }),
>         on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>     };
> 
>     let token = messenger.install_entry::&lt;H&gt;(entry)
>         .expect("send_request called outside dispatch context");
> 
>     // Write to wire AFTER install
>     if let Some(ref tx) = self.write_tx {
>         let _ = tx.send((self.session_id, outer_payload));
>     }
> 
>     // CancelHandle that removes from dispatch
>     // (needs a cancel channel — separate concern)
>     CancelHandle::new(|| {})
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Why this is the right answer
> 
> 1. **Matches devmnt exactly.** The handler has access to the dispatch infrastructure through a context object (Messenger = Mnt*), calls a function that does install+write atomically (`send_request` = `mountrpc`), and never touches the dispatch table directly.
> 
> 2. **No handler trait change.** The Messenger is already passed to every place that matters. Adding `&amp;Messenger` to `send_request` is a minor signature change on `ServiceHandle`, not on the `Handles&lt;P&gt;` trait.
> 
> 3. **Install-before-write ordering.** The entry is in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` before the frame hits the wire. No ordering subtlety to document.
> 
> 4. **No extra channel or drain step.** No buffering, no "install after."
> 
> 5. **The unsafe is tightly scoped.** The pointer is valid for exactly the duration of handler dispatch, guaranteed by LooperCore's set/clear bracket. This is the same pattern as Plan 9's per-process `up` pointer — it's set by the scheduler before the process runs and is valid for the duration of the system call.
> 
> ### What I'd reject
> 
> **Option C (thread-local)** — it's the same as what I'm proposing but with TLS instead of a field on Messenger. TLS is the wrong tool because Messenger already exists as the context carrier. TLS adds a global state dependency that makes testing harder and the code less transparent. devmnt didn't use per-cpu state for this — it passed `Mnt *m` explicitly.
> 
> **Option D (leak Dispatch&lt;H&gt; to ServiceHandle)** — devmnt didn't give the handler direct access to `m-&gt;queue`. It gave it `Mnt *m` and provided `mountrpc()` as the interface. The dispatch internals were hidden. Option D is the equivalent of handing the handler `m-&gt;queue` directly.
> 
> ### Confidence
> 
> High on the design direction. The unsafe pointer pattern deserves scrutiny — if you'd prefer to avoid unsafe, Option B (pending-entry buffer with mpsc channel) is the correct fallback, with the caveat that the CancelHandle is not immediately effective and you need to document why the ordering is safe despite install-after-write. The single-threaded looper guarantee makes it safe, but it's a fragile invariant.
> 
> The one thing I haven't verified: whether `Messenger` currently gets passed through to the right places where `send_request` is called. Looking at the current `send_request` signature, it does **not** take `&amp;Messenger` — it's called as `service_handle.send_request::&lt;H, R&gt;(msg, on_reply, on_failed)`. Adding `&amp;Messenger` as a parameter is a minor API change but it's the right one — it makes the dispatch context explicit, exactly as devmnt makes `Mnt *m` explicit.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>63797</total_tokens><tool_uses>37</tool_uses><duration_ms>237231</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>
