> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a519ee888958c8bbb</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01A9zppX6oiufYZeK1LQQEdN</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a519ee888958c8bbb.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: Task 5 Dispatch access" completed</summary>
> <result>I now have a complete picture. Let me formulate the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Design Review: Dispatch Access from Handler Callbacks
> 
> ### 1. Is giving Messenger mutable/hidden state a category error?
> 
> **Yes.** The polarity classification is unambiguous here.
> 
> Messenger is currently a **positive value** — `Clone`, no side effects, `↑(negative)` in the duploid classification (matching BeOS BMessenger). The polarity table in the duploid analysis assigns it as `↑(negative)` heritage from `Be (BMessenger)`, meaning it is a *thunked positive reference* to a negative endpoint. The key property is that it is data-like: copyable, freely sharable, no mutation protocol.
> 
> `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is **negative** — it is a demand-driven callback store, a collection of pending continuations keyed by token. Mutating it is a cross-polarity operation (positive command → negative state modification).
> 
> **Option A** (Messenger gains `&amp;mut Dispatch`) would make Messenger a carrier of negative mutable state. This breaks the polarity assignment. A `Clone` type carrying `&amp;mut` anything is a contradiction in Rust terms, and the duploid reading confirms why: you'd be making a positive type non-thunkable by embedding negative mutable state in it. The BMessage polarity confusion (from the polarity classifications memory) — "compound type `positive_data × ↑(negative_continuation)` that failed to decompose" — is exactly this mistake. BeOS made it, and it caused bugs (aliased continuations, thread-unsafe reply, coupled lifetimes).
> 
> **Option C** (thread-local) hides the same pollution behind implicit state. From the categorical perspective this is worse: it makes the cross-polarity operation invisible at the type level. The `dispatch` call in `looper_core.rs` (line 144) takes `FnOnce(&amp;mut H) -&gt; Flow` — the handler sees `&amp;mut self` and nothing else. A TLS-based dispatch registration would introduce an implicit comonadic context (a hidden `!Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` available everywhere) that is not tracked in the type system. This violates both the ghost state discipline (R8: "ServiceHandle in handler not optic layer") and the duploid principle that cross-polarity composition must be serialized explicitly at actor boundaries.
> 
> **Verdict on Q1:** Both A and C are category errors. Messenger must remain a pure positive value.
> 
> ### 2. Should the coproduct eliminators be enriched with Dispatch access?
> 
> No. The ServiceReceiver/RequestReceiver closures are labeled coproduct eliminators in the code comments and architecture doc, and that classification is correct. They are the case-analysis function on the coproduct of protocol message types — given a `ServiceFrame::Notification{P₁} + ServiceFrame::Notification{P₂} + ...`, the dispatch table selects the right eliminator by session_id.
> 
> A coproduct eliminator `[f₁, f₂, ...] : A₁ + A₂ + ... → B` should depend only on the injected value, not on ambient mutable context. The current signatures confirm this:
> 
> ```rust
> ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;  = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8]) → Flow&gt;
> RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;  = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], u64) → Flow&gt;
> ```
> 
> The `&amp;mut H` is the handler state (negative, owned by the looper). The `&amp;Messenger` is the positive identity handle. The `&amp;[u8]` is the positive payload. The `u64` is the positive token. All inputs are either the negative state being transformed or positive data. No mutable framework state leaks in.
> 
> If we enriched these closures with `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, we'd be conflating two different concerns:
> - **Protocol dispatch** (coproduct elimination): routing a message to its typed handler
> - **Request registration** (E-Suspend): installing a continuation in the dynamic dispatch table
> 
> These are categorically distinct operations. Protocol dispatch is a *pure structural morphism* — it selects which handler to call. Request registration is an *effect* — it modifies the pending continuation store. Conflating them would destroy the clean factoring where `ServiceDispatch` (static, frozen) and `Dispatch` (dynamic, mutable) are separate structures.
> 
> **Verdict on Q2:** Keep dispatch access orthogonal to protocol dispatch. The coproduct eliminators should not be enriched.
> 
> ### 3. Is the temporal gap in Option B problematic?
> 
> Option B proposes: handler calls `send_request`, which buffers a `DispatchEntry` in a `Vec`. After the callback returns to `LooperCore`, the looper drains the buffer into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`.
> 
> In categorical terms, `send_request` is supposed to be the E-Suspend operation from EAct (Fowler/Hu): it atomically registers a continuation and sends the request frame. The question is whether splitting this into "prepare entry" and "install entry" breaks anything.
> 
> The key observation: **during single-threaded dispatch, no replies can arrive**. The looper is executing the handler callback. The mpsc channel from the reader thread is not being drained. So there is no race between "entry buffered but not installed" and "reply arrives for that token." The reply can only arrive after the callback returns, at which point the looper drains the buffer.
> 
> However, there is a subtlety. If the handler calls `send_request` twice in one callback, and the second call depends on the first having been installed (e.g., a cancel-and-resend pattern), the buffered entries are not yet queryable. This is a *coherence* issue: the handler's view of the dispatch state is inconsistent during the callback. But since `Dispatch` is looper-internal and handlers can't query it, this doesn't manifest as an observable problem.
> 
> The real concern is more operational: the buffer introduces a second mutable location (`Vec&lt;PendingEntry&gt;`) that must be drained reliably — including after panics. If the handler panics after buffering entries but before the looper drains them, those entries must be cleaned up. This is manageable (drain in the `catch_unwind` fallback path) but adds a cleanup obligation.
> 
> **Verdict on Q3:** The temporal gap is not categorically problematic because single-threaded dispatch guarantees no reply can arrive during the gap. It is an implementation concern (cleanup after panic), not a structural one.
> 
> ### 4. Recommendation: Option D variant — explicit param, scoped to the looper
> 
> The cleanest option preserves all existing factoring. Here is the concrete proposal.
> 
> **Core insight:** `send_request` needs two capabilities: (1) allocate a token and build a `DispatchEntry`, (2) install it in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. The handler already has capability (1) via `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`. Only capability (2) is missing, and it belongs to the looper, not the handler or Messenger.
> 
> **The type:**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Scoped, non-Clone handle to the looper's dispatch table.
> /// Passed to handler callbacks during dispatch. Not stored,
> /// not sent, not cloned — exists only for the duration of one
> /// dispatch cycle.
> ///
> /// Polarity: negative (mutable framework state, demand-driven).
> /// Not embeddable in positive types (Messenger, Message).
> pub struct DispatchAccess&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     dispatch: &amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     primary_connection: PeerScope,
> }
> 
> impl&lt;'a, H&gt; DispatchAccess&lt;'a, H&gt; {
>     /// Install a dispatch entry (E-Suspend). Returns the Token
>     /// for cancellation.
>     pub(crate) fn insert(&amp;mut self, entry: DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;) -&gt; Token {
>         self.dispatch.insert(self.primary_connection, entry)
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **The send_request change:**
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;P: Protocol&gt; ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; {
>     pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         dispatch: &amp;mut DispatchAccess&lt;'_, H&gt;,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>         R: pane_proto::Message,
>     {
>         // ... serialize msg, build frame, send on wire ...
>         
>         let token = dispatch.insert(DispatchEntry {
>             session_id: self.session_id,
>             on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>                 let reply = payload.downcast::&lt;R&gt;().expect("reply type mismatch");
>                 on_reply(h, m, *reply)
>             }),
>             on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>         });
>         
>         CancelHandle::new(move || { /* cancel via token */ })
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **How it flows through LooperCore:**
> 
> The `dispatch` method on `LooperCore` currently takes `FnOnce(&amp;mut H) -&gt; Flow`. The service dispatch closures receive `(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8])`. Neither passes `DispatchAccess`. The change is:
> 
> ```rust
> // In LooperCore, the service dispatch path:
> pane_proto::ServiceFrame::Notification { payload: inner } =&gt; {
>     let dispatch_access = DispatchAccess {
>         dispatch: &amp;mut self.dispatch,
>         primary_connection: self.primary_connection,
>     };
>     // The service receiver closure gains DispatchAccess
>     service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(
>         session_id, handler, messenger, &amp;inner, dispatch_access
>     )
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait — this conflicts with the borrow structure. `self.dispatch` and `self.handler` are both behind `&amp;mut self`. The current code already handles this by destructuring:
> 
> ```rust
> let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
> let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;
> let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;
> ```
> 
> This works because `handler`, `service_dispatch`, and `messenger` are disjoint fields. Adding `dispatch` to the set of split borrows is fine:
> 
> ```rust
> let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
> let dispatch = &amp;mut self.dispatch;
> let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;
> let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;
> let primary = self.primary_connection;
> let mut access = DispatchAccess { dispatch, primary_connection: primary };
> ```
> 
> **But now the closure signatures change.** The `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` and `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` type aliases would need to accept `DispatchAccess`. Per the answer to Q2 above, that enriches the coproduct eliminators with dispatch state. I argued against that.
> 
> **Revised approach — two levels:**
> 
> The coproduct eliminators stay pure. `DispatchAccess` is passed *alongside* them, not *through* them. The handler receives it as a separate parameter. This means the handler's `receive` method signature could change:
> 
> ```rust
> // Current
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> 
> // With dispatch access
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> 
> But this changes every handler implementation. That's Option A again.
> 
> **Better revised approach — the handler already owns ServiceHandle:**
> 
> The handler stores `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` in its own fields. `send_request` is called on `self.some_service`. The handler has `&amp;mut self`. The missing piece is `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which the handler cannot reach.
> 
> The cleanest path that doesn't change handler signatures: **make `send_request` buffer entries, and have the looper install them after the callback returns** (Option B), but with the buffering mechanism threaded through `ServiceHandle` at construction time rather than through Messenger or a new parameter.
> 
> Concretely:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Pending dispatch entries, buffered during a handler callback.
> /// Drained by the looper after the callback returns.
> /// 
> /// Interior mutability: ServiceHandle::send_request pushes entries
> /// via &amp;self (handler has &amp;mut self containing ServiceHandle).
> /// The looper drains after each dispatch cycle.
> pub(crate) struct PendingEntries&lt;H&gt; {
>     entries: RefCell&lt;Vec&lt;(PeerScope, DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;)&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait. `RefCell` is not `Send`. And `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is `Send`. Plus we need `H` to be known, but `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is not parameterized by `H`.
> 
> This is the fundamental tension. `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is protocol-scoped and handler-agnostic. `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is handler-scoped. The type parameter `H` doesn't exist on `ServiceHandle`. The current `send_request` signature introduces `H` only at the call site (`send_request&lt;H, R&gt;`). A buffer inside ServiceHandle would need to be `H`-generic, which it can't be if constructed before `H` is known.
> 
> **This is why Option D (explicit param) is the right shape, but the param should be handler-invisible — carried by ServiceHandle at construction, not passed by the handler.**
> 
> Here is the concrete resolution:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Type-erased pending entry. The closure captures the concrete
> /// DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt; construction; the looper knows H.
> type PendingInsert = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn std::any::Any) + Send&gt;;
> 
> /// Shared buffer for pending dispatch entries.
> /// ServiceHandle pushes type-erased inserters via &amp;self.
> /// LooperCore drains after each callback, downcasting to
> /// &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;.
> #[derive(Clone)]
> pub(crate) struct DispatchBuffer {
>     pending: Arc&lt;Mutex&lt;Vec&lt;PendingInsert&gt;&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> No. This is getting complicated. Let me step back and find the minimal intervention.
> 
> **The actual minimal intervention:**
> 
> The looper already has `&amp;mut self.dispatch` and `&amp;mut self.handler` as disjoint borrows. The dispatch happens in `dispatch_service`. The handler callback is called through a closure. The handler calls `self.some_service.send_request(...)`. The only thing missing is how that `send_request` call reaches `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`.
> 
> The simplest approach that doesn't change handler signatures, doesn't pollute Messenger, and doesn't use TLS:
> 
> **Give ServiceHandle a clone of a `SyncSender&lt;DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;&gt;` at construction time. The looper holds the receiver and drains it after each dispatch cycle.**
> 
> But `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` is not `Send` in general... actually it is, because `on_reply` and `on_failed` are `Box&lt;dyn FnOnce + Send&gt;`. And `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is not generic over `H` in its fields. So the channel type would need to be type-erased.
> 
> **Here is where I land:**
> 
> There are two viable options, with different tradeoffs. Let me present them cleanly.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Two Viable Options
> 
> ### Option B-refined: Pending entry buffer via channel
> 
> ServiceHandle gains a `SyncSender` for type-erased pending entries at construction time. After each dispatch cycle, the looper drains the receiver into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`.
> 
> ```rust
> /// Type-erased dispatch entry for the pending buffer.
> /// H is erased at the ServiceHandle level, recovered by the
> /// looper when it drains the buffer.
> pub(crate) type ErasedEntry = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn std::any::Any) + Send&gt;;
> 
> // In ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;:
> pub struct ServiceHandle&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     session_id: u16,
>     write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;,
>     pending_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;ErasedEntry&gt;&gt;,
>     _protocol: PhantomData&lt;P&gt;,
> }
> 
> // send_request captures H and builds the DispatchEntry inside the
> // erased closure:
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>     R: pane_proto::Message,
> {
>     // ... serialize and send frame ...
>     
>     let session_id = self.session_id;
>     let token = NEXT_TOKEN.fetch_add(1, Ordering::Relaxed);
>     // Still need a token source — keep global for now, or pass in
>     
>     if let Some(ref ptx) = self.pending_tx {
>         let entry_fn: ErasedEntry = Box::new(move |dispatch_any| {
>             let dispatch = dispatch_any
>                 .downcast_mut::&lt;Dispatch&lt;H&gt;&gt;()
>                 .expect("dispatch type mismatch");
>             dispatch.insert(
>                 PeerScope(0), // needs connection info
>                 DispatchEntry {
>                     session_id,
>                     on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, payload| {
>                         let reply = payload.downcast::&lt;R&gt;().expect("reply type");
>                         on_reply(h, m, *reply)
>                     }),
>                     on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>                 },
>             );
>         });
>         let _ = ptx.send(entry_fn);
>     }
>     
>     CancelHandle::new(|| {})
> }
> 
> // In LooperCore, after each dispatch cycle:
> fn drain_pending(&amp;mut self) {
>     while let Ok(entry_fn) = self.pending_rx.try_recv() {
>         entry_fn(&amp;mut self.dispatch as &amp;mut dyn std::any::Any);
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Pros:**
> - Handler signature unchanged
> - Messenger unchanged (stays Clone, pure)
> - ServiceReceiver/RequestReceiver unchanged
> - No TLS, no RefCell
> 
> **Cons:**
> - `downcast_mut` at runtime — not checked at compile time (though it's looper-internal, always the same `H`)
> - PeerScope and token allocation happen in the wrong place (ServiceHandle doesn't know the primary connection or have the looper's token counter)
> - Cleanup after panic: must drain the pending_rx in the destruction sequence
> - The `CancelHandle` returned by `send_request` doesn't actually have a valid token yet (the entry isn't installed until drain)
> 
> The CancelHandle problem is the real issue. The caller gets a `CancelHandle` immediately, but the entry isn't in `Dispatch` until after the callback returns. If the handler tries to cancel immediately (unlikely but possible), the cancel is a no-op because the entry doesn't exist yet.
> 
> ### Option D-refined: DispatchCtx passed to dispatch callbacks
> 
> Instead of changing the handler trait, change the looper's internal dispatch protocol. The `dispatch()` method on LooperCore changes from `FnOnce(&amp;mut H) -&gt; Flow` to `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;) -&gt; Flow`. The handler trait stays the same — the ctx is threaded by the framework, not the handler.
> 
> But the handler calls `self.service.send_request(...)` from inside `receive`, so `receive` would need access to the ctx. Unless send_request takes ctx as a parameter.
> 
> This circles back to the handler-visible API question. Let me be precise about what changes in each option.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> **Option B-refined is the closest to preserving the existing factoring**, with one amendment to fix the CancelHandle problem: `send_request` returns a `PendingCancel` instead of `CancelHandle`. The `PendingCancel` resolves to a real `CancelHandle` when the looper drains the buffer. Or simpler: the CancelHandle's cancel closure captures the token and a clone of pending_tx, and cancel sends a cancel command through the same channel.
> 
> But I want to flag that I think the cleanest long-term answer is actually **Option D with a context parameter on send_request only**, not on receive:
> 
> ```rust
> // Handler trait: UNCHANGED
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> 
> // ServiceHandle::send_request: gains &amp;DispatchAccess&lt;H&gt; param
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     ctx: &amp;mut DispatchAccess&lt;'_, H&gt;,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: ...,
>     on_failed: ...,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle;
> 
> // The handler calls it as:
> self.some_service.send_request(&amp;mut ctx, msg, on_reply, on_failed);
> ```
> 
> The question is: **how does the handler get `ctx`?** Two sub-options:
> 
> **(D1)** Change `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` to pass it:
> ```rust
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchAccess&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> Every handler impl changes. This is what the roundtable said ("Messenger carries &amp;mut Dispatch during handler dispatch") but implemented correctly — not Messenger, a separate ctx type.
> 
> **(D2)** Change `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` to pass it alongside the handler, and let the handler store it transiently:
> ```rust
> // ServiceReceiver now passes ctx:
> type ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;mut DispatchAccess&lt;'_, H&gt;, &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;;
> ```
> The coproduct eliminator gains the ctx — but it's the *framework's* ctx passed through, not state the eliminator owns. The eliminator is still structurally a case-analysis; it just has access to an ambient capability.
> 
> This is the distinction I want to draw: giving the eliminator *access* to a capability is different from making the capability *part of* the coproduct structure. A coproduct eliminator `[f₁, f₂] : A₁ + A₂ → B` in a *context* Γ is `Γ ⊢ [f₁, f₂] : A₁ + A₂ → B`. The context Γ includes `DispatchAccess`. The eliminator's structure (case-analysis by protocol) is unchanged; it just runs in a richer context.
> 
> **D2 is my recommendation.** It:
> - Keeps Messenger as a pure positive value (no category error)
> - Keeps the handler trait signature unchanged
> - Passes `DispatchAccess` through the framework's dispatch path, not the handler API
> - Makes the E-Suspend operation (`insert`) atomic — no temporal gap
> - Keeps the CancelHandle valid immediately
> - Is explicit about the cross-polarity operation (negative mutable state accessed under positive message dispatch)
> - Matches the duploid principle: cross-polarity composition is serialized at the actor boundary (LooperCore), and `DispatchAccess` is the scoped witness of that serialization
> 
> The cost is: `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` and `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;` type aliases change, all closure constructors (`make_service_receiver`, `make_request_receiver`) gain the param, and `dispatch_notification`/`dispatch_request` on `ServiceDispatch` gain it. These are all `pub(crate)` — no public API change.
> 
> **But**: `send_request` on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is public, and it would need to take `&amp;mut DispatchAccess&lt;'_, H&gt;`. The handler would need to get `ctx` from somewhere. If `receive` doesn't pass it, how does the handler have it?
> 
> One resolution: the handler stores a `Cell&lt;Option&lt;DispatchAccess&gt;&gt;` set by the framework before dispatch and cleared after. But that's runtime state management, and `DispatchAccess` has a lifetime tied to the looper's borrow — can't store it in the handler.
> 
> **This is the fundamental tension:** the handler method signature determines what the handler can access, and the handler method signature is a public trait. There is no way to give the handler `DispatchAccess` without either (a) changing the trait, or (b) using some form of hidden state (TLS, Cell, channel).
> 
> Given this, **I think D1 (changing the handler trait) is actually the right call**, with the understanding that the roundtable's recommendation was directionally correct but named the wrong carrier (Messenger vs. a dedicated context type). The change is:
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-proto:
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> 
> // DispatchCtx is a trait object or a concrete type in pane-app
> // that the handler uses to call send_request.
> ```
> 
> But `DispatchCtx` needs `H` (because `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` is `H`-generic). And `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is in `pane-proto`, which doesn't know about `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (that's in `pane-app`). So `DispatchCtx` would need to be either a trait or passed through a different mechanism.
> 
> **Let me present these as the two options for Lane to decide between, since the design decision escalation policy applies here.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary for Lane
> 
> **Q1:** Giving Messenger mutable state (A) or hidden global state (C) is a category error. Messenger is positive/Clone; Dispatch is negative/mutable. Mixing them reproduces BeOS's BMessage polarity confusion.
> 
> **Q2:** The coproduct eliminators (ServiceReceiver/RequestReceiver) should remain pure case-analysis. Dispatch access is a context (Γ), not part of the coproduct structure. Passing it *alongside* the eliminators is fine; embedding it *in* them conflates dispatch routing with effect registration.
> 
> **Q3:** The temporal gap in Option B is not structurally problematic (single-threaded dispatch guarantees no race), but it creates a CancelHandle validity problem and a panic-cleanup obligation. It's manageable, not elegant.
> 
> **Q4: Two options for your decision:**
> 
> **Option B-channel:** ServiceHandle gains a `SyncSender` for type-erased pending entries at construction. Looper drains after each callback. Handler trait unchanged. Public API unchanged. Cost: runtime downcast, deferred CancelHandle validity, panic-path drain obligation. Advantage: zero trait signature changes, zero public API changes.
> 
> **Option D1-context:** `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` gains a `&amp;mut DispatchCtx` parameter (not Messenger — a dedicated negative-polarity context). send_request takes it. Entry installation is atomic. CancelHandle is valid immediately. Cost: every Handles&lt;P&gt; impl changes (but there are few right now — this is the time to do it, per the functoriality principle). The `DispatchCtx` type needs careful design to live in pane-app (not pane-proto) while being passable through pane-proto's trait — likely requires a trait object or a type parameter on Handles.
> 
> My structural preference is **D1** because it makes the cross-polarity operation explicit in the type system, matches the duploid principle of serialized polarity crossings, and avoids runtime type erasure. The functoriality principle argues for getting the trait signature right now rather than working around it. But it has a real crate-boundary cost that needs design work.</result>

> <result>Now I have everything I need. Let me formulate the response.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Path recommendation: Path 1 (DispatchCtx on the handler trait)
> 
> The categorical argument is straightforward once you look at the profunctor structure of what dispatch is doing.
> 
> **What dispatch IS, categorically.** The handler trait `Handles&lt;P&gt;` defines a *copattern* -- it's negative, demand-driven (see polarity table: `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` is co-Kleisli). The copattern's signature `(&amp;mut self, P::Message) → Flow` determines the *observations* available on the handler: you observe it by feeding it a message and reading back a flow decision.
> 
> The profunctor encoding from the reference material (Pickering et al., and the "Don't Fear" walkthrough in `.gist/DontFearTheProfunctorOptics/ProfunctorOptics.md`) gives us the general shape:
> 
> ```
> type Optic s t a b = forall p. C p =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t
> ```
> 
> The key insight: **the constraints C determine what the optic can do**. A lens needs Cartesian (product context passes through). A prism needs Cocartesian (sum context branches). The choice of constraint is *precisely* the choice of what capabilities are available at the focus.
> 
> Right now, `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` provides `(&amp;mut self, P::Message)` -- that is, the handler is the focus of a lens where the context is the message. But dispatch also needs access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (to install reply callbacks). In profunctor terms, **adding DispatchCtx is extending the context of the lens**. Currently we have:
> 
> ```
> receive : self × Message → Flow
> ```
> 
> Path 1 makes this:
> 
> ```
> receive : self × Message × DispatchCtx → Flow
> ```
> 
> This is adding a product factor to the context -- it's the Cartesian `first` operation in the profunctor diagram. The handler (as a profunctor from messages to flows) gains additional contextual input. This is clean: it's a natural transformation that extends the copattern with more structure, and composition works because Cartesian profunctors compose.
> 
> **Path 2 buries the context in the whole.** ServiceHandle gains an `Arc&lt;AtomicPtr&lt;()&gt;&gt;` that secretly aliases `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. In profunctor terms, this is *hiding context inside the data*. The equivalent would be: instead of adding a Cartesian wire for the context, you smuggle a function pointer inside the focus value and extract it at use sites. This violates the separation between the optic's structure (how you decompose and reconstruct the whole) and the optic's data (what flows through the focus). The profunctor encoding works precisely because these are separate -- `p a b` is the focus computation, and the Cartesian/Cocartesian structure provides context. Merging them is not a type error, but it abandons the compositional structure that makes the encoding useful.
> 
> **Path 1 preserves compositionality. Path 2 does not.**
> 
> ## 2. Polarity vs. safety: different concerns, same region
> 
> You're right that the observation "ServiceHandle is already effectful" means there's no polarity violation in Path 2 per se. ServiceHandle is already classified as ↑(negative) -- a positive wrapper around a negative endpoint. It carries `write_tx`, it fires effects on Drop. Adding another effectful capability (access to dispatch) doesn't cross a polarity boundary.
> 
> But the unsafe cast is a *separate* concern, and here they interact badly:
> 
> - **Polarity concern**: "Is it correct to put dispatch access here?" Answer: no polarity violation, ServiceHandle is already effectful. Your round-1 observation was correct.
> 
> - **Rust safety concern**: The `*mut () → &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` cast is unsound if H is wrong. This is a runtime invariant that the type system cannot check.
> 
> - **Where they interact**: Polarity discipline tells you *which* operations compose safely across the actor boundary. The single-threaded actor model prevents non-associative cross-polarity composition (from the duploid analysis). But the unsafe cast introduces a *new* invariant -- the pointed-to Dispatch must outlive the ServiceHandle and have the right H -- that is *orthogonal* to polarity. Polarity discipline can't protect you here because polarity classifies *what kind of computation* something is (value vs. demand), not *whether a raw pointer aliases the right concrete type*.
> 
> So: polarity says Path 2 is categorically acceptable in terms of effect placement. Rust safety says Path 2 introduces a soundness hole that no amount of categorical discipline can close. These are different concerns, but the safety concern is a hard blocker regardless of the polarity analysis.
> 
> ## 3. Option (c) and existential quantification at the dispatch boundary
> 
> Option (c) introduces `&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps` -- in categorical terms, this is indeed existential quantification. Where the concrete type is `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (parameterized by the specific handler), the trait object erases H:
> 
> ```
> &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps  ≅  ∃H. &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;
> ```
> 
> Actually, more precisely, it's the *interface* that's quantified: the trait object provides a fixed set of methods (install entry, cancel, etc.) without revealing H. In coend notation from the reference material (`.gist/profunctor-optics/arxivmain.tex`, Definition at line 870-876):
> 
> ```
> Optic(A,B,S,T) = ∫^M C(S, M·A) × D(M·B, T)
> ```
> 
> The coend ∫^M existentially quantifies over the context M. Option (c)'s `dyn DispatchOps` similarly existentially quantifies over H at the trait boundary -- you get access to dispatch operations without knowing which handler type they're parameterized over.
> 
> **How this interacts with the labeled coproduct eliminator structure.** The service dispatch table is a labeled coproduct eliminator: for each protocol P, there's a branch that deserializes P::Message and calls `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive`. This is Cocartesian structure -- the `left`/`right` branching in the profunctor encoding. The critical question: does the existentially-quantified dispatch context compose with the coproduct elimination?
> 
> Yes, cleanly. The coproduct elimination happens in `ServiceDispatch` (choosing which protocol handler to invoke based on session_id). The dispatch context is *orthogonal* to this choice -- every branch needs the same `DispatchOps` interface. In categorical terms, the dispatch context is Cartesian (product context that threads through all branches), while protocol selection is Cocartesian (sum branches). These are independent constraints that compose via the affine optic structure (Cartesian + Cocartesian), which is exactly what the profunctor encoding predicts:
> 
> ```
> type AffineP s t a b = forall p. (Cartesian p, Cocartesian p) =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t
> ```
> 
> The dispatch context passes through the sum branches as a Cartesian wire. The existential quantification in `dyn DispatchOps` is fine because the Cartesian wire carries the *interface*, not the *implementation*.
> 
> **One concern with (c).** The trait object introduces dynamic dispatch at a hot path (every message dispatch). This is a Rust pragmatic concern, not a categorical one. Categorically, the existential quantification is correct. But you're paying vtable overhead per dispatch. Whether this matters depends on message rates.
> 
> ## 4. Which boundary solution preserves the cleanest categorical structure?
> 
> Let me evaluate each against the theoretical criteria:
> 
> **(a) Move Handles\&lt;P\&gt; to pane-app.** This makes the crate boundary match the categorical structure -- everything that touches the dispatch context lives in the same module. But it violates the functoriality principle: pane-proto is the membrane crate where session vocabulary lives (R1-R10, boundary rule finding). Moving Handles&lt;P&gt; out of pane-proto means protocol crates that impl Handles&lt;P&gt; must depend on pane-app, creating a dependency inversion. The categorical structure is preserved but the module structure is wrong.
> 
> **(b) Opaque raw pointer in pane-proto.** This is Path 2 dressed up as Path 1. It has the same soundness problem -- a raw pointer whose concrete type is unknown to pane-proto. The existential quantification is implicit and unsafe rather than explicit and safe. Categorically worse than (c).
> 
> **(c) Trait object `&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps`.** Existentially quantified, safe, categorically clean as analyzed above. The vtable overhead is the cost. The trait is defined in pane-proto (it's just method signatures), the impl is in pane-app on `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. This respects the crate boundary: pane-proto defines the interface, pane-app provides the implementation.
> 
> **(d) Generic type parameter.** `Handles&lt;P, Ctx = ???&gt;` with an associated type or generic parameter. Categorically this is universal quantification (∀Ctx) rather than existential, which is the *right* encoding from the profunctor perspective (profunctor optics are universally quantified: `forall p. C p =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t`). But as you noted, this is unstable/awkward in Rust -- GATs, associated type defaults, or where-clause elaboration would be needed to avoid infecting every impl site with the Ctx parameter.
> 
> **(e) My proposed option: DispatchOps as a supertrait of Handler, defined in pane-proto.**
> 
> Here's the idea. The insight is that Handler is already in pane-proto and is already the negative copattern base trait. Instead of adding a new parameter to `Handles&lt;P&gt;`, add the dispatch capability to the *handler's view of itself*:
> 
> ```rust
> // pane-proto
> pub trait DispatchAccess {
>     fn dispatch_ctx(&amp;mut self) -&gt; &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps;
> }
> 
> // Handles&lt;P&gt; stays unchanged:
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> The handler struct holds an opaque `DispatchSlot` (a `Box&lt;dyn DispatchOps&gt;` or similar) that pane-app installs before the event loop starts. The handler accesses it through `self.dispatch_ctx()` when it needs to install reply callbacks.
> 
> Wait -- this doesn't work either, because the handler doesn't know about dispatch. Let me reconsider.
> 
> Actually, the cleanest categorical option is **(c)**, but with a refinement. Define the trait in pane-proto:
> 
> ```rust
> // pane-proto/src/dispatch_ops.rs
> pub trait DispatchOps {
>     fn install_reply(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         session_id: u16,
>         on_reply: Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
>         on_failed: Box&lt;dyn FnOnce() -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle;
> }
> ```
> 
> Then the handler trait gains a context parameter:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> pane-app implements `DispatchOps` for a wrapper around `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (which knows H). The existential quantification hides H from pane-proto. The Cartesian wire (ctx) threads through the Cocartesian branching (protocol dispatch) cleanly.
> 
> **This is option (c) and it's my recommendation.**
> 
> The categorical structure: the dispatch context is a Cartesian extension of the handler's copattern. The existential quantification (∃H) at the trait object boundary matches the coend in the optic definition -- you're integrating over all possible handler types and retaining only the interface. The profunctor encoding's universal quantification (∀p. C p =&gt; ...) becomes, in Rust's type system, the trait object's existential (∃impl. DispatchOps for impl), and these are related by the end/coend duality from the reference material (line 592-593: "Ends can be thought of as some form of universal quantifier, whereas coends can be thought of as their existential counterparts"). The trait object is the coend; the universal quantification over profunctors satisfying the constraint is the end. They're dual constructions that serve the same structural purpose -- abstracting over the concrete context type.
> 
> **Closure-erased callbacks inside DispatchOps avoid the H-leakage problem.** The `on_reply` and `on_failed` closures capture `H` by closure, not by trait parameter. This is exactly the pattern already used in `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` -- the closures close over `&amp;mut H`, and the dispatch infrastructure is parameterized by H only at the impl site, not at the interface site. Option (c) extends this existing pattern to the handler-visible surface.
> 
> **Summary of my position:**
> 
> | | Categorical | Safety | Crate boundary | Perf |
> |---|---|---|---|---|
> | (a) | Clean | Safe | Violates membrane principle | Zero-cost |
> | (b) | Dirty (implicit ∃) | Unsafe | Respects | Zero-cost |
> | (c) | Clean (explicit ∃) | Safe | Respects | vtable per dispatch |
> | (d) | Cleanest (∀) | Safe | Infects all impls | Zero-cost |
> | (e) | N/A | - | - | - |
> 
> Option (c) has the right tradeoff. The vtable cost is one indirect call per message dispatch, which is negligible compared to the channel recv + deserialization already on the path. The categorical structure is preserved: explicit existential quantification at the crate boundary, Cartesian threading through Cocartesian branches, and the handler's copattern signature correctly reflects what the handler can access.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Deliberation: Path 1 vs Path 2" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. pane-app depends on pane-proto, not the reverse. Now I have the full picture. Let me formulate the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Path 1 (DispatchCtx), conditionally sound. Path 2 (DispatchSlot), unsound as described.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Path recommendation: Path 1, with option (c) for the crate boundary
> 
> ### Session-type justification
> 
> The core question is: where does the handler store sigma (the EAct continuation map) live relative to the handler dispatch? In Fowler/Hu's EAct (section 3.2, Definition 3.3), the handler store sigma is co-located with the handler -- the `suspend` primitive simultaneously registers a continuation in sigma AND yields to the event loop. The handler and its continuation store are accessed in the same atomic step.
> 
> This is precisely what DispatchCtx achieves. The handler receives `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` alongside the message, and `ctx` wraps `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. The `send_request` call on the ServiceHandle can install a `DispatchEntry` in the same dispatch step as the handler processes the triggering message. This makes `send_request` an E-Suspend (EAct section 3.2): register callback, continue execution, return Flow.
> 
> Path 2 (DispatchSlot on ServiceHandle) breaks this co-location. The ServiceHandle would load an `AtomicPtr&lt;()&gt;` and cast it to `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. But ServiceHandle lives in the handler's struct fields -- it's part of the handler state, not the dispatch context. The dispatch table is being accessed through a side channel (interior pointer) rather than through the structured context parameter. This is analogous to giving an EAct handler direct pointer access to the event loop's internal state rather than going through the `suspend` primitive.
> 
> DLfActRiS (Hinrichsen/Krebbers/Birkedal, POPL 2024, section 5, Definition 5.1) models the actor mailbox and internal state as separate resources in the separation logic. The protocol ownership assertion `own_prot(gamma, s)` tracks the protocol state `s` separately from the handler's resources. Path 1 respects this separation: `ctx` is the protocol state (looper-owned), `self` is the handler state (handler-owned). Path 2 smears the boundary by embedding protocol state access inside a handler-owned field.
> 
> The Be heritage confirms this. BHandler::MessageReceived received BMessage*, and the BHandler could call `BLooper* Looper()` to get the looper context. But Looper() returned a typed pointer -- not a raw `void*` cast. The context was accessed through the API surface, not through an interior unsafe mechanism. BeOS's `src/kits/app/Handler.cpp:277-281` shows that the handler's self-reference to its looper was always through the typed `fLooper` field, set by `BLooper::AddHandler()` (Looper.cpp:463). DispatchCtx is the pane equivalent of BHandler::Looper().
> 
> ### Why not Path 2
> 
> Beyond the structural argument, Path 2 has a concrete type safety problem (addressed in section 2 below) that Path 1 avoids entirely.
> 
> ## 2. Critical question: Path 2 type safety
> 
> **Path 2 is unsound. The H parameter is unchecked at the cast site.**
> 
> Here's the attack scenario. ServiceHandle has `send_request&lt;H, R&gt;()` where H is provided by the caller:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> ```
> 
> Under Path 2, `send_request` would load the `AtomicPtr&lt;()&gt;` from the ServiceHandle, cast it to `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, and call `insert()`. The problem: **nothing constrains H to be the actual handler type of the looper that set the pointer.**
> 
> Consider:
> 
> ```rust
> struct MyHandler {
>     clipboard: ServiceHandle&lt;Clipboard&gt;,
> }
> 
> struct EvilHandler {
>     secret: String,
> }
> 
> impl Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt; for MyHandler {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, _msg: ClipboardMessage) -&gt; Flow {
>         // H is inferred as MyHandler here, which is correct.
>         // But nothing stops this:
>         self.clipboard.send_request::&lt;EvilHandler, Reply&gt;(
>             msg,
>             |evil: &amp;mut EvilHandler, _, _| {
>                 // This callback will be stored in Dispatch&lt;EvilHandler&gt;,
>                 // but fire_reply will call it with &amp;mut MyHandler
>                 // reinterpreted as &amp;mut EvilHandler.
>                 evil.secret.push_str("corrupted");
>                 Flow::Continue
>             },
>             |_, _| Flow::Continue,
>         );
>         Flow::Continue
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is parameterized by `H`. If `send_request` loads a `*mut ()` and casts to `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;EvilHandler&gt;`, it inserts a `DispatchEntry&lt;EvilHandler&gt;`. But `fire_reply` in the looper calls the callback with `&amp;mut self.handler` which is `&amp;mut MyHandler`. The closure receives `&amp;mut EvilHandler` but is actually handed `&amp;mut MyHandler`. This is undefined behavior -- the two types have different layouts, sizes, and field offsets.
> 
> **Current code already has this latent issue.** Looking at `service_handle.rs:81-87`, `send_request&lt;H, R&gt;` takes H as a generic parameter with only `H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static`. There's no mechanism connecting H to the actual handler type of the LooperCore that will fire the callback. Right now it's harmless because the callbacks are never installed (line 112: `let _ = (on_reply, on_failed);`). But Path 2 would activate this unsoundness.
> 
> **Path 1 eliminates this entirely.** With `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`, the `Self` in the Handles impl IS the handler type. The DispatchCtx is parameterized by the same H as the LooperCore that created it. No cast, no type parameter mismatch possible. The Rust type system enforces the correspondence.
> 
> To make Path 2 sound, you'd need to verify at the `AtomicPtr` load site that H matches the concrete handler type. This requires either:
> - A TypeId check at runtime (losing the static guarantee, adding overhead per send_request)
> - Removing the H parameter from send_request entirely and using type erasure (which is essentially option (c) from Path 1)
> 
> Neither recovery preserves the claimed advantage of Path 2 ("no trait change").
> 
> ## 3. Does `dyn DispatchOps` affect session-type guarantees?
> 
> **No. The session-type guarantees are preserved because the critical properties are enforced at the trait boundary, not by monomorphization.**
> 
> The methods on `DispatchOps` would be something like:
> 
> ```rust
> trait DispatchOps {
>     fn insert_erased(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         session_id: u16,
>         on_reply: Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn Any, &amp;Messenger, Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
>         on_failed: Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn Any, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
>     ) -&gt; Token;
> }
> ```
> 
> The session-type properties at stake are:
> 
> 1. **Token uniqueness (S1):** Enforced by Dispatch's monotonic counter. The vtable dispatch doesn't touch this -- `insert_erased` calls the same `self.next_token += 1` path. S1 is preserved.
> 
> 2. **One-shot consumption (E-React):** Enforced by `HashMap::remove` in `fire_reply`. The vtable has no bearing on whether the entry is consumed. Preserved.
> 
> 3. **Protocol fidelity (Ferrite, Chen/Balzer/Toninho ECOOP 2022, section 4.2, Theorem 4.3):** The guarantee that every Request produces exactly one Reply or Failed. This is enforced by the request dispatch path in `ServiceDispatch::dispatch_request` (totality) and `ReplyPort` Drop compensation. The `dyn DispatchOps` trait is on the consumer send path, not the provider reply path. The totality invariant is unaffected.
> 
> 4. **Type erasure overhead:** The vtable adds one indirect call per `send_request`. This is in the same cost class as the existing `Box&lt;dyn FnOnce&gt;` closures already stored in `DispatchEntry`. The system already pays for type erasure at the dispatch entry level. Adding one more layer at the insert site is not a qualitative change.
> 
> **What IS lost with `dyn DispatchOps`:** The concrete type of the handler inside `DispatchEntry` callbacks. Currently, callbacks close over `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)` where H is known. With a `dyn DispatchOps` trait object, the insert call must use `dyn Any` downcasting inside the callback. This means:
> 
> - The `on_reply` and `on_failed` closures must downcast `&amp;mut dyn Any` to `&amp;mut H` at invocation time
> - A failed downcast (which would indicate a type confusion bug -- the same category as the Path 2 unsoundness) becomes a runtime panic rather than a compile error
> 
> This is strictly better than Path 2's unsoundness (panic vs UB), and the downcast can never fail in practice because the DispatchOps impl is created by the same LooperCore&lt;H&gt; that fires callbacks -- the H type is the same by construction. But it's a gap: the compile-time guarantee that `on_reply` receives the correct handler type is replaced by a runtime downcast. This is a known cost of putting `Handles&lt;P&gt;` in pane-proto while `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` lives in pane-app.
> 
> **Better option (c) variant:** Instead of full `dyn Any` erasure, make `DispatchOps` generic-free but have `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` implement it by capturing H in the implementation. The trait methods take `&amp;mut self` and internally know H because the implementing struct is `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`. The callbacks stored in the `DispatchEntry` are constructed inside the LooperCore's implementation of `DispatchOps::insert_erased`, where H is in scope. This way, the `dyn DispatchOps` trait object in pane-proto carries no H parameter, but the implementation in pane-app is fully typed. No downcast needed.
> 
> ```rust
> // pane-proto (no H parameter):
> pub trait DispatchOps {
>     fn insert_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         session_id: u16,
>         on_reply_box: Box&lt;dyn std::any::Any + Send&gt;,  // actually Box&lt;FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)&gt;
>         on_failed_box: Box&lt;dyn std::any::Any + Send&gt;,  // actually Box&lt;FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)&gt;
>     ) -&gt; u64;  // token
> }
> 
> // pane-app:
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; DispatchOps for LooperCore&lt;H&gt; { ... }
> ```
> 
> The type erasure is at the trait object boundary only. Inside the implementation, the closure types are known. The `dyn Any` boxes are downcast once, inside pane-app, where H is known. This preserves the static guarantee within pane-app while allowing pane-proto to reference dispatch operations without naming H.
> 
> ## 4. Practical example: notification triggers request, reply updates state
> 
> ### Scenario
> 
> A text editor handler receives a `Clipboard::ContentChanged` notification. In response, it sends a `Clipboard::GetContent` request. The on_reply callback updates the handler's paste buffer.
> 
> ### Path 1: DispatchCtx
> 
> ```rust
> impl Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt; for EditorHandler {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: ClipboardMessage, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             ClipboardMessage::ContentChanged { format } =&gt; {
>                 if self.accepts_format(&amp;format) {
>                     // E-Suspend: install callback + send request in one dispatch step.
>                     // ctx provides &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;Self&gt; — H is Self by construction.
>                     ctx.send_request(
>                         &amp;self.clipboard,      // ServiceHandle&lt;Clipboard&gt;
>                         ClipboardMessage::GetContent,
>                         |handler: &amp;mut Self, _messenger, content: ClipboardContent| {
>                             // E-React: this fires when Reply arrives.
>                             handler.paste_buffer = content.data;
>                             handler.paste_format = content.format;
>                             Flow::Continue
>                         },
>                         |handler: &amp;mut Self, _messenger| {
>                             // Failure: clipboard service died between
>                             // ContentChanged and our GetContent request.
>                             handler.paste_buffer.clear();
>                             Flow::Continue
>                         },
>                     );
>                 }
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>             _ =&gt; Flow::Continue,
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The lifecycle mapping:
> 1. **E-Suspend** (EAct section 3.2): `ctx.send_request()` registers the (on_reply, on_failed) pair in Dispatch&lt;H&gt; with a token, serializes GetContent to the wire. Handler returns Flow::Continue. Looper continues.
> 2. **E-React** (EAct section 3.2): When Reply arrives, LooperCore's `dispatch_service` matches `ServiceFrame::Reply`, calls `dispatch.fire_reply()`. The entry is consumed (HashMap::remove). The on_reply closure fires with `&amp;mut self.handler`. paste_buffer is updated. Snapshot published.
> 3. **Affine gap compensation:** If the handler panics between steps 1 and 2, the DispatchEntry is cleaned up by `run_destruction` (fail_connection or clear). The entry's on_failed fires if fail_connection reaches it, or the closure is dropped silently by clear. Either way, no orphan. If the Reply never arrives (service crashes), ServiceTeardown triggers `fail_session`, which fires on_failed for all entries on that session.
> 
> ### Path 2: DispatchSlot
> 
> ```rust
> impl Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt; for EditorHandler {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: ClipboardMessage) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             ClipboardMessage::ContentChanged { format } =&gt; {
>                 if self.accepts_format(&amp;format) {
>                     // send_request on ServiceHandle loads AtomicPtr,
>                     // casts to &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;Self&gt;, inserts entry.
>                     self.clipboard.send_request::&lt;Self, ClipboardContent&gt;(
>                         ClipboardMessage::GetContent,
>                         |handler, _messenger, content| {
>                             handler.paste_buffer = content.data;
>                             handler.paste_format = content.format;
>                             Flow::Continue
>                         },
>                         |handler, _messenger| {
>                             handler.paste_buffer.clear();
>                             Flow::Continue
>                         },
>                     );
>                 }
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>             _ =&gt; Flow::Continue,
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The lifecycle mapping is the same, BUT:
> - The `Self` in `send_request::&lt;Self, ClipboardContent&gt;` is not enforced. Nothing prevents `send_request::&lt;WrongType, ClipboardContent&gt;`. This is the type safety gap from section 2.
> - The AtomicPtr load/cast is inside `send_request`. The bracket (set pointer before dispatch, clear after) is enforced by LooperCore, not by the type system. If LooperCore's `dispatch()` method fails to set the pointer (bug), `send_request` dereferences null or a stale pointer. UB.
> - The `unsafe` is tightly scoped to the single-threaded context, but the LooperCore must maintain the bracket invariant across all dispatch paths (lifecycle, service, ServiceTeardown). Every new dispatch path added in the future must remember to set/clear the pointer. This is a maintenance hazard.
> 
> ### Which preserves E-Suspend/E-React better?
> 
> **Path 1.** The correspondence is structural:
> 
> | EAct concept | Path 1 | Path 2 |
> |---|---|---|
> | E-Suspend (register callback) | `ctx.send_request()` -- ctx IS the handler store | `self.clipboard.send_request()` -- loads pointer to handler store |
> | E-React (consume callback) | `dispatch.fire_reply()` -- same Dispatch | same |
> | Handler store sigma | `DispatchCtx` wraps `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` | AtomicPtr inside ServiceHandle |
> | Type safety | H = Self by construction | H is caller-provided, unchecked |
> | Install-before-wire | ctx.send_request installs entry, then serializes | same (if pointer is valid) |
> 
> The install-before-wire property deserves emphasis. In both paths, the Dispatch entry must be installed before the serialized request hits the wire. Otherwise, a fast Reply could arrive before the entry exists, and fire_reply would return None (entry not found -- Reply dropped). Path 1 makes this natural: ctx.send_request controls the ordering internally. Path 2 also does this if the implementation is correct, but the ordering guarantee is inside the unsafe block, not visible at the type level.
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> | Criterion | Path 1 (DispatchCtx) | Path 2 (DispatchSlot) |
> |---|---|---|
> | Type safety | Sound (H = Self by construction) | **Unsound** (H unchecked at cast) |
> | Unsafe | None | `AtomicPtr` load + cast |
> | Crate boundary | Needs option (c): `dyn DispatchOps` in pane-proto | No trait change |
> | E-Suspend fidelity | Direct (ctx IS the handler store) | Indirect (pointer to handler store) |
> | Maintenance | DispatchOps trait is explicit contract | Bracket invariant is implicit |
> | Be heritage | BHandler::Looper() | No direct analogue |
> | DLfActRiS separation | Respects handler/protocol state separation | Smears the boundary |
> 
> **Recommendation:** Path 1 with the `dyn DispatchOps` variant from section 3 (type-erased trait in pane-proto, concrete implementation in pane-app, no downcast needed in the hot path). This resolves the crate boundary blocker while preserving all session-type guarantees statically.
> 
> The `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` signature change from `fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow` to `fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, ctx: &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps) -&gt; Flow` is a breaking change to the trait, but pane-proto is pre-1.0 and the serena memory `pane/no_stability_commitment` explicitly permits this.</result>

> </task-notification>

> ---
> 
> ## Recommendation: Path 2 (DispatchSlot on ServiceHandle), with one important refinement
> 
> ### 1. The Haiku precedent: BHandler::fLooper
> 
> The Haiku source makes the precedent crystal clear. Look at `BHandler` (Handler.h:102-103, Handler.cpp:646-648):
> 
> ```cpp
> // Handler.h
> BLooper*    fLooper;    // set by BLooper::AddHandler
> 
> // Handler.cpp
> void BHandler::SetLooper(BLooper* looper) {
>     fLooper = looper;
> }
> ```
> 
> Every `BHandler` carries a raw pointer to its owning `BLooper`. This pointer is set when the handler is added to a looper (`BLooper::AddHandler` calls `handler-&gt;SetLooper(this)`) and cleared when removed. During dispatch, the handler can call `Looper()` to get back to its owning looper and use `PostMessage`, `Lock`, etc.
> 
> **This is exactly Path 2's pattern.** The handler holds a pointer to the framework infrastructure, set before dispatch, available during dispatch. It was never part of the `MessageReceived` signature (Path 1) -- it was ambient state on the handler, reachable through `Looper()`.
> 
> The critical thing about Haiku's design: `fLooper` is set once at `AddHandler` time and stays valid for the entire lifetime of the handler's association with that looper. It's not bracketed around individual dispatches. This is stronger than what Path 2 proposes (set/clear around dispatch), but also simpler.
> 
> ### 2. Why Path 1's trait-level DispatchCtx is the wrong level of abstraction
> 
> In Haiku, `BHandler::MessageReceived(BMessage*)` takes one argument. The handler's access to the looper is through `this-&gt;fLooper`, not through a parameter. This wasn't an oversight -- it was deliberate. The Be Newsletter Issue 4-29 explains the architecture: the handler lives *inside* a looper, and the looper is the handler's ambient context. Passing the looper as a parameter to every message receipt would have implied that the handler might receive messages in different looper contexts, which was never true.
> 
> The same holds for pane. A handler is associated with exactly one `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`, which owns exactly one `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. The dispatch table doesn't change per-message -- it's the same table for the handler's entire lifetime. Making it a parameter on `receive()` would overstate the dynamism.
> 
> Beyond the conceptual argument, Path 1 has a concrete structural problem. Look at the crate boundary:
> 
> - `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is in pane-proto (handles.rs:24). It's there because Handler has a blanket impl `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` (handler.rs:48).
> - `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is in pane-app (dispatch.rs:47). It depends on `Messenger` which is pane-app, and its `DispatchEntry` callbacks take `&amp;mut H` and `&amp;crate::Messenger`.
> 
> Of the four sub-options for crossing this:
> 
> **(a) Move Handles&lt;P&gt; to pane-app** -- Breaks the blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` in handler.rs:48, which requires both `Handler` and `Handles` to be in the same crate. You'd have to move Handler too, which guts pane-proto.
> 
> **(b) Opaque type in pane-proto with raw pointer interior** -- Duplicating the unsafe that Path 2 is proposing, but in a more awkward place (a phantom type in a crate that's supposed to be pure protocol vocabulary).
> 
> **(c) Trait object `dyn DispatchOps` in pane-proto** -- This is the cleanest Path 1 variant, but now every `receive()` call pays for dynamic dispatch on `DispatchOps`, and the trait object has to type-erase the dispatch insertion. You're losing the type information that makes `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` sound. The insert method needs to accept `Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...) -&gt; Flow&gt;`, but `H` is on the other side of the trait object boundary, so you'd need `dyn DispatchOps&lt;H&gt;` which still requires `H` in scope at the pane-proto level.
> 
> **(d) Associated type default `Ctx=()`** -- Unstable Rust. Non-starter.
> 
> None of these are good. Path 1 fights the crate structure because `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is vocabulary and `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is runtime. Mixing them is a layering violation.
> 
> ### 3. The refined Path 2: not on ServiceHandle, on LooperCore's dispatch bracket
> 
> The Plan 9 agent's original insight is right, but I'd refine the placement. Rather than putting an `AtomicPtr` on `ServiceHandle`, look at how `LooperCore::dispatch_service` already works (looper_core.rs:254-328):
> 
> ```rust
> pub(crate) fn dispatch_service(&amp;mut self, session_id: u16, payload: &amp;[u8]) -&gt; DispatchOutcome {
>     // ...
>     pane_proto::ServiceFrame::Notification { payload: inner } =&gt; {
>         let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
>         let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;
>         let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;
>         // ...
>         service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(session_id, handler, messenger, &amp;inner)
>     }
> ```
> 
> `LooperCore` already does a split borrow: it borrows `self.handler` mutably and `self.service_dispatch` and `self.messenger` immutably. The problem is that `self.dispatch` (the request/reply table) isn't available to the handler during this call.
> 
> The natural fix is: `ServiceHandle::send_request` doesn't need to touch `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` directly. It needs to *produce* a `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` and hand it back to the framework. The framework installs it.
> 
> Here's the concrete mechanism, no unsafe needed:
> 
> **send_request returns a DispatchEntry instead of installing it.** The looper core calls `dispatch.insert(...)` with the entry. The ServiceHandle doesn't need a pointer to the Dispatch at all.
> 
> But wait -- `send_request` is called by user code *inside* a handler dispatch, and the handler has `&amp;self` access to its ServiceHandle fields. The handler can't call back into the framework to install the entry because `&amp;mut self` is exclusively borrowed by the dispatch call.
> 
> This is where the Haiku pattern applies. In Haiku, `BHandler::Looper()` returned the looper pointer that was set at AddHandler time. The handler used it to call `PostMessage()`. The equivalent in pane: **Messenger already exists as the "ambient framework handle" passed to handler methods.** The right answer is to make `Messenger` the vehicle for dispatch entry installation, not ServiceHandle.
> 
> But Messenger is currently passed as `&amp;Messenger` (immutable). And `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` needs `&amp;mut`. This is the real tension.
> 
> The clean solution: **a side channel from Messenger to LooperCore for dispatch entries.** Same pattern as `PostMessage` in Be -- the handler doesn't directly mutate the looper's queue; it sends through a port. In pane, `send_request` on the ServiceHandle pushes a `DispatchEntry` into a thread-local or a channel that the LooperCore drains after the handler returns from receive.
> 
> But we're single-threaded, so it's simpler than that. A `RefCell&lt;Vec&lt;PendingEntry&lt;H&gt;&gt;&gt;` on the LooperCore, shared with Messenger via an `Rc` or a raw pointer, would work. But that's complex for what's actually needed.
> 
> Let me step back and look at what Haiku *actually* does for the analogous operation. When a handler wants to send a message that requires a reply, it calls:
> 
> ```cpp
> BMessenger msgr(target);
> msgr.SendMessage(&amp;msg, this);  // reply comes back to 'this' handler
> ```
> 
> The reply address is embedded in the *sent* message (reply_port, reply_target, reply_team -- see `beapi_internals` memory on BMessage). The looper's task_looper reads the reply from the port and dispatches it through the normal handler chain. There is no "dispatch entry table" in Haiku -- replies are just messages with the IS_REPLY flag set, dispatched like any other message.
> 
> Pane's `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is a *better* design than what Be had -- typed callbacks keyed by token, consumed on use, no ghost state. But the mechanism for installing entries during handler dispatch is a new problem that Be didn't face because Be didn't have entries to install.
> 
> ### 4. My recommended solution: two-phase send_request
> 
> Here's what I'd actually build. No unsafe, no crate boundary surgery:
> 
> **`send_request` on ServiceHandle sends the wire frame immediately (it already has write_tx) and returns a `PendingReply&lt;H&gt;` value.** The handler stores this value (or passes it to the framework). The LooperCore, after `receive()` returns, collects pending entries.
> 
> Concretely:
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-proto (no Dispatch dependency):
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
>     // No change to signature.
> }
> 
> // In pane-app, ServiceHandle:
> pub fn send_request&lt;R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     R: pane_proto::Message,
> {
>     // 1. Serialize and send the wire frame (already works)
>     // 2. Build DispatchEntry
>     // 3. Push entry to the install channel
>     // 4. Return CancelHandle
> }
> ```
> 
> The "install channel" is a `Vec&lt;(PeerScope, DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;)&gt;` behind a shared mutable cell. LooperCore owns the Vec; it shares a write-reference with ServiceHandle. Since everything is single-threaded, `RefCell` is sound.
> 
> But ServiceHandle doesn't have `H` in its type... Actually, looking at send_request (service_handle.rs:81-113), it already takes `H` as a type parameter on the method. So the callback types are known. The issue is getting the entry into the Dispatch.
> 
> **Simplest correct path**: LooperCore, before dispatching to the handler, sets a thread-local `Option&lt;*mut dyn DispatchInstaller&gt;` where `DispatchInstaller` is a pane-app-internal trait. `send_request` loads it, installs the entry. LooperCore clears it after dispatch.
> 
> This is essentially Path 2 scoped to a single-purpose vtable rather than the full Dispatch. The unsafe is:
> - One `thread_local!` storing a raw pointer
> - Set before dispatch, cleared after
> - Single-threaded (pane's fundamental invariant)
> - The pointer target lives in LooperCore, which is alive for the entire dispatch
> 
> This is morally equivalent to `BHandler::fLooper`: a pointer to the owning framework, set at association time (dispatch bracket in this case), used during dispatch.
> 
> ### 5. Comparison of unsafe scope: Haiku vs. this proposal
> 
> In Haiku's BLooper (Looper.cpp:1162-1279, `task_looper`):
> - `fLastMessage` is set, handler dispatched, fLastMessage cleared -- same bracket pattern
> - `fLooper` on BHandler is a raw C++ pointer set at AddHandler time, never checked for validity during dispatch
> - Thread safety is the developer's responsibility (Lock/Unlock)
> - Priority inversion, dangling pointer after delete, aliasing after realloc -- all real bugs
> 
> In pane Path 2 (refined):
> - Thread-local pointer set/cleared around dispatch -- same bracket as fLastMessage
> - Single-threaded guarantee (no Lock/Unlock needed -- Rust borrow checker enforces)
> - Pointer target lifetime is LooperCore, which is alive by construction during dispatch
> - No aliasing possible (thread-local, not shared between loopers)
> 
> The unsafe surface is dramatically smaller than Haiku's. One thread-local store/load/clear per dispatch cycle. The Plan 9 agent's `AtomicPtr` on ServiceHandle is also fine, but a thread-local on the dispatch side is even tighter because it doesn't add any fields to ServiceHandle's public surface.
> 
> ### 6. Practical example: handler handling notifications from protocol A and sending requests to protocol B
> 
> **Path 2 (recommended):**
> 
> ```rust
> struct Bridge {
>     weather_handle: ServiceHandle&lt;WeatherProto&gt;,
>     forecast_count: u32,
> }
> 
> impl Handler for Bridge {}
> 
> // Receive notifications from protocol A (Weather)
> impl Handles&lt;WeatherProto&gt; for Bridge {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: WeatherMessage) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             WeatherMessage::Alert { region, severity } =&gt; {
>                 // Send request to protocol B (Forecast) for details.
>                 // send_request internally pushes a DispatchEntry to
>                 // the thread-local install channel. LooperCore drains
>                 // it after receive() returns.
>                 let _cancel = self.weather_handle.send_request::&lt;Bridge, ForecastReply&gt;(
>                     ForecastMessage::GetForecast { region: region.clone() },
>                     |handler, _messenger, reply: ForecastReply| {
>                         handler.forecast_count += 1;
>                         println!("forecast for {}: {:?}", reply.region, reply.conditions);
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                     |handler, _messenger| {
>                         println!("forecast request failed");
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                 );
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Path 1 (DispatchCtx), for comparison:**
> 
> ```rust
> // Would require changing Handles trait signature:
> impl Handles&lt;WeatherProto&gt; for Bridge {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: WeatherMessage, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             WeatherMessage::Alert { region, severity } =&gt; {
>                 // ctx provides dispatch access
>                 let _cancel = ctx.send_request(
>                     &amp;self.weather_handle,
>                     ForecastMessage::GetForecast { region: region.clone() },
>                     |handler, messenger, reply: ForecastReply| {
>                         handler.forecast_count += 1;
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                     |handler, messenger| {
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                 );
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The Path 1 version forces `ctx` into every `receive` signature, even for handlers that never send requests. Every `Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt;` blanket impl has to thread the ctx through. The blanket impl in handler.rs:48-57 becomes:
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: LifecycleMessage, _ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow {
>         // ctx unused for lifecycle messages
>         match msg { ... }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> That `_ctx` on every lifecycle dispatch is wasted parameter passing and cognitive load. The BeOS principle was that the framework context is ambient (Looper()), not threaded through every call site.
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> | Criterion | Path 1 (DispatchCtx) | Path 2 (thread-local install) |
> |---|---|---|
> | Crate boundary | Requires surgery (options a-d all have costs) | No boundary crossed |
> | Trait signature | Pollutes Handles&lt;P&gt; for all protocols | No change |
> | Haiku precedent | No -- BHandler::MessageReceived didn't take a looper param | Yes -- BHandler::fLooper was ambient framework access |
> | Unsafe scope | Zero (if option c), or same (if option b) | One thread-local ptr per dispatch cycle |
> | Categorical cleanliness | Clean (positive context wrapping negative dispatch) | Clean (ServiceHandle already effectful, per optics agent) |
> | Session type fidelity | Atomic install (entry exists before handler can observe reply) | Entry installed after receive() returns, before next dispatch; reply can't arrive earlier because single-threaded |
> 
> The session type consultant's concern about atomicity is addressed by single-threaded dispatch: the reply can't arrive during the handler's receive() call because the same thread that's running receive() is the thread that would dispatch the reply. The entry is installed between dispatch cycles, which is exactly when it needs to be ready.
> 
> **I recommend Path 2 with the thread-local install channel refinement.** It follows the Haiku precedent (ambient framework access, not explicit parameter), respects the crate layering (pane-proto stays pure vocabulary), and the unsafe is tightly scoped and sound by construction.</result>
