### `2026-04-06T23:03:44.007Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>abbe8862b77ac7872</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017DmC2g77kjMoTjW7E6kzo3</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/abbe8862b77ac7872.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Deliberation: Path 1 vs Path 2" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have a thorough picture. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Recommendation: Path 2 (DispatchSlot on ServiceHandle), with a specific constraint on the unsafe bracket.**
> 
> Here's the full analysis across all four questions.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Which path, and why
> 
> Path 2. Three reasons, grounded in what I've seen work and fail.
> 
> **The crate boundary is the real constraint, not a solvable subproblem.** Path 1's four crate-crossing solutions each carry meaningful cost:
> 
> - **(a) Move Handles&lt;P&gt; to pane-app:** Collapses the pane-proto/pane-app separation. pane-proto is the vocabulary crate with no IO, no dispatch, no framework coupling. Moving Handles&lt;P&gt; there pulls protocol-level message receiving into the framework crate. Every downstream consumer now depends on pane-app. This is architectural damage that costs more than the benefit.
> 
> - **(b) Opaque type with raw pointer interior:** You pay the same unsafe cost as Path 2 but add a new public type in pane-proto whose entire purpose is to hide an unsafe cast. This is worse — the unsafety is laundered through a type boundary instead of being visible at the one call site.
> 
> - **(c) Trait object `dyn DispatchOps`:** Viable, but you've now introduced a trait in pane-proto whose sole implementor lives in pane-app. The trait becomes a permanent API surface for something that's really an implementation detail of the looper. And the `dyn` dispatch adds a vtable indirection on every handler call — not a performance concern today, but an unnecessary permanent cost.
> 
> - **(d) Generic type parameter on Handles:** `Handles&lt;P, Ctx = ()&gt;` means every existing `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impl, every test, every macro, and every downstream consumer now carries a type parameter that's `()` 99% of the time. This is the kind of generalization that Plan 9 specifically avoided — parameterizing interfaces for cases that don't exist yet.
> 
> **Plan 9's exportfs shows the pattern.** Look at the dispatch table in `exportfs.c:21-36`:
> 
> ```c
> void (*fcalls[])(Fsrpc*) =
> {
>     [Tversion]  Xversion,
>     [Tattach]   Xattach,
>     [Twalk]     Xwalk,
>     [Topen]     slave,
>     [Tread]     slave,
>     ...
> };
> ```
> 
> The handlers (`Xwalk`, `Xversion`, etc.) access the dispatch infrastructure — `reply()`, `getfid()`, `newfid()`, `file()` — through **module-level state**. `netfd` is a global (`exportfs.c:45`). `fhash` is a global (`exportfs.h:95`). `Workq` is a global (`exportfs.h:91`). The handlers don't receive a context parameter. They reach into shared state that the main loop set up before dispatch began.
> 
> This is the C equivalent of Path 2: the infrastructure is available through a pointer that the main loop establishes, and the handlers access it through that pointer. No context parameter threaded through the handler signature.
> 
> Plan 9 made this choice because the handler signature is the public interface — it's what every 9P server implementor writes to. Adding a context parameter to every handler changes every implementation. The infrastructure access is a framework concern, not a protocol concern. Keeping it out of the handler signature keeps the protocol layer clean.
> 
> **The Be precedent the Be-agent cites doesn't quite work the way they describe.** Yes, `BHandler::MessageReceived` had `Looper()` access. But `Looper()` returned the already-known looper — it was a field on BHandler, set when AddHandler was called (`src/kits/app/Handler.cpp:73-79`). It wasn't threaded through MessageReceived's signature. It was ambient state on `this`. pane's equivalent of `this-&gt;fLooper` is having the dispatch infrastructure accessible through the handler's own ServiceHandle, which is exactly what Path 2 does.
> 
> ## 2. The unsafe pattern: comparison with Plan 9's `Mnt *m` and `up`
> 
> The proposed `Arc&lt;AtomicPtr&lt;()&gt;&gt;` on ServiceHandle, set by LooperCore before dispatch and cleared after, is structurally analogous to two Plan 9 patterns:
> 
> **`Mntrpc.m` (devmnt.c:27).** Each outstanding RPC has `Mnt *m` — the mount point it's operating on. This is set in `mountio()` at line 787: `r-&gt;m = m;`. It's set before the RPC is queued and used throughout the lifetime of the request. It's not a context parameter to a handler — it's state on the request object, established by the framework before the handler runs.
> 
> **`up` (the per-process Proc pointer).** `up` is the current process, set by the scheduler on context switch. Every kernel function uses it — `mountrpc()` at line 764 prints `up-&gt;text, up-&gt;pid`, `mountio()` at line 777 checks `m-&gt;rip == up`, `mntrdwr()` at line 731 checks `up-&gt;nnote`. It's per-thread ambient state, not threaded through function signatures. The set/clear bracket is the scheduler's context switch.
> 
> **Is the set/clear bracket safe?** Yes, with two conditions:
> 
> 1. **Single-threaded dispatch.** pane's looper dispatches sequentially on one thread (I6). This means the set/clear bracket is not racy — no other thread accesses the pointer between set and clear. This matches `up`, which is per-CPU and only changed by the scheduler on that CPU.
> 
> 2. **catch_unwind must clear.** If the handler panics, the pointer must be cleared in the unwind path, not left dangling. LooperCore already wraps dispatch in `catch_unwind` (`looper_core.rs:154`). The clear must be in a scope guard or equivalent — not after the dispatch call, because a panic would skip it.
> 
> The concrete implementation should look like:
> 
> ```rust
> // In LooperCore::dispatch_service, before calling the handler:
> self.service_dispatch.set_dispatch_ptr(&amp;self.dispatch as *const _ as *mut ());
> let _guard = scopeguard::guard((), |_| {
>     self.service_dispatch.clear_dispatch_ptr();
> });
> // ... dispatch ...
> ```
> 
> Or equivalently, use a struct with Drop. The critical invariant: **the pointer is valid for exactly the duration of the handler call, and clearing is unwind-safe.**
> 
> The `AtomicPtr` is overkill for single-threaded access — a `Cell&lt;*mut ()&gt;` would be sufficient and cheaper. But `AtomicPtr` is fine if ServiceHandle needs to be `Send` (which it does, since it's stored on handler structs that are `Send`).
> 
> **What this is NOT.** This is not the `up` pattern in the dangerous sense — `up` was a truly global per-CPU variable that the scheduler swapped. pane's pointer is per-ServiceHandle, scoped to one looper, cleared deterministically. The blast radius of a bug is one pane, not the kernel.
> 
> ## 3. How Plan 9 handled the compilation unit boundary
> 
> Plan 9 did **not** separate handler definitions from dispatch infrastructure at a compilation boundary.
> 
> In exportfs, the handlers (`Xwalk`, `Xversion`, etc.) are in `exportsrv.c`, the dispatch loop is in `exportfs.c`, and both share `exportfs.h`. They compile into a single binary. The globals (`netfd`, `fhash`, `Workq`, `messagesize`) are declared `Extern` in the header, defined once in the main file.
> 
> In the kernel's devmnt.c, the entire client-side 9P implementation — mount table, tag management, RPC dispatch, reply muxing — is one compilation unit. `mountrpc()`, `mountio()`, `mountmux()`, `mntchk()` are all in the same file. The dev table (`devtab[]`) dispatches to per-device functions (mntread, mntwrite, etc.), but each device is its own compilation unit and the device functions access the mount infrastructure through the `Chan *c` argument, which carries `c-&gt;mchan-&gt;mux` — the Mnt pointer. Look at `mntchk()` (`devmnt.c:1130-1151`):
> 
> ```c
> Mnt*
> mntchk(Chan *c)
> {
>     Mnt *m;
>     if(c-&gt;mchan == nil)
>         panic("mntchk 1: nil mchan c %s\n", chanpath(c));
>     m = c-&gt;mchan-&gt;mux;
>     ...
>     return m;
> }
> ```
> 
> Every mnt operation starts with `m = mntchk(c)` — extracting the dispatch infrastructure from the channel. The channel is the analog of pane's ServiceHandle. The Mnt is the analog of Dispatch&lt;H&gt;.
> 
> **The key insight:** Plan 9 solved this by putting the infrastructure pointer *on the handle* (`Chan.mchan.mux`), not by threading it through the handler function signature. This is exactly Path 2's approach: the dispatch pointer lives on ServiceHandle, extracted when needed.
> 
> The compilation boundary in Plan 9 was between devices (`devmnt.c` vs `devsrv.c` vs `devproc.c`), not between protocol handlers and dispatch infrastructure within one device. pane's pane-proto/pane-app split is a more aggressive separation than Plan 9 ever attempted. Path 2 avoids needing to bridge it.
> 
> ## 4. Practical example: nested Tread from a Twalk handler
> 
> Consider a 9P server that receives `Twalk` for path `/remote/foo` and needs to issue a `Tread` to an underlying server to resolve `foo`.
> 
> ### Plan 9 (exportfs)
> 
> In exportfs, `Xwalk()` (`exportsrv.c:155`) handles Twalk. It needs to stat the underlying filesystem to resolve the walk elements. It does this by calling `file()` (`exportfs.c:617`), which calls `dirstat(path)` — a normal system call that goes through the kernel's devmnt to the underlying server. The dispatch infrastructure (`reply()`, `netfd`) is accessed through globals:
> 
> ```c
> void Xwalk(Fsrpc *t) {
>     // ... resolve walk elements via file() → dirstat() ...
>     // file() calls dirstat() which does a Twalk+Tstat to the underlying server
>     // through the kernel's devmnt — transparent to Xwalk
>     
>     reply(&amp;t-&gt;work, &amp;rhdr, e);  // reply() uses global netfd
>     t-&gt;busy = 0;
> }
> ```
> 
> The nested file operation happens *through the kernel's namespace*, not through the exportfs dispatch infrastructure. exportfs doesn't do 9P multiplexing in the handler — the kernel does. The handler calls ordinary syscalls, the kernel translates them to 9P operations on the underlying mount.
> 
> ### pane under Path 2
> 
> A handler receives a request on protocol P and needs to send a nested request on protocol Q to an underlying service:
> 
> ```rust
> impl HandlesRequest&lt;FsProto&gt; for MyHandler {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: FsMessage,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;FsReply&gt;,
>     ) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             FsMessage::Walk { path } =&gt; {
>                 // Need to resolve `path` by querying the underlying service.
>                 // self.underlying is a ServiceHandle&lt;BackendProto&gt; obtained
>                 // from PaneBuilder::open_service during setup.
>                 self.underlying.send_request::&lt;Self, BackendReply&gt;(
>                     BackendMessage::Stat { path },
>                     move |handler, _messenger, stat_reply| {
>                         // Callback fires when the underlying server replies.
>                         // `reply` was moved into this closure.
>                         reply.reply(FsReply::WalkResult(stat_reply.qid));
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                     move |handler, _messenger| {
>                         // Underlying server failed — propagate failure.
>                         // reply is dropped → ReplyPort Drop sends Failed
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                 );
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Under Path 2, `self.underlying.send_request()` accesses the dispatch pointer that LooperCore set before calling this handler. It loads the `AtomicPtr`, casts to `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;Self&gt;`, and calls `dispatch.insert()` to install the reply callback. The pointer is valid because we're inside the dispatch bracket.
> 
> ### pane under Path 1 (for comparison)
> 
> ```rust
> impl HandlesRequest&lt;FsProto&gt; for MyHandler {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: FsMessage,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;FsReply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;,  // additional parameter
>     ) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             FsMessage::Walk { path } =&gt; {
>                 ctx.send_request(
>                     &amp;self.underlying,
>                     BackendMessage::Stat { path },
>                     move |handler, messenger, stat_reply| {
>                         reply.reply(FsReply::WalkResult(stat_reply.qid));
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                     move |handler, messenger| {
>                         Flow::Continue
>                     },
>                 );
>                 Flow::Continue
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The difference is small at the call site. The ergonomic cost of Path 1 is the `ctx` parameter on every handler — for handlers that never issue nested requests, it's dead weight. The architectural cost is the crate boundary crossing.
> 
> ### Exportfs analogy verdict
> 
> The exportfs pattern most closely matches Path 2. The handler (`Xwalk`) accesses dispatch infrastructure (`reply()`, `netfd`) through ambient state, not through a parameter. The nested file operations go through system calls that the kernel routes through devmnt — the handler doesn't explicitly manage the 9P mux for nested requests. In pane, `send_request` on a ServiceHandle is the equivalent of that syscall: the handler calls it, the framework handles the muxing.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Confidence level: High for the recommendation, medium for specific AtomicPtr implementation details.** The architectural argument (keep handler signature clean, put infrastructure on the handle) is well-grounded in both Plan 9 practice and the existing codebase structure. The exact unsafe mechanism needs careful review of the scope guard / unwind interaction, which I haven't verified against the current catch_unwind path in detail.</result>

> soundness result wins, but lets forward the ergonomic concern to the agents and see if they have an ideas on how to refine the proposal based on it

### `2026-04-06T23:08:01.458Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ad9b4dfb95aa855fa</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01SavpTpKkqbVSe6UYrjTTGi</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/ad9b4dfb95aa855fa.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Refine: ctx ergonomics on Handles" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have a thorough understanding of both sides. Let me write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Three questions, three answers. I'll go through them with source citations, then give you a concrete recommendation.
> 
> ## Question 1: Did 9P server handlers that didn't need nested requests still receive the full context?
> 
> **Yes, uniformly.** Look at the lib9p `Srv` callback table (`reference/plan9/man/2/9p:31-58`):
> 
> ```c
> void (*read)(Req *r);
> void (*write)(Req *r);
> void (*open)(Req *r);
> void (*stat)(Req *r);
> // ... all take (Req *r)
> ```
> 
> Every handler takes `Req *r`. The `Req` struct carries `r-&gt;srv` (back-pointer to the server), `r-&gt;fid`, `r-&gt;ifcall`, `r-&gt;ofcall`, and `r-&gt;aux`. Most read/write/stat handlers never touch `r-&gt;srv` -- they look at `r-&gt;fid-&gt;file` or `r-&gt;fid-&gt;aux` and call `respond(r, nil)`. They never issue nested 9P requests. The `r-&gt;srv` pointer sits there unused.
> 
> The key observation: **`Req` is a fat context object, and nobody complained.** The unused fields cost nothing at runtime -- they're pointer-sized members of a struct the library allocates anyway. The consistency of the signature mattered more than the ergonomic weight of an unused field.
> 
> Compare this with the kernel-level device driver table, which is even more uniform. Every `Dev` has the same set of function pointers (`read`, `write`, `walk`, etc.), and most devices provide `devcreate`, `devremove` (no-op defaults from `reference/plan9/src/sys/src/9/port/devpipe.c:415-416`). There was no lighter-weight "read-only device driver" variant.
> 
> The one place Plan 9 *did* provide two interface levels was **walk vs. walk1/clone** (`reference/plan9/man/2/9p:46-47`, `srv.c:326-331`). If you provide `srv-&gt;walk1`, lib9p calls `walkandclone` for you instead of demanding you implement the full multi-element walk protocol. But this isn't context reduction -- it's *complexity* reduction. `walk1` still receives `Fid*` plus `void *arg` (which is `Srv*` in the oldwalk1 wrapper at `srv.c:274-286`). The context is always available; the question is how much protocol machinery you need to handle yourself.
> 
> **Takeaway for pane:** Plan 9 strongly favored uniform signatures. The unused `_ctx` parameter in a handler that never sends requests is analogous to `r-&gt;srv` in a handler that never issues nested operations. The cost is aesthetic, not operational.
> 
> ## Question 2: Could ctx live on ServiceHandle instead of the trait signature?
> 
> This is the interesting design question. You're asking: can the *outbound* path (`ServiceHandle::send_request`) pull the dispatch context from somewhere the handler stores it, rather than `receive()` passing it in?
> 
> The problem is circular: the handler needs the ctx to call `send_request`, but `send_request` lives on `ServiceHandle`, which doesn't have access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. You'd need to either:
> 
> **(a) Store it on &amp;mut self.** The handler holds a `&amp;mut DispatchCtx` that the looper injects before calling `receive()` and removes after. This is the `Fid-&gt;aux` pattern -- state carried on the handle, not the call signature. But in Rust, you can't inject a mutable borrow into a struct that `receive` already borrows mutably. The lifetime conflict is fundamental, not accidental.
> 
> **(b) Put it on Messenger.** But Messenger is `Clone`, deliberately. Making it carry `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` would make it non-Clone and parameterize it on H, which defeats its purpose as a lightweight, passable self-reference.
> 
> **(c) Thread-local / Cell.** The looper sets a thread-local `RefCell&lt;&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;&gt;` before dispatch, `send_request` borrows it. This actually works -- pane is single-threaded per looper (C6). But thread-locals with lifetimes that change per dispatch call are fragile and hard to reason about. And it's invisible at the type level, which undermines the session-type discipline.
> 
> Plan 9 never had this problem because C doesn't have lifetimes. In `acme/fsys.c`, the handler functions (`fsysread`, `fsyswrite` -- see `reference/plan9/src/sys/src/cmd/acme/fsys.c:27-39`) all receive `(Xfid*, Fid*)`, and when they need to reach back into the global state, they use global variables (`row`, `seq`, etc. in `acme/dat.h`). That's the C "everything is global or on the stack" approach. Rust's ownership model makes the same trick expensive at the type level.
> 
> **Takeaway:** None of these alternatives are cheaper than `_ctx`. Option (a) fights the borrow checker. Option (b) contaminates Messenger. Option (c) works but hides the dependency in a thread-local, which is the kind of action-at-a-distance that Plan 9 specifically avoided by making contexts explicit in function signatures.
> 
> ## Question 3: Did Plan 9 have handler functions with two variants (with/without context)?
> 
> **One case, and it's instructive.** The walk/walk1 split mentioned above. From `srv.c:299-332`:
> 
> ```c
> if(r-&gt;fid-&gt;file){
>     filewalk(r);           // uses File tree — no user callback
> } else if(srv-&gt;walk1)
>     walkandclone(r, oldwalk1, oldclone, srv);  // simplified interface
> else if(srv-&gt;walk)
>     srv-&gt;walk(r);          // full interface
> else
>     sysfatal("no walk function, no file trees");
> ```
> 
> This is a *three*-tier dispatch: automatic (file tree), simplified (walk1/clone), or full (walk). But notice: it's **not** two variants of the same trait. It's an if/else chain on which function pointers are non-nil. In Rust terms, this is more like "provide a default impl that most handlers override" than "two separate traits."
> 
> No other 9P operation had this split. There was no `read1` (simplified read). There was no "stat without the ability to wstat." The walk split existed because multi-element walk is genuinely difficult to implement correctly (error-on-partial-walk semantics, clone-on-newfid, etc.), not because some walk implementations needed more context than others.
> 
> ## Recommendation
> 
> Keep the `_ctx` parameter on `receive()`. Here's why this is the right call, and what to do about the ergonomic sting:
> 
> **1. The `_ctx` is cheap.** It's a reference. In Plan 9 terms, it's `r-&gt;srv` -- always there, used by those who need it, ignored by those who don't. A handler that receives `_ctx` and ignores it is like `sclunk` receiving `Srv *srv` and ignoring it -- nobody ever proposed a second signature for clunk.
> 
> **2. The macro erases the pain.** The `#[pane::protocol_handler(P)]` attribute macro generates the `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` impl. It can suppress the `_ctx` parameter from the user-visible method signatures when none of the match arms reference it. The user writes:
> 
> ```rust
> #[pane::protocol_handler(Display)]
> impl MyHandler {
>     fn frame_resized(&amp;mut self, new_rect: Rect) -&gt; Flow {
>         // no ctx needed, no ctx visible
>         Flow::Continue
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The macro generates:
> 
> ```rust
> impl Handles&lt;Display&gt; for MyHandler {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: DisplayMessage, _ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg {
>             DisplayMessage::FrameResized(new_rect) =&gt; self.frame_resized(new_rect),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Methods that *do* need to issue requests declare it:
> 
> ```rust
> fn handle_query(&amp;mut self, q: Query, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx) -&gt; Flow {
>     self.clipboard.send_request(GetContents, ctx, |h, m, reply| { ... });
>     Flow::Continue
> }
> ```
> 
> The macro sees `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx` in the method signature and threads it through. Methods without it get the underscore-prefixed discard.
> 
> **3. The blanket `Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt;` on Handler stays clean.** The blanket impl already does:
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: LifecycleMessage) -&gt; Flow {
>         match msg { ... }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> If `receive` gains `_ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx`, the blanket impl simply doesn't pass it to the Handler methods. Lifecycle methods (`ready`, `close_requested`, `pulse`, `disconnected`) don't send requests -- they're the "sclunk/swstat" of pane. The ctx goes to `_`.
> 
> **4. Confidence level: high.** This is the same decision Plan 9 made, for the same reasons, and it held up across every server written against lib9p for fifteen years. The uniform signature is the right choice. The macro is where you absorb the ergonomic cost.
> 
> **What you pay:** One unused parameter in generated code. Approximately zero runtime cost. The trait signature is slightly wider than necessary for pure-notification protocols. The macro makes this invisible to users.
> 
> **What you gain:** Soundness (no thread-local tricks, no hidden state). Every handler *can* issue nested requests without changing its signature. Adding request capability to a handler that didn't have it is a method-level change, not a trait-level change.
> 
> **The simpler alternative that's worse:** Two traits, `Handles&lt;P&gt;` (no ctx) and `HandlesWithCtx&lt;P&gt;` (with ctx). This is the walk/walk1 split, but unlike walk, where the complexity difference between full-walk and walk1 is substantial, here the difference is one parameter. Not worth a second trait. And it would mean `ServiceDispatch` needs to know which trait each protocol handler implements -- an if/else chain at the type level.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a440741273b1d3026</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01BUNrN3qJVnYYBLtoecxmQW</tool-use-id>

> <summary>Agent "Refine: ctx ergonomics on Handles" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. Now I have the full picture. Let me formulate the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> The question maps precisely to a known structure in the profunctor optics literature. Here is the categorical reading, then the Rust translation, then three concrete options.
> 
> ## The categorical setup
> 
> The handler dispatch has the shape of a **copattern** (a negative eliminator): the handler is a product of method implementations, one per protocol. Each `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` is a morphism in a copresheaf category -- it's one component of the handler's "interface." The proposed `ctx: &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps` parameter would extend every component with access to a shared context.
> 
> In profunctor terms, this is exactly the **Cartesian (Strong) strength** applied uniformly. A Cartesian profunctor satisfies:
> 
> ```
> first :: p a b -&gt; p (a, c) (b, c)
> ```
> 
> The `c` wire -- the "extra context" -- threads alongside the focus without the focus necessarily using it. From the reference material (Profunctors.md, lines 123-183), the Cartesian typeclass's defining property is that `c` **passes through untouched** unless the profunctor actively consumes it.
> 
> Your `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` is the inner `p a b`. Adding `ctx` to every handler signature is `first` -- lifting every handler into `p (a, ctx) (b, ctx)`. The issue you've identified is real: **you're requiring every profunctor in the system to be Cartesian even when some are plain Profunctors (adapters)**. A notification-only handler that ignores `ctx` is a plain profunctor being forced through `first` -- the `c` wire exists but is dead.
> 
> The deeper question is: should the Cartesian strength be **intrinsic** to the handler (every handler carries the wire) or **extrinsic** (applied only at the site that needs it)?
> 
> ## The three options, typed concretely
> 
> ### Option A: Cartesian everywhere (ctx on receive)
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchOps, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> This is `first` applied uniformly. Every handler is `p (msg, ctx) (flow, ctx)`.
> 
> **Categorical assessment:** Correct but overly constrained. It's like defining all optics as `forall p. (Cartesian p, Cocartesian p, Monoidal p) =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t` -- you get traversals everywhere, even when you only need an adapter. The extra constraints don't break anything (every adapter *is* trivially Cartesian via the identity wire), but they pollute the interface. The dead `ctx` parameter on notification handlers is the Rust manifestation of a unit wire: `first h` where the `c` channel carries `()` semantically even though it carries `&amp;mut DispatchOps` physically.
> 
> **Ergonomic cost:** Every `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impl gains a parameter it ignores. Every test needs to construct or mock a `DispatchOps`. The trait stops being expressible as a simple message-to-flow function.
> 
> ### Option B: Cartesian on ServiceHandle (ctx on send_request)
> 
> Move `DispatchOps` to the call site where it's needed:
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;P: Protocol&gt; ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; {
>     pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchOps,  // &lt;-- here, not on receive
>         msg: P::Message,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>         R: pane_proto::Message,
>     { ... }
> }
> ```
> 
> But `ServiceHandle::send_request` is called *inside* the handler. So the handler needs `ctx` to pass to `send_request`. You haven't eliminated the wire -- you've just moved where it plugs in. The handler is still `p (msg, ctx) (flow, ctx)`, but now the `ctx` comes from... where?
> 
> Currently, `self` (the handler struct) holds `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` as a field. If `send_request` needs `ctx`, the handler needs it too. You'd need either:
> 1. `ctx` on `receive` anyway (circular -- back to Option A), or
> 2. `ctx` stored on the handler struct at setup time
> 
> Option 2 is interesting -- it means the handler captures `ctx` as part of its construction, not as a parameter at each dispatch. But `&amp;mut DispatchOps` as a stored field is problematic: the handler outlives any single dispatch call, and `&amp;mut` borrows don't store. You'd need to store an `Rc&lt;RefCell&lt;DispatchOps&gt;&gt;` or similar, which trades a clean parameter for interior mutability.
> 
> **Categorical assessment:** This is trying to make the Cartesian strength a **closed-over capability** rather than a **parametric one**. In profunctor terms, it's the difference between `first :: p a b -&gt; p (a,c) (b,c)` and the **closed monoidal** version where `c` is in the environment of `p` itself (a reader monad). Categorically valid, but the Rust encoding via `Rc&lt;RefCell&lt;_&gt;&gt;` is an approximation that loses the borrowing discipline you currently have.
> 
> ### Option C: Capability witness (phantom proof token)
> 
> The handler trait stays clean:
> 
> ```rust
> // Unchanged
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> `send_request` requires a proof token:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Zero-sized proof that we are inside a dispatch context.
> /// Cannot be constructed outside the looper.
> pub struct InDispatch {
>     _private: (),  // private field prevents external construction
> }
> 
> impl&lt;P: Protocol&gt; ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; {
>     pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         witness: &amp;InDispatch,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
>         R: pane_proto::Message,
>     { ... }
> }
> ```
> 
> But the question is: how does the handler get `InDispatch`? Three sub-options:
> 
> **C1. On receive after all (defeats the purpose):**
> ```rust
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, token: &amp;InDispatch) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> This is Option A with a ZST instead of `&amp;mut DispatchOps`. Slightly cleaner (no mock needed in tests -- you can have `InDispatch::for_test()`), but the parameter is still there.
> 
> **C2. On the handler struct at setup time:**
> ```rust
> pub struct MyHandler {
>     witness: InDispatch,
>     echo_service: ServiceHandle&lt;EchoProto&gt;,
> }
> ```
> The looper constructs `InDispatch` and hands it to the handler during `PaneBuilder::run_with`. The handler stores it. `send_request` takes `&amp;self.witness`.
> 
> **Categorical assessment:** This is a **Tambara module structure on the handler itself**, not on each dispatch invocation. The proof of being in a dispatch context is part of the handler's *identity*, not a per-message capability. This is categorically well-typed: the handler *is* a dispatch-context-aware entity; it doesn't *receive* the context per message, it *has* the context by construction.
> 
> The risk: `InDispatch` stored on a struct is no longer temporally scoped. It proves "this handler was constructed by a looper" but not "this handler is currently being dispatched." If handlers are ever invoked outside the looper (tests, migrations, etc.), the witness is stale. That said, in pane's architecture, handlers are *only* invoked by the looper, so this is more a formal than practical concern.
> 
> **C3. Via Messenger (the existing threading mechanism):**
> ```rust
> impl Messenger {
>     fn dispatch_token(&amp;self) -&gt; &amp;InDispatch { ... }
> }
> ```
> If `Messenger` is already threaded through the framework (it's on `ServiceReceiver`, `RequestReceiver`, and `DispatchEntry` callbacks), then attaching the proof token to `Messenger` means no new parameter on `Handles&lt;P&gt;`. The handler uses `self.messenger.dispatch_token()` when calling `send_request`.
> 
> But -- looking at the code -- `Messenger` is *not* on `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive`. It's on the internal closures (`ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;`, `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;`, `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;`), but the trait method itself only takes `msg`. So this sub-option requires one of:
> - Adding `&amp;Messenger` to `receive` (a different version of Option A's ergonomic cost), or
> - Having the handler store its own `Messenger` clone (which it already does implicitly via `PaneBuilder::run_with`)
> 
> ## Assessment
> 
> The fundamental tension is between two real constraints:
> 
> 1. **Handles&lt;P&gt; should be simple.** `fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow` is a beautiful, minimal interface. Adding parameters erodes this.
> 
> 2. **send_request needs dispatch-context capabilities** (installing entries in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`). These capabilities live on the looper, not the handler.
> 
> Currently, `send_request` doesn't actually use `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` -- the TODO at line 109 of `service_handle.rs` says "install Dispatch entry keyed by token so replies route back. For now, stub callbacks are not installed." When this gets wired, `send_request` will need to register a `DispatchEntry` in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which lives on `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`.
> 
> This reframes the question: `send_request` doesn't need "a dispatch context" in the abstract -- it needs a specific capability: **the ability to install a DispatchEntry**. Currently the `send_request` signature already captures `on_reply` and `on_failed` as closures. What it's missing is the channel to install them.
> 
> **The clean path that avoids contaminating Handles&lt;P&gt;:** Wire the dispatch-entry installation channel into `ServiceHandle` at construction time, the same way `write_tx` is already wired in. `ServiceHandle` already captures its write channel at `PaneBuilder::open_service` time. Add a second channel (or closure) that installs `DispatchEntry` items. Then `send_request` can install entries without any runtime context parameter at all.
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct ServiceHandle&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     session_id: u16,
>     write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;,
>     // New: channel to install dispatch entries
>     dispatch_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;DispatchEntry&lt;Box&lt;dyn Any&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;,
>     _protocol: PhantomData&lt;P&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> **Categorical reading:** This makes `ServiceHandle` itself the Tambara module -- it carries the monoidal action (dispatch-entry installation) as part of its structure, not as a parameter threaded through the handler. The handler stays a plain profunctor (no Cartesian requirement). The capability is **closed over** in the handle at construction time, which is exactly the right move when the capability is invariant across the handle's lifetime (it always installs entries in the same `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`).
> 
> This avoids all three problems: no parameter on `receive`, no phantom token ceremony, no `Rc&lt;RefCell&lt;_&gt;&gt;` interior mutability. The trade-off is that `ServiceHandle` becomes slightly heavier (one more channel clone), and `DispatchEntry` installation becomes asynchronous (the entry is sent via channel to the looper, which installs it). But the looper already processes messages sequentially, so the entry will be installed before the next dispatch cycle -- the ordering is guaranteed by the single-threaded looper.
> 
> I'd flag the **closed-over dispatch channel on ServiceHandle** as the strongest option because it preserves `Handles&lt;P&gt;` as-is, requires no new trait parameters, and matches the existing pattern (write_tx is already captured the same way). Options A and C1 both pollute the handler trait. Option B needs interior mutability. Options C2 and C3 are workable but add ceremony for a capability that has a natural home in the handle itself.</result>

### `2026-04-06T23:08:30.343Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aeef4efde12dad237</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01AEDVSF93AAYYayC1buZw4e</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/aeef4efde12dad237.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Refine: ctx ergonomics on Handles" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have the complete picture. Let me formulate my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Good question. This is exactly the kind of ergonomic detail that determines whether an API is pleasant or tolerable. Let me work through it concretely.
> 
> ## The Be Precedent: Ambient Context via `Looper()`
> 
> In BeOS, `BHandler::MessageReceived(BMessage*)` took only the message — no context parameter. When a handler needed to send messages, interact with the looper, or access other handlers, it reached for `this-&gt;Looper()` — a stored pointer to its parent looper, set when the handler was added via `BLooper::AddHandler()`:
> 
> ```cpp
> // From Haiku headers/os/app/Handler.h, line 41
> virtual void MessageReceived(BMessage* message);
> BLooper* Looper() const;  // line 42 — stored as fLooper
> 
> // From Haiku src/kits/app/Handler.cpp, line 287
> BLooper* BHandler::Looper() const { return fLooper; }
> ```
> 
> This was ambient context — the handler always had access to its looper because it was a field, not a parameter. The result was that `MessageReceived` had the lightest possible signature. A handler that just processed data and mutated its own state never had to acknowledge the looper's existence. A handler that needed to send messages could call `Looper()-&gt;PostMessage(...)`.
> 
> The downside was that `fLooper` was a raw pointer that could be null (handler not yet attached), dangling (handler outlives looper), or create lock-ordering problems (you needed `LockLooper()` before touching anything). We've already eliminated all three problems through ownership and borrowing. The question is whether we can preserve the ergonomic benefit.
> 
> ## Analyzing Your Four Options
> 
> Let me be precise about what "ctx" needs to do. Looking at the current code, the reason for `DispatchOps` is Task 5 — `send_request` needs to install a `DispatchEntry` in the looper's `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. Currently, `send_request` lives on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` (line 81 of `service_handle.rs`). It needs access to the `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` table to register the reply callback. That's the capability that `DispatchOps` would provide.
> 
> **Option 1: ctx with a default.** Rust doesn't have default parameters, and there's no trait-level trick that avoids the caller seeing the parameter. You could provide a no-op `DispatchOps` implementor (a `NullDispatch` struct), but the handler still has to write `_ctx: &amp;mut dyn DispatchOps` in the signature. Dead parameter — you just gave it a harmless value. Doesn't solve the problem.
> 
> **Option 2: Separate trait `HandlesWithDispatch&lt;P&gt;: Handles&lt;P&gt;`.** This is more interesting but has a fatal problem with specialization. The framework would need to call `HandlesWithDispatch&lt;P&gt;::receive_with_ctx` when `H: HandlesWithDispatch&lt;P&gt;`, and fall back to `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` otherwise. That's specialization, which is unstable. You could work around it with a sealed helper trait and blanket impls, but the gymnastics would be worse than the `_ctx` parameter. And there's a deeper problem: it splits the dispatch surface for the same protocol based on whether the handler sends requests — an impl detail leaking into the trait hierarchy.
> 
> **Option 3: `ctx: Option&lt;&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps&gt;`.** You correctly noted this weakens the guarantee. A handler that calls `ctx.unwrap()` on a `None` panics at runtime. We didn't build this system to panic at runtime. Rejected.
> 
> **Option 4: Something else.**
> 
> Yes. And it's exactly what Be did, translated through Rust's type system.
> 
> ## The Right Answer: Messenger Carries the Dispatch Capability
> 
> Look at what already flows through every dispatch path. In `service_dispatch.rs`, `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` is:
> 
> ```rust
> pub type ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;;
> ```
> 
> Every handler dispatch already receives `&amp;Messenger`. But look at `Messenger` today (in `messenger.rs`) — it's a stub. It has `self_address`, `set_content`, `set_pulse_rate`. It's the pane's "what I am" handle.
> 
> The Be analogy: `BHandler::Looper()` returned your looper. From your looper, you could `PostMessage()`, access other handlers, everything. It was ambient. The handler didn't receive it as a parameter to `MessageReceived` — it was available as a method.
> 
> The pane translation: **the handler stores its Messenger as a field, set during construction**. The handler already has `&amp;mut self`, so it can access `self.messenger`. No parameter needed on `receive()`.
> 
> But `Messenger` today is `Clone` and has no `&amp;mut` surface for dispatch registration. That's the issue. `send_request` needs to mutate `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which is owned by `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`. The Messenger can't hold `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` because it's stored in the handler, which is inside `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` — that would be a self-referential borrow.
> 
> So where does dispatch entry installation actually happen? Let me trace the data flow.
> 
> ## The Solution: Dispatch Registration Through ServiceHandle, Not Messenger
> 
> Look at `send_request` on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` (line 81-113 of `service_handle.rs`). It already takes `on_reply` and `on_failed` closures. The TODO at line 109 says "install Dispatch entry keyed by token so replies route back."
> 
> The question is: who has the `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` to install the entry?
> 
> The answer is: **the closure inside ServiceReceiver/RequestReceiver does**. Look at `dispatch_service` in `looper_core.rs` (line 254). The looper already has `&amp;mut self`, which includes `&amp;mut self.dispatch`. The framework has full access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` at dispatch time. The handler doesn't need it.
> 
> Here's the key insight: **`send_request` doesn't need to install the dispatch entry at call time**. It needs to *return a token and queue the entry for installation*. The installation happens when control returns to the framework.
> 
> Concretely, `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` already has a `write_tx: SyncSender`. It can *also* carry a `SyncSender` for dispatch entries — a registration channel. `send_request` serializes the message, sends it on the wire channel, and sends the `DispatchEntry` on the registration channel. The framework drains the registration channel between dispatches (or as part of the current dispatch cycle, since `LooperCore` owns both channels).
> 
> This means:
> 
> 1. `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow` — **unchanged**. No ctx parameter.
> 2. `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;) -&gt; Flow` — **unchanged**. No ctx parameter.
> 3. `Handler` blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` — **unchanged**. No `_ctx`.
> 4. `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;::send_request` installs entries through a channel, not through a context parameter.
> 
> The handler accesses `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` through its own fields (like it would have accessed `Looper()` in BeOS). The `ServiceHandle` was stored in the handler struct at construction. The handler calls `self.my_service.send_request(...)`. The ServiceHandle sends the wire message AND the dispatch entry through their respective channels. The framework installs the entry.
> 
> ## The Mechanics
> 
> ```rust
> // In ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; — add a dispatch registration channel
> pub struct ServiceHandle&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     session_id: u16,
>     write_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;&gt;,
>     // New: channel to send dispatch entries to the looper
>     dispatch_tx: Option&lt;SyncSender&lt;BoxedDispatchEntry&gt;&gt;,
>     _protocol: PhantomData&lt;P&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> Where `BoxedDispatchEntry` is a type-erased dispatch entry that the looper can install:
> 
> ```rust
> // Type-erased dispatch entry for the registration channel
> pub(crate) type BoxedDispatchEntry = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut dyn std::any::Any) + Send&gt;;
> ```
> 
> Wait — that loses the `H` type parameter. Let me think about this more carefully.
> 
> `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` is parameterized on `H`. `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is parameterized on `P`, not `H`. Currently `send_request` takes `H` as a type parameter on the method. So the entry is typed, but the channel needs to be `H`-erased since `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` doesn't know `H` at construction time.
> 
> Actually, looking more carefully at the current `send_request`:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>     &amp;self,
>     msg: P::Message,
>     on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
> ) -&gt; CancelHandle
> where
>     H: Handler + Handles&lt;P&gt; + 'static,
> ```
> 
> `H` is a type parameter on the *method*, not the struct. That's the current design's way of getting `H` in scope. But `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` (the original Path 1 approach) was supposed to bind `H = Self` — meaning `H` is the concrete handler type. The soundness guarantee is that the closures match the actual handler they'll be called on.
> 
> With a channel approach, the channel type has to know `H` to carry `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;`. But `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` is constructed by `PaneBuilder` where `H` IS known. So:
> 
> ```rust
> // ServiceHandle knows H at construction time, not as a struct parameter,
> // but the dispatch_tx can be typed internally
> pub(crate) fn with_channel_and_dispatch(
>     session_id: u16,
>     write_tx: SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;,
>     dispatch_tx: SyncSender&lt;PendingEntry&lt;H&gt;&gt;,  // H known at construction
> ) -&gt; Self { ... }
> ```
> 
> But then `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` would need to be `ServiceHandle&lt;P, H&gt;` or store the channel type-erased. Adding `H` to `ServiceHandle` is a type parameter infection that reaches into the handler's fields. Ugly.
> 
> Let me reconsider. There's a simpler formulation.
> 
> ## Simpler: The Framework Wraps send_request
> 
> The handler doesn't call `ServiceHandle::send_request` directly. Instead, the handler calls a method that routes through the Messenger. `Messenger` is constructed by the looper with a dispatch registration capability baked in. The `H` type is erased behind a channel at construction time (the looper knows `H`).
> 
> But actually, stepping back — the accepted design says `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`. That binds `H = Self` through `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive`. Your concern was that every `receive` gets `_ctx`. My counter-proposal is: don't put ctx on receive at all.
> 
> **The `DispatchCtx` capability goes on `Messenger`, not on the trait method.**
> 
> The looper constructs the `Messenger` with a `dispatch_tx` channel. The handler stores the `Messenger` as a field (set during `ready()` or at construction). When the handler needs to send a request, it calls:
> 
> ```rust
> self.messenger.send_request(&amp;self.my_service, msg, on_reply, on_failed)
> ```
> 
> or equivalently:
> 
> ```rust
> self.my_service.send_request(&amp;self.messenger, msg, on_reply, on_failed)
> ```
> 
> Either way, the `Messenger` carries the dispatch capability. The `Handles&lt;P&gt;` trait stays clean. The `H = Self` binding is enforced because `Messenger` is constructed by the framework with the concrete `H` baked into its dispatch channel — the framework knows `H`, erases it behind a channel, and the channel only accepts entries that match `H`.
> 
> ## The Soundness Argument
> 
> The `H = Self` guarantee still holds. Here's why:
> 
> 1. `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;` constructs the `Messenger` and the `ServiceHandle`s. `H` is known.
> 2. The dispatch channel inside `Messenger` is `SyncSender&lt;DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;&gt;` — typed to `H`.
> 3. The handler receives its `Messenger` during `ready()` or construction. It stores it as a field.
> 4. When the handler calls `send_request`, it passes closures `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)`. Since `self` is `&amp;mut H` in the receive method, the closures naturally capture `H = Self`.
> 5. The channel ensures only `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` can be sent — no type confusion possible.
> 
> The binding is at construction time, not at dispatch time. Same guarantee, different enforcement point.
> 
> ## What Changes vs. the Accepted Path 1(c)
> 
> - `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` stays `fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow`. No ctx.
> - `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` stays `fn receive_request(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;) -&gt; Flow`. No ctx.
> - `Handler` blanket impl unchanged. No `_ctx` noise.
> - `Messenger` gains dispatch capability (internal, channel-based).
> - `ServiceHandle::send_request` takes `&amp;Messenger` as a parameter instead of `&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps`.
> - `LooperCore` drains the dispatch registration channel as part of the dispatch cycle.
> 
> The ergonomic improvement is complete: handlers that never send requests never see any dispatch-related types. Handlers that do send requests use their stored `Messenger` — which they already have for `set_content`, `set_pulse_rate`, etc.
> 
> This is the Looper() pattern translated faithfully. BHandler had ambient Looper(). pane handlers have an ambient Messenger. Both provide framework capabilities without polluting the dispatch method signature.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>65906</total_tokens><tool_uses>27</tool_uses><duration_ms>161692</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-06T23:08:30.348Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a86e7d463a513de79</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01Um9QoAw1ZBF2neV3Y7eDYe</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a86e7d463a513de79.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Refine: ctx ergonomics on Handles" completed</summary>
> <result>I have all the context I need. Let me now construct the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Proposal 1 (split) is sound and is the right design. Proposal 3 (two-method fallback) is unsound.**
> 
> Here's the full analysis.
> 
> ## 1. The Split: `Handles&lt;P&gt;` clean, `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` gets ctx
> 
> **Sound.** This is the correct factoring, and here's why.
> 
> The H = Self soundness invariant requires that the concrete handler type `H` be known at the point where a `DispatchEntry&lt;H&gt;` is installed into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. Currently, `send_request` on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` takes `on_reply: FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...) -&gt; Flow` -- the `H` type parameter binds the entry to the handler.
> 
> The question is: where does the handler get access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` to install that entry?
> 
> Right now `send_request` lives on `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` with a TODO for installing the dispatch entry. Task 5's accepted design says: pass `&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps` (or a concrete `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`) into the handler method, so the handler can call `service_handle.send_request(msg, ctx, on_reply, on_failed)` where `ctx` provides the `insert` method.
> 
> **Key observation**: `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` takes only `P::Message`. A notification handler processes a value. It doesn't produce an obligation handle, doesn't need to correlate a future reply. The `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` machinery is entirely request/reply infrastructure.
> 
> `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` already takes `ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;` -- an obligation handle. It's already in the "interacts with framework infrastructure" category. Adding a dispatch context there is a natural extension of the same principle: request handlers participate in the dispatch lifecycle, notification handlers don't.
> 
> The trait split becomes:
> 
> ```rust
> // Notification: pure value processing. No framework interaction needed.
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> 
> // Request: framework interaction. Gets ReplyPort (obligation) + DispatchCtx (outgoing requests).
> pub trait HandlesRequest&lt;P: RequestProtocol&gt; {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx,
>     ) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> The blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` stays clean. Handler's lifecycle methods (`ready`, `close_requested`, etc.) stay clean. No ergonomic cost for the common case.
> 
> ## 2. The Notification-Sends-Request Pattern
> 
> This pattern exists but it is *not* the common case, and it should not drive the trait design.
> 
> Consider the "clipboard changed" -&gt; "get clipboard content" example. The notification is arriving on the clipboard protocol's `Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt;`. The outbound request goes through a `ServiceHandle&lt;Clipboard&gt;` (or potentially a different service). The handler needs `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` access to install the reply callback.
> 
> But here's the thing: **the handler already holds the `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` as a field on `self`**. The only missing piece is the dispatch context to install the entry. There are two clean ways to provide this without polluting `Handles&lt;P&gt;`:
> 
> **(a) Messenger carries a dispatch context during dispatch.** The looper already passes `&amp;Messenger` to the closures in `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` and `RequestReceiver&lt;H&gt;`. Currently `Messenger` is `Clone` and stateless beyond address. If the looper temporarily loans a `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` into a scoped dispatch context that `Messenger` provides access to, the handler can issue outbound requests from anywhere -- notification handlers, request handlers, lifecycle methods.
> 
> But this has a problem: `Messenger` is `Clone` and `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is `&amp;mut`. You'd need interior mutability or a scoped-borrow pattern, which is ugly and error-prone.
> 
> **(b) The handler stores a `DispatchHandle` (obtained during setup) and uses it.** A `DispatchHandle` is a sender into the looper's dispatch registration queue. But this breaks the single-threaded invariant -- `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is not thread-safe, and all manipulation must happen on the looper thread.
> 
> **(c) The actual right answer: `send_request` doesn't need dispatch access in the handler.** Look at the current `ServiceHandle::send_request` signature -- it takes `on_reply` and `on_failed` closures. When this gets wired, the installation path should be:
> 
> 1. Handler calls `service_handle.send_request(msg, on_reply, on_failed)` -- returns a `CancelHandle`
> 2. `send_request` needs to insert into `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` -- but it doesn't have access to it
> 3. So `send_request` takes a `&amp;mut DispatchCtx` parameter
> 
> This means ANY handler method that wants to send a request needs DispatchCtx. The question is whether to pass it through the trait signature or through some other channel.
> 
> **The frequency analysis**: In BeOS, `BHandler::MessageReceived` handled all messages. Handlers routinely sent replies and forwarded messages to other handlers in response to notifications. In pane's split model, though, the *most common* notification-handler action is state mutation + redraw -- "I got a clipboard-changed notification, update my display" or "I got a settings-changed notification, update my cache." These don't send requests.
> 
> The notification-triggers-request pattern is real but secondary. When a handler *does* need it, the handler can hold a `DispatchWriter` (a newtype around a channel sender that queues dispatch entry registrations to be processed by the looper on the next tick). This is an explicit opt-in stored as handler state, not a trait method argument.
> 
> But wait -- that deferred registration breaks the H = Self invariant if the channel carries type-erased entries without the H binding. Let me examine this more carefully.
> 
> ## 3. The Two-Method Fallback: Unsound
> 
> The sketch would be:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> 
>     fn receive_with_dispatch(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx,
>     ) -&gt; Flow {
>         // Default: ignore ctx, call receive
>         self.receive(msg)
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The framework calls `receive_with_dispatch` always, passing ctx. Handlers that don't need ctx just implement `receive` and get the default. Handlers that need ctx override `receive_with_dispatch`.
> 
> **This is unsound for the H = Self invariant.** Here's why:
> 
> The `make_service_receiver` closure currently calls `handler.receive(msg)`. If we change it to call `handler.receive_with_dispatch(msg, ctx)`, we need to pass a `DispatchCtx` into the closure. The `DispatchCtx` carries `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` -- and `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is parameterized by `H`. So the closure must capture or receive something that carries the `H` parameter.
> 
> Currently `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;`. Adding ctx means either:
> 
> (a) The ctx is passed through: `Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, &amp;[u8], &amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;` -- this works, H is already bound.
> 
> (b) The ctx is a `&amp;mut dyn DispatchOps` trait object -- this requires `DispatchOps` to be object-safe, and the methods on `DispatchOps` that take `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)` closures cannot be object-safe because H is a type parameter, not `Self`.
> 
> Option (a) works, but then the *real* problem is: every `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` gets `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, even for notification-only handlers. This means the service dispatch closure now borrows both `&amp;mut H` (the handler) and `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (the dispatch table) simultaneously from `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`. Currently this works because `dispatch_notification` only borrows `handler` and `service_dispatch` from `LooperCore` -- they're separate fields. Adding `&amp;mut self.dispatch` to the same call means you're passing three `&amp;mut` borrows from `self`:
> 
> ```rust
> // This is what the looper currently does:
> let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
> let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;
> let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;
> service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(session_id, handler, messenger, &amp;inner)
> ```
> 
> Adding dispatch:
> ```rust
> let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;
> let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;
> let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;
> let dispatch = &amp;mut self.dispatch;
> service_dispatch.dispatch_notification(session_id, handler, messenger, &amp;inner, dispatch)
> ```
> 
> This compiles fine in Rust -- split borrows of different struct fields. But the ergonomic argument was to *not* pass dispatch to notification handlers. The two-method design still passes it to the closure and thus to every notification dispatch, just to support the override path. **The ergonomic saving is illusory**: the framework always constructs and passes the context; the trait default just ignores it.
> 
> Worse: the two-method design creates a subtle bug surface. If a handler implements both `receive` and `receive_with_dispatch`, which one wins? The default calls `self.receive(msg)`, so if someone overrides both, the `receive_with_dispatch` override doesn't call `receive` unless they remember to. This is the classic "template method pattern" footgun.
> 
> **Verdict on two-method: reject.** No soundness gain over the split, ergonomic saving is illusory, and it introduces a method-shadowing footgun.
> 
> ## 4. The Clean Design
> 
> The right answer is the split you started with, with one refinement.
> 
> ### Notification handlers: no ctx
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;P: Protocol&gt; {
>     fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Request handlers: ctx included
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait HandlesRequest&lt;P: RequestProtocol&gt; {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx,
>     ) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Lifecycle: no ctx (blanket impl stays clean)
> 
> Handler's lifecycle methods stay as-is. The blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` never needs dispatch access because lifecycle events are framework-internal -- they don't trigger request/reply flows.
> 
> ### For notification-triggers-request: explicit handler state
> 
> When a handler needs to send a request from a notification handler, it holds a `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` and uses `send_request`. But `send_request` needs a dispatch context.
> 
> **Solution**: change `send_request` to take `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx` as a parameter. Notification handlers that need to send requests get their `DispatchCtx` from the looper, but *not through the trait signature*. Instead:
> 
> Option A: Add `ctx` to the `Messenger` (scoped, not cloneable). Messenger already goes to every handler invocation. But this conflicts with Messenger being Clone.
> 
> Option B: **Pass `DispatchCtx` through the `ServiceReceiver&lt;H&gt;` closure, but don't surface it in the `Handles&lt;P&gt;` trait.** The closure has the context; it calls `handler.receive(msg)` (no ctx); if the handler needs to send a request in response, it must defer. This is actually correct for the BLooper model: a notification handler that needs to trigger a request should post an internal message to itself. The looper processes it on the next tick, and the dispatch context is available at that point.
> 
> But that's a different mechanism (self-messaging / PostMessage). It's Phase 2+ at least.
> 
> **For Phase 1, the practical answer is**: notification handlers that need to send requests are uncommon enough that they can use `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` if the protocol supports it, or wait for the self-messaging mechanism. The trait split is correct *now*, and the notification-triggers-request escape hatch is a Phase 2 feature.
> 
> ### H = Self invariant preservation
> 
> The invariant holds for all paths:
> 
> 1. **`make_service_receiver&lt;H, P&gt;()`**: `H: Handles&lt;P&gt;` bound at call site (PaneBuilder). The closure captures H through the type parameter. No dispatch interaction -- no H binding needed at runtime.
> 
> 2. **`make_request_receiver&lt;H, P&gt;(write_tx, session_id)`**: `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` bound at call site. If `DispatchCtx` is added, it arrives at dispatch time from `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`, which owns `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. The `H` in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` and the `H` in the closure are the *same* `H` -- unified at `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;` construction. No opportunity for type confusion.
> 
> 3. **`ServiceHandle::send_request`**: The current `H` parameter on `send_request` is specified by the caller. When the caller is inside a `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` and gets `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx`, the DispatchCtx was constructed from `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` by the looper, and `H = Self` because the looper is `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` and the handler is `&amp;mut H`. The type parameter flows: `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` -&gt; `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` -&gt; `DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;` -&gt; `send_request&lt;H&gt;`. At no point can H diverge.
> 
> 4. **Blanket `Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt;`**: No dispatch involvement. Sound trivially.
> 
> ### What about `DispatchCtx` being generic?
> 
> `DispatchCtx` should be `DispatchCtx&lt;'_, H&gt;` (borrows `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`), not a trait object. This preserves the H binding statically. The `send_request` method on it takes `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...) -&gt; Flow` closures, which is why H must be a type parameter, not erased. Object safety is not achievable here without giving up the H = Self guarantee, so don't try.
> 
> But then `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` has a `DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;` in its signature, which means the trait is now parameterized by H:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait HandlesRequest&lt;P: RequestProtocol&gt; {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: P::Message,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;,  // Self IS H
>     ) -&gt; Flow;
> }
> ```
> 
> `Self` is the handler type. `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` binds H = Self at the trait level. This is the soundness guarantee. The impl writes:
> 
> ```rust
> impl HandlesRequest&lt;MyProto&gt; for MyHandler {
>     fn receive_request(
>         &amp;mut self,
>         msg: MyMsg,
>         reply: ReplyPort&lt;MyReply&gt;,
>         ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;,  // DispatchCtx&lt;MyHandler&gt;
>     ) -&gt; Flow {
>         // ctx.send_request takes FnOnce(&amp;mut MyHandler, ...) -- correct binding
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This is exactly the `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` design you accepted. And it appears *only* on `HandlesRequest`, not on `Handles`. Notification handlers never see it.
> 
> ### Summary Table
> 
> | Path | Gets DispatchCtx? | H = Self enforced by | Sound? |
> |------|-------------------|---------------------|--------|
> | `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` | No | N/A (no dispatch interaction) | Yes |
> | `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` | Yes, `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` | `Self` in trait signature | Yes |
> | Lifecycle (blanket) | No | N/A | Yes |
> | Notification-triggers-request | Deferred to Phase 2 | Self-messaging mechanism | N/A yet |
> 
> The ergonomic cost is zero for notification-only handlers and lifecycle. The only cost is the additional parameter on `HandlesRequest`, which already takes a `ReplyPort` -- handlers that deal with requests are already in "framework infrastructure" territory. Adding `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` is the same kind of parameter as `ReplyPort`: a framework-provided capability scoped to this dispatch.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>69887</total_tokens><tool_uses>23</tool_uses><duration_ms>148174</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: HandlesRequest move" completed</summary>
> <result>
> 
> 1. **Yes, it addresses the ergonomic concern.** The notification path (`Handles&lt;P&gt;`) stays clean and context-free, which is the right default. Request handlers get `DispatchCtx` because they genuinely need to reply and often need to send follow-up messages — that's a real asymmetry in the programming model, not an arbitrary split.
> 
> 2. **The trait move doesn't violate Be principles — it clarifies something Be left implicit.** In BeOS, `BHandler::MessageReceived` handled both notifications and requests in the same method. You dispatched on `message-&gt;what` and called `message-&gt;SendReply()` when appropriate. The reply capability was always *there* on the BMessage, whether you needed it or not. That was convenient but sloppy — it meant every handler signature promised reply capability even for fire-and-forget messages. Splitting `Handles&lt;P&gt;` (notification) from `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` (request) makes the protocol's *expectation* visible in the type. Be would have done this if we'd had the type system for it. The crate boundary follows from the capability boundary: notifications need no dispatch context, requests do. That's sound.
> 
> 3. **Accept.** One small thing to watch: make sure the pane-app re-export story is clean. If users import `Handles` from pane-proto and `HandlesRequest` from pane-app, that's two import paths for closely related traits. A `pane_app::prelude` that re-exports both (plus `Handler`, `Flow`, etc.) would keep the ergonomics tight. But that's polish, not architecture.</result>

### `2026-04-06T23:15:08.526Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a1bcadad0883254f6</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_014MVJKYBUn3uGYLxV4996n8</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a1bcadad0883254f6.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: HandlesRequest move" completed</summary>
> <result>
> 
> 1. **Yes, this is better than the macro approach.** The macro solution was workable but it was papering over a structural problem — the trait lived in the wrong place relative to the type that needed to appear in its signature. Moving `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` to pane-app puts the dispatch context next to the dispatch machinery, which is where it belongs. Notification handlers staying clean (no phantom ctx they'd ignore) is the right outcome. The macro can still exist for other ergonomic reasons, but it shouldn't be load-bearing for hiding a misplaced abstraction.
> 
> 2. **The split is natural and has direct precedent.** In Plan 9, the 9P message vocabulary — `Tmsg` and `Rmsg` structs, the fcall format — lived in `libc` (specifically `&lt;fcall.h&gt;`) where any program could reference them. But the dispatch infrastructure — the `Srv` struct, `srv(2)`'s handler registration, the `srvproc` threading model — lived in `lib9p`. A file server linked both; a client that just spoke raw 9P only needed `libc`. The vocabulary was protocol; the dispatch was application framework. Your `Handles&lt;P&gt;` in pane-proto / `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` in pane-app maps directly to this: pane-proto is `fcall.h`, pane-app is `lib9p`. Clean separation with the right thing in the right layer.
> 
> 3. **No concerns. Accept.**
> 
> One minor note: make sure the pane-proto documentation makes clear that `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is the notification trait and that request/reply dispatch lives in pane-app. The split is natural but only if someone reading pane-proto doesn't go looking for the request handler trait and conclude it's missing. A one-line doc comment pointing to `HandlesRequest` in pane-app handles this.</result>

### `2026-04-06T23:15:52.775Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ab7e9d240352cf9b1</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01B4WwXC4MRZ5dud3Ak2btLX</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/ab7e9d240352cf9b1.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: HandlesRequest move" completed</summary>
> <result>I have the complete picture. Here's my judgment.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Accept.** Sound, with one note on the notification-triggers-request question.
> 
> **1. H = Self guarantee: preserved.**
> 
> The proposed `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` in pane-app with `fn receive_request(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message, reply: ReplyPort&lt;P::Reply&gt;, ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;) -&gt; Flow` keeps `Self` as the concrete handler type in the trait signature. `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` wraps `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` where `H = Self` is enforced by Rust's trait resolution at impl site. No trait object erasure occurs. The `make_request_receiver` factory in pane-app already demonstrates this pattern: the `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` bound is captured in the closure at registration time (line 200-203 of `service_dispatch.rs`), and `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` is parameterized by the same `H`. Adding `&amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` to `receive_request` is strictly additive -- the `Self = H` identity is resolved the same way.
> 
> No soundness concern.
> 
> **2. Does pane-proto need to know about request handling?**
> 
> No. The protocol *vocabulary* crate defines Protocol, RequestProtocol, Message types, and ServiceFrame wire variants. These are the types that cross the IPC boundary and must be shared between both sides of a session. `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` is about *how the receiving actor dispatches* -- that's an implementation concern of the receiving pane, not a protocol concern.
> 
> The theoretical basis: in session type terms, the protocol specifies the *type* of the channel (what messages flow in what order). The handler is the *process* that consumes that channel. These are categorically distinct -- the type lives in the session calculus, the process lives in the process calculus. Ferrite (Chen/Balzer/Toninho, ECOOP 2022, Section 3) makes this separation explicit: protocol types are defined independently of processes, processes are typed *against* protocols. Moving `HandlesRequest` out of pane-proto enforces this separation at the crate boundary.
> 
> The existing `Handles&lt;P&gt;` remains in pane-proto. This is defensible: `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is a minimal interface (`fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg) -&gt; Flow`) that doesn't depend on any pane-app types. It's the universal signature for "something that processes messages of type P." `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` with `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` inherently depends on `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, which is pane-app infrastructure. The split aligns with the dependency graph.
> 
> One subtlety: `make_request_receiver` in `service_dispatch.rs` already uses `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` as a bound. Currently that bound resolves to a trait in pane-proto. After the move, the bound resolves to a trait in pane-app. Since `make_request_receiver` is already in pane-app, this changes nothing about the dependency graph -- it actually *removes* an outward dependency from pane-proto.
> 
> **3. Notification-triggers-request gap: deferring to Phase 2 remains acceptable.**
> 
> The concern was: a handler receives a notification via `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` and wants to send a request in response. Currently `receive` doesn't have access to `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (and therefore can't call `send_request`). Moving `HandlesRequest` to pane-app doesn't make this harder or easier to solve later. The solution path is the same either way: give `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` access to a context object (either by adding a `ctx` parameter to `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` or by making `Messenger` carry a `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` reference during dispatch).
> 
> Moving `HandlesRequest` to pane-app does *not* foreclose this path. If anything it makes the precedent cleaner: when the time comes, you can either (a) add a `DispatchCtx`-style parameter to `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` (which would move `Handles&lt;P&gt;` to pane-app too, or require a trait in pane-proto that pane-app extends), or (b) make `Messenger` carry `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` for the duration of handler dispatch (already in scope per Task 5 in the roadmap). Option (b) doesn't touch `Handles&lt;P&gt;` at all and keeps it in pane-proto.
> 
> The trait move does not create a ratchet that constrains Phase 2 options.
> 
> **4. Accept.**</result>
