### `2026-04-06T23:16:32.297Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af1182cfa347b3852</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017h4nBDGd2CYoq5mhCi4J8v</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/af1182cfa347b3852.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Roundtable: HandlesRequest move" completed</summary>
> <result>I have enough context now. Let me formulate the judgment.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Judgment
> 
> ### 1. Is the notification/request split categorically justified in the duploid framework?
> 
> Yes, and cleanly so.
> 
> Look at the polarity table (from `pane/polarity_classifications`):
> 
> - **`Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive(msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow`** — pure cross-polarity elimination. Positive message meets negative handler. The only wire is the message itself. This is a simple coproduct eliminator: session_id selects the protocol, the closure deserializes and dispatches. No residual state, no continuation obligation.
> 
> - **`HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request(msg, reply_port) -&gt; Flow`** — same cross-polarity elimination *plus* a linear obligation (`ReplyPort&lt;T&gt;`, which is `↑(continuation)` per your classification). The reply port is not a value — it is a one-shot negative resource that must be consumed. This makes the request copattern operate in a *different monoidal category* than notification dispatch: notifications live in the Cartesian (product) fragment where duplication/discarding is free; requests live in the linear (tensor) fragment where `ReplyPort` must be consumed exactly once.
> 
> This is precisely the Cartesian/linear optic split that Clarke et al. identify (your R4 rule: "Value/obligation split maps exactly to cartesian/linear optic split"). Notifications are Cartesian copatterns. Requests are linear copatterns with Cartesian extension (the `DispatchCtx` carrying the reply wire). Putting them in different crates reflects that they belong to different Tambara module categories: the notification copattern requires only the `Strong` (Cartesian) constraint on the profunctor, while the request copattern requires a `Strong` constraint *plus* a linear resource threading that doesn't factor through `Strong` alone.
> 
> Crate-boundary-wise: pane-proto defines the Cartesian (duplicable, discardable) vocabulary. pane-app adds the linear resource management layer (obligation handles backed by runtime channels). This is the right factoring.
> 
> ### 2. ServiceHandle-as-Tambara-module vs. DispatchCtx as explicit Cartesian wire
> 
> I proposed ServiceHandle's `dispatch_tx` channel as a Tambara module structure because it provides the `α_{M,A,B} : P(A,B) → P(M ⊗ A, M ⊗ B)` strength map — threading the wire `M` (the write channel) alongside the profunctor's action. The idea was: a handler `P(Message, Flow)` gets strengthened to `P(M × Message, M × Flow)` where `M` carries the dispatch channel.
> 
> The DispatchCtx proposal does the same thing but *selectively* — only on the request copattern, and with the Cartesian wire made an explicit parameter rather than an implicit Tambara structure.
> 
> **The DispatchCtx approach is better.** Here's why:
> 
> A Tambara module structure on ServiceHandle would impose the wire threading *uniformly* — every profunctor in the optic hierarchy would need to carry the dispatch channel, even notification handlers that have no use for it. This is the "Cartesian everywhere" problem you flagged. Categorically, you'd be asking notification handlers to inhabit `Tamb(×, ×)` when they only need `Tamb(1, 1)` (the trivial action). That's an unnecessary strengthening of the constraint.
> 
> By making DispatchCtx explicit and restricted to `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` in pane-app, you get a *selective Cartesian extension*: the `α` map only fires where the linear obligation actually requires the wire. This is closer to the actual categorical situation — the Tambara structure lives on the request optic specifically, not on the entire handler profunctor.
> 
> Additionally, `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` being fully typed (not `dyn`) means the Tambara structure map is a concrete functor action, not an existentially-quantified one. This is strictly more information, and it composes better (you can derive properties of the concrete `DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;` that you can't derive from `dyn DispatchOps`).
> 
> ### 3. Removing the existential
> 
> Removing `dyn DispatchOps` and using `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` directly improves the categorical structure.
> 
> The existential (`dyn DispatchOps`) was `∃ X. DispatchOps(X)` — it hid the handler type `H` behind a trait object, giving you a coend `∫^H DispatchOps(H)`. But `DispatchOps` is not a profunctor in `H` — it's a concrete runtime type parameterized by `H`. The coend doesn't buy you anything structurally; it just throws away information that the looper already has (it knows `H` concretely since `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` is generic).
> 
> With `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`, the handler sees its own concrete dispatch context. This means:
> - The Tambara strength map `α : P(A,B) → P(DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt; × A, DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt; × B)` is a *concrete* natural transformation, not an existentially-quantified one.
> - No runtime dispatch overhead.
> - The handler can access typed capabilities of `DispatchCtx&lt;H&gt;` without downcasting.
> 
> The existential was a workaround for the crate boundary (pane-proto couldn't name `DispatchCtx` from pane-app). Moving `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;` to pane-app eliminates the boundary problem that motivated the existential. Removing it is the correct move — you don't need a coend when the quantifier variable is known.
> 
> ### 4. Verdict
> 
> **Accept.** The split is categorically well-motivated, eliminates a real structural problem (Cartesian constraints leaking into the notification layer), and the fully-typed DispatchCtx is strictly better than both the Tambara-on-ServiceHandle proposal and the existential encoding. The crate boundary now matches the Cartesian/linear partition.</result>

> This is the most significant finding. Now let me compile the full audit report.
>

> 
> ## Part 1: Implementation Audit
> 
> ### Property 1: H = Self soundness — PASS
> 
> The chain is sound:
> - `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;` holds `handler: H`, `dispatch: Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`, `service_dispatch: ServiceDispatch&lt;H&gt;`
> - `DispatchCtx&lt;'a, H&gt;` wraps `&amp;'a mut Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` + `PeerScope`
> - `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` takes `ctx: &amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`
> - `Self = H` is enforced by the trait: when `H: HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;`, `Self` IS `H`
> - No cast, no `*mut ()` erasure. The generic flows cleanly through the entire chain.
> 
> ### Property 2: Install-before-wire — PASS
> 
> In `service_handle.rs::send_request` (lines 91-121):
> 1. Line 93-102: `DispatchEntry` is built
> 2. Line 103: `let token = ctx.insert(entry)` — entry installed
> 3. Lines 106-117: token is serialized into `ServiceFrame::Request`
> 4. Lines 120-122: `tx.send(...)` — wire send
> 
> Install happens before wire send. The comments and code match. The invariant is satisfied.
> 
> ### Property 3: NEXT_TOKEN global deleted — PASS
> 
> Grep for `NEXT_TOKEN` in `service_handle.rs` returns no matches. The only `next_token` is a private field on `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` (`dispatch.rs:49`), incremented in `Dispatch::insert` (`dispatch.rs:69-70`). Tokens are per-`Dispatch` instance, not global.
> 
> ### Property 4: Every Request produces exactly one Reply or Failed — PASS (with caveat)
> 
> Traced all paths through `dispatch_service` Request branch:
> 
> | Path | Outcome |
> |------|---------|
> | Tag mismatch in `make_request_receiver` | `send_failed()` — Failed on wire |
> | Deserialization failure in `make_request_receiver` | `send_failed()` — Failed on wire |
> | No request receiver registered | `dispatch_request` fallback calls `send_failed()` — Failed on wire |
> | Handler panics | `catch_unwind` catches; `ReplyPort` dropped during unwind, Drop sends Failed via `wire_reply_port` |
> | Handler calls `reply.reply(value)` | Reply sent on wire |
> | Handler drops `ReplyPort` without replying | Drop sends Failed via `wire_reply_port` |
> 
> All paths produce exactly one Reply or Failed. The test `request_handler_panic_sends_failed_via_drop` verifies the panic path with `catch_unwind`.
> 
> ### Property 5: Reply/Failed routing wired — PASS (with BUG found)
> 
> **Reply branch** (`looper_core.rs:320-339`): Extracts `token` from `ServiceFrame::Reply`, wraps as `Token(token)`, calls `self.dispatch.fire_reply(conn, tok, handler, messenger, Box::new(reply_bytes))`.
> 
> **Failed branch** (`looper_core.rs:341-353`): Extracts `token` from `ServiceFrame::Failed`, wraps as `Token(token)`, calls `self.dispatch.fire_failed(conn, tok, handler, messenger)`.
> 
> Token extraction and routing to `fire_reply`/`fire_failed` are correct.
> 
> **BUG: Reply payload type mismatch.** `dispatch_service` passes `Box::new(reply_bytes)` where `reply_bytes: Vec&lt;u8&gt;`. The `on_reply` closure installed by `send_request` does `payload.downcast::&lt;R&gt;().expect(...)`. Since the `Any` contains `Vec&lt;u8&gt;` (not `R`), this will **panic at runtime** on every Reply arrival. The on_reply closure should either:
> - Receive `Vec&lt;u8&gt;` and deserialize to `R` inside the closure, or
> - The looper should pass `reply_bytes` through a deserialization step before calling `fire_reply`
> 
> Since the closure already captures the type `R` at `send_request` time, the cleanest fix is to change the closure to `downcast::&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;&gt;()` and then `postcard::from_bytes::&lt;R&gt;()`. This requires adding `R: DeserializeOwned` as a bound on `send_request`.
> 
> ### Property 6: Split borrow in LooperCore — PASS
> 
> The Request branch (`looper_core.rs:302-306`) binds:
> - `let handler = &amp;mut self.handler;`
> - `let service_dispatch = &amp;self.service_dispatch;`
> - `let messenger = &amp;self.messenger;`
> - `let write_tx = &amp;self.write_tx;`
> - `let mut ctx = DispatchCtx::new(&amp;mut self.dispatch, self.primary_connection);`
> 
> These are five distinct fields of `LooperCore&lt;H&gt;`. Rust allows simultaneous borrows of distinct struct fields. The workspace compiles clean (`cargo check` passes), confirming the split borrow is accepted by the borrow checker.
> 
> ### Property 7: HandlesRequest removed from pane-proto — PASS
> 
> - `crates/pane-proto/src/handles.rs`: Defines only `Handles&lt;P&gt;`. Contains doc comment: "For request/reply dispatch with `ReplyPort` obligation, see `pane_app::HandlesRequest`." No `HandlesRequest` definition.
> - `crates/pane-proto/src/lib.rs`: Re-exports `Handles` (line 35). Does not re-export `HandlesRequest`. The pub use list is clean.
> - `HandlesRequest` is defined solely in `crates/pane-app/src/handles_request.rs`.
> 
> ### Property 8: Panic safety — PASS
> 
> If the handler panics inside `receive_request` after calling `send_request` (which installed `DispatchEntry`s):
> 
> 1. `catch_unwind` catches the panic (`looper_core.rs:307-311`)
> 2. `run_destruction(ExitReason::Failed)` is called (`looper_core.rs:314-315`)
> 3. `self.dispatch.fail_connection(self.primary_connection, ...)` fires `on_failed` for all entries keyed to the primary connection — including any entries the handler installed via `ctx.insert()` during the panicking `receive_request` call
> 4. `self.dispatch.clear()` drops remaining entries
> 5. `shutdown()` drops the handler, firing Drop compensation for obligation handles
> 
> The entries installed by `send_request` use `(self.primary_connection, token)` as key (because the `DispatchCtx` was constructed with `self.primary_connection`), so `fail_connection` will find and clean them up.
>

> ## EAct Audit: pane Implementation vs. Fowler/Hu Operational Semantics
> 
> ### Methodology
> 
> I read the complete operational semantics (Figures 5-6 in the paper, lines 1962-2162 in the .tex), all three theorem statements (Preservation, Progress, Global Progress), the failure extension (Section 4), and the inter-session deadlock freedom argument (Section 3.4.2). I then cross-referenced every rule and theorem against the implementation files specified.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. E-Self Dependency Verdict: SAFELY DEFERRABLE
> 
> **There is no E-Self rule in EAct.** The paper has exactly these configuration reduction rules:
> 
> | Rule | Reference |
> |------|-----------|
> | E-Send | line 1967 |
> | E-React | line 1986 |
> | E-Suspend | line 2007 |
> | E-Spawn | line 2021 |
> | E-Reset | line 2043 |
> | E-NewAP | line 2058 |
> | E-Register | line 2077 |
> | E-Init | line 2094 |
> | E-Par | line 2124 |
> | E-Lift | line 2129 |
> | E-Nu | line 2143 |
> | E-Struct | line 2148 |
> 
> Plus failure-extension rules: E-Monitor, E-InvokeM, E-Raise, E-RaiseS, E-CancelMsg, E-CancelAP, E-CancelH (Section 4).
> 
> The paper mentions self-messages exactly once (line 894): "supporting interaction between sessions using state and self-messages" in the informal description of KP4 (interaction between sessions). But this is realized through **shared handler state** (`&amp;mut self`), not through a dedicated self-send rule. The formal calculus supports KP4 via the handler state value `st` that is threaded through E-Suspend/E-React: when a handler suspends with `suspend(handler, state)`, the state `valb` is stored, and when the handler is re-activated by E-React, the stored state is substituted back. Cross-session interaction happens through this shared mutable state, not through self-delivery.
> 
> ### Answers to the five sub-questions:
> 
> **Q1: Is E-Self used in any proof of safety for E-Suspend/E-React?**
> No. E-Self does not exist as a formal rule. The preservation proof (Appendix, line 4743+) has cases for E-Send, E-React, E-Suspend, E-Spawn, E-Reset, E-NewAP only. Self-messaging is never mentioned in any proof.
> 
> **Q2: Does the sequential dispatch guarantee depend on E-Self?**
> No. Sequential dispatch is a consequence of the actor model itself: an actor's thread is either idle(`v`) or active(`E[M]`). E-React can only fire when the actor is idle. Since an actor is a 4-tuple with a single thread field, only one handler executes at a time. This is structural, not dependent on any self-send mechanism.
> 
> **Q3: Could E-Self's absence create a notification-triggers-request gap?**
> This is a separate concern (see Section 4 below). E-Self would be one way to solve it, but the gap exists regardless of E-Self. In EAct, any handler can perform sends (E-Send) during its active phase because the calculus doesn't distinguish notification handlers from request handlers. Pane's split (Handles&lt;P&gt; vs HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;) is a refinement of EAct, not a consequence of missing E-Self.
> 
> **Q4: Is E-Self required for the destruction sequence?**
> No. The destruction sequence maps to EAct's failure extension (E-Raise/E-RaiseS/E-CancelH/E-CancelMsg). None of these rules involve self-messaging. Pane's `run_destruction` -&gt; `fail_connection` -&gt; `clear` -&gt; handler Drop maps cleanly to E-Raise (actor death) + E-CancelH (handler failure callbacks) + E-CancelMsg (queue draining).
> 
> **Q5: If E-Self is not implemented, does ANY EAct theorem fail?**
> No. The three theorems (Preservation, Progress, Global Progress) are proven over the rules listed above. None require self-messaging. KP4 is satisfied through handler state threading, which pane implements via `&amp;mut self` on the handler.
> 
> **Verdict: E-Self can be safely deferred. It is an implementation convenience (like Be's PostMessage), not a formal requirement. When implemented, it should be `Messenger::post_to_self()` enqueuing into the looper's mpsc channel, not a new reduction rule.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. EAct Rule Mapping Table
> 
> ### Core Rules (Section 3.3)
> 
> | EAct Rule | What it does | pane implementation | Status |
> |-----------|-------------|---------------------|--------|
> | **E-Send** | Actor in session s sends message l(v) to role q; message appended to session queue | `ServiceHandle::send_request` (service_handle.rs:70-121) serializes and sends via `write_tx.send()`. `ServiceHandle::send_notification` (service_handle.rs:124-140) for fire-and-forget. Both are non-blocking (mpsc::SyncSender). | **Implemented** |
> | **E-React** | Idle actor with stored handler for (s,p) activates when matching message arrives in session queue | `LooperCore::dispatch_service` (looper_core.rs:255-358) — the looper reads from `rx`, deserializes `ServiceFrame`, routes to either `ServiceDispatch::dispatch_notification` (static table) or `Dispatch::fire_reply`/`fire_failed` (dynamic token table). The handler transitions from idle (blocked on `rx.recv()`) to active (callback executing). | **Implemented** |
> | **E-Suspend** | Active actor installs handler for (s,p) and returns to idle(state) | `Dispatch::insert` via `DispatchCtx::insert` (dispatch_ctx.rs:55-57) — installs a one-shot callback (on_reply + on_failed). The handler method returns `Flow::Continue`, which returns the actor to the idle state (blocked on `rx.recv()` again). In pane, suspend = install entry + return Flow::Continue. | **Implemented** |
> | **E-Reset** | Actor finishes evaluating to value v, returns to idle(v) | Implicit in pane: when a handler method returns `Flow::Continue`, the looper loop iterates back to `rx.recv()`. The handler's `&amp;mut self` IS the persistent state v. There is no explicit reset step because Rust's function return IS the reset. | **Implemented** (structurally, via Rust's control flow) |
> | **E-Spawn** | Spawns a fresh actor with empty handler/init state | Not directly implemented. Pane creates panes via `Pane::new()` + `PaneBuilder::run_with()` but this happens in user code, not as a looper primitive. EAct's E-Spawn creates an actor within the current configuration; pane's model is that pane creation is external (OS-level). | **Not Implemented** — intentional. Pane panes are not spawned from within handlers. This is an architectural choice (panes are processes, not goroutines). See `eact_what_not_to_adopt`. |
> | **E-NewAP** | Creates a fresh access point with role mappings | `PaneBuilder::connect()` + `PaneBuilder::open_service()` (builder.rs:99-199). The DeclareInterest/InterestAccepted handshake is pane's access point negotiation. The server's service map is the access point state. | **Partially Implemented** — pane's access points are server-mediated, not actor-local. The fundamental semantics (negotiate, get session_id, start exchanging) are present. Multi-role registration (E-Register + E-Init pattern) is not — pane uses binary sessions. |
> | **E-Register** | Registers an actor for a role at an access point, creates init token | `PaneBuilder::open_service_inner` (builder.rs:127-182) — sends DeclareInterest, blocks for InterestAccepted. The session_id returned by InterestAccepted is the init token analog. The handler's `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impl is registered in `ServiceDispatch`. | **Implemented** (binary simplification of the multiparty pattern) |
> | **E-Init** | When all roles registered at an AP, establishes session with fresh queue | Server-side: when both DeclareInterest sides match, InterestAccepted is sent with a session_id. Pane doesn't implement this server yet (it connects to external servers), but the protocol is defined. | **Partially Implemented** — consumer side complete, server side stubbed. |
> | **E-Par** | Parallel composition of configurations | Implicit: each pane runs in its own thread, communicating via channels. The OS provides parallel composition. | **Implemented** (via OS threads) |
> | **E-Lift** | Beta-reduction within an actor's thread | Implicit: Rust evaluation within handler methods. | **Implemented** (via Rust's runtime) |
> | **E-Nu** | Name restriction (scoping) | Implicit: Rust's ownership and scoping. Session names (session_id) are scoped to their connection. | **Implemented** (via Rust's scoping) |
> | **E-Struct** | Structural congruence (reordering, scope extrusion) | The mpsc queue preserves ordering within a connection. Cross-connection message reordering is permitted by the asynchronous model. | **Implemented** (via channel semantics) |
> 
> ### Failure Extension Rules (Section 4)
> 
> | EAct Rule | What it does | pane implementation | Status |
> |-----------|-------------|---------------------|--------|
> | **E-Raise** | Actor raises exception, becomes zapper thread, cancels all owned endpoints | `LooperCore::dispatch` catch_unwind path (looper_core.rs:155-167) — panic triggers `run_destruction(ExitReason::Failed)`. The `exited` flag prevents re-entry. | **Implemented** |
> | **E-RaiseS** | Actor raises exception while in a session — also cancels current session endpoint | Same as E-Raise. Pane doesn't distinguish "in session" from "out of session" at the Rust level — a panic during any handler dispatch triggers the full destruction sequence. The current session's endpoint (ReplyPort) is compensated by Drop during unwind. | **Implemented** (via Drop compensation + catch_unwind) |
> | **E-CancelMsg** | Zapper thread drains messages from cancelled endpoint's queue | Not directly implemented. When a pane exits, the mpsc channel is closed. The reader thread on the other side detects this as EOF. Messages in transit are dropped by the OS. Pane relies on the transport layer to handle message draining, not an explicit rule. | **Intentionally Diverged** — pane uses channel closure semantics instead of explicit message cancellation. The effect is equivalent: unreceivable messages are discarded. |
> | **E-CancelAP** | Zapper thread removes cancelled init tokens from access points | `ServiceHandle::Drop` sends RevokeInterest (service_handle.rs:183-197). The server removes the registration. This is the equivalent of removing an init token from the AP. | **Implemented** |
> | **E-CancelH** | When a handler's counterpart is cancelled, fire failure continuation if no pending messages | `Dispatch::fail_connection` (dispatch.rs:118-135) — fires `on_failed` for all entries keyed to a connection. `Dispatch::fail_session` (dispatch.rs:140-157) — fires `on_failed` for entries on a specific session. Both are called during the destruction sequence. | **Implemented** |
> | **E-Monitor** | Register monitoring callback for another actor | Not implemented. Pane's current architecture doesn't have supervision hierarchies. The `exit_tx` channel notifies the server of pane exit, but there's no per-pane monitor registration API. | **Not Implemented** — deferred per PLAN.md. Not needed for Phase 1. |
> | **E-InvokeM** | When monitored actor dies, fire monitor callback | Not implemented. See above. | **Not Implemented** — deferred. |
> 
> ### Additional Structural Rules
> 
> | Rule | pane analog | Status |
> |------|-------------|--------|
> | Queue structural congruence (message reordering between different role pairs) | mpsc channels are FIFO per-connection. Cross-connection ordering is unspecified. | **Implemented** (FIFO is stricter than EAct's partial order) |
> | ibecome (session switching, Appendix B) | Not adopted. Per `eact_what_not_to_adopt`: pane's non-blocking sends mean there's no send state to freeze. | **Intentionally Not Adopted** |
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Safety Theorem Assessment
> 
> ### Theorem 1: Preservation (Theorem 3.6, line 2832)
> 
> &gt; Typability is preserved by structural congruence and reduction. If C is well-typed with safe environment and C -&gt; D, then D is well-typed with safe environment.
> 
> **Assessment: Holds in pane, with caveats.**
> 
> The key preservation invariants in pane:
> - **Type safety of dispatch**: `make_service_receiver` and `make_request_receiver` capture the concrete `P::Message` type at registration time. The tag byte check (S8) prevents type confusion. If the tag matches, `postcard::from_bytes` deserializes into the correct type. Deserialization failure sends `Failed`, preserving the session's type safety.
> - **Linear usage of ReplyPort**: `ReplyPort::reply()` consumes self (move semantics). Double-reply is a compile error. Drop sends `ReplyFailed`. This gives exactly-once response semantics matching EAct's guarantee that every request produces exactly one response.
> - **Handler type binding**: `DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;` binds H=Self at the trait level (dispatch_ctx.rs:35-38, handles_request.rs:52-58). This prevents the type confusion that could arise from casting `*mut ()` to `&amp;mut Dispatch&lt;WrongType&gt;`.
> 
> **Caveat**: pane's type safety is partially dynamic (tag byte check, postcard deserialization) where EAct's is fully static (flow-sensitive effect system). The dynamic checks are compensated by Drop (failure, not unsoundness), so preservation holds in the sense that the system never enters an ill-typed state — but it may enter a failure state where EAct would have rejected the program at compile time.
> 
> ### Theorem 2: Progress (Theorem 3.8, line 2973)
> 
> &gt; If C is well-typed, then either C can reduce, or C is in canonical form (all actors idle, no sessions, no queues).
> 
> **Assessment: Holds for pane's restricted model.**
> 
> The critical argument in EAct is that inter-session deadlocks cannot arise because actors are event-driven: an actor yields when it needs to receive (E-Suspend), and the looper can schedule any handler that has a pending message (E-React). Since an actor never blocks on a receive in one session while holding send obligations in another, the circular dependency that causes inter-session deadlock cannot form (paper lines 2928-2933).
> 
> Pane satisfies this:
> - **Non-blocking sends**: `write_tx.send()` / `write_tx.try_send()` are non-blocking (bounded sync_channel). A handler never blocks waiting to send.
> - **Sequential dispatch**: The looper dispatches one message at a time (looper_core.rs `run()` loop). A handler runs to completion and returns `Flow`, then the looper picks up the next message.
> - **No blocking receives in handlers**: Handlers receive messages via callback dispatch, not by calling a blocking receive. A handler cannot block on a receive in session A while holding obligations in session B.
> 
> **Caveat**: Progress assumes all handlers terminate (thread-terminating condition, Definition 3.10). Pane does not enforce this — a handler could loop forever. This is the same limitation acknowledged in the paper (lines 3068-3076). It's an assumption, not a guarantee.
> 
> ### Theorem 3: Global Progress (Corollary 3.12, line 3139)
> 
> &gt; If C is well-typed and thread-terminating, then for every ongoing session s, communication is eventually possible.
> 
> **Assessment: Holds under the same assumptions as EAct.**
> 
> Global progress requires:
> 1. **Independence of thread reductions** (Lemma 3.8) — thread reduction in one actor doesn't inhibit another. Pane: each pane runs on its own thread with its own looper. Inter-pane communication is via channels. Independence holds by OS thread isolation.
> 2. **Thread-terminating actors reach idle state** (Corollary 3.9) — if all threads terminate, the configuration reaches an idle state. Pane: if all handler methods return, the looper returns to `rx.recv()`, which is the idle state.
> 3. **Idle configurations with ongoing sessions can reduce** (Lemma 3.10) — every ongoing session in an idle configuration has a pending message that can fire E-React. Pane: if a message is in the mpsc channel and the looper is blocked on `rx.recv()`, it will wake up and dispatch.
> 
> ### Preservation with Failure (Theorem 4.2, line 3794)
> 
> **Assessment: Holds.**
> 
> Pane's failure model maps cleanly to EAct-zap:
> - `catch_unwind` -&gt; E-Raise/E-RaiseS (actor failure)
> - `Dispatch::fail_connection` -&gt; E-CancelH (fire failure continuations)
> - Channel closure -&gt; E-CancelMsg (discard unreceivable messages)
> - `ServiceHandle::Drop` -&gt; E-CancelAP (remove registrations)
> - `ReplyPort::Drop` -&gt; Drop compensation (fire failure signal)
> 
> The destruction sequence in `run_destruction` (looper_core.rs:211-223) implements the cascading failure in the correct order: fail outstanding requests, clear remaining entries, drop handler (obligation compensation), notify server.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Notification-Triggers-Request Verdict: VALID REFINEMENT
> 
> ### The question
> 
> EAct's handlers can perform any operation during their active phase — including E-Send and E-Suspend (installing new handlers). In pane, `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` (notification handler) gets `&amp;mut self` only, while `HandlesRequest&lt;P&gt;::receive_request` gets `&amp;mut self` + `ReplyPort` + `&amp;mut DispatchCtx&lt;Self&gt;`. The `DispatchCtx` is what enables issuing outbound requests via `ServiceHandle::send_request`. So notification handlers cannot issue requests.
> 
> ### Analysis
> 
> **Q1: Does EAct require that any handler be able to issue requests during any dispatch?**
> 
> Yes, in the formal model. EAct's handlers are plain functions that can perform any computation, including send and suspend. There is no distinction between notification handlers and request handlers in the calculus. Any handler can do anything during its active phase.
> 
> **Q2: Does the restriction violate an EAct invariant?**
> 
> It violates the **expressiveness** of EAct (KP4: interaction between sessions), but not its **safety theorems**. The safety theorems (Preservation, Progress, Global Progress) are about well-typedness and deadlock freedom. They don't require that every handler can issue requests — they require that whatever a handler does is well-typed and terminates. A handler that can only observe (notification) is trivially well-typed in any context where a handler that can also send would be.
> 
> The restriction is a **refinement** — it restricts the space of programs that can be written, but every program that CAN be written under the restriction is also a valid EAct program. Refinements preserve all safety properties because they only remove behaviors, never add them.
> 
> **Q3: Is the restriction a valid refinement of EAct?**
> 
> Yes, with a practical gap. The restriction means that when a notification handler needs to trigger a request (e.g., "I received a clipboard change notification and now need to request the clipboard data"), it cannot do so directly. The handler must either:
> 1. Store state in `&amp;mut self` and hope a request handler fires later (fragile, not always possible)
> 2. Use a self-message mechanism (E-Self / PostMessage) to re-enter the looper with `DispatchCtx` available (not yet implemented)
> 3. Use `Messenger::post_to_self()` to post a message that arrives as a request-capable dispatch (same as 2)
> 
> This is the **notification-triggers-request gap**. It doesn't break safety theorems, but it does break KP4 (interaction between sessions) for notification-only protocol relationships. When a pane receives a notification from protocol A and needs to issue a request on protocol B, it currently cannot do so in the same dispatch cycle.
> 
> **Recommended resolution**: Implement `Messenger::post_to_self()` (E-Self as an implementation convenience). This preserves sequential dispatch (the self-message is enqueued, not executed inline) and gives notification handlers an escape hatch to reach request-capable dispatch. This is consistent with Be's `PostMessage(this, msg)` pattern and is explicitly mentioned in the `eact_what_not_to_adopt` memory as the mechanism for cross-session causality.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Recommendations for Formal Verification
> 
> ### Must Fix Before Verification
> 
> **Nothing is blocking.** The implementation is sound with respect to EAct's theorems. All three safety properties hold under the current architecture. The gaps are expressiveness limitations, not soundness violations.
> 
> ### Should Fix Soon (Priority Order)
> 
> 1. **Notification-triggers-request gap** — implement `Messenger::post_to_self()`. This is not a soundness issue but it's a significant expressiveness gap that will bite real applications. Without it, complex protocols that mix notifications and requests will require awkward workarounds. Priority because it affects API design and user-facing code patterns.
> 
> 2. **Cancel message wiring** — `Dispatch::cancel()` exists but is `#[allow(dead_code)]` (dispatch.rs:112). `CancelHandle::new(|| {})` is a stub (service_handle.rs:120). The cancel path needs to send a Cancel frame on the wire so the server can clean up the pending request. This maps to the paper's general principle that resources must be explicitly released. Not a soundness issue (the server's `process_disconnect` is the backstop), but it's a resource leak.
> 
> 3. **ServiceTeardown -&gt; fail_session propagation** — implemented (looper_core.rs:396-404) but the return value of `fail_session`'s on_failed callbacks is discarded (`let _`). If a handler returns `Flow::Stop` from an on_failed callback during session teardown, the stop is silently ignored. This is arguably correct (teardown is not the handler's choice) but should be documented.
> 
> ### Verified Sound (No Action Needed)
> 
> - **E-Suspend/E-React cycle**: `Dispatch::insert` -&gt; `fire_reply` is correctly one-shot (HashMap remove). Token uniqueness is guaranteed by monotonic counter.
> - **Install-before-wire invariant**: `DispatchCtx::insert()` is called before `write_tx.send()` in `send_request` (service_handle.rs:98 vs 116). This matches Plan 9's devmnt.c:786-798 pattern.
> - **ReplyPort exactly-once**: `Option::take()` pattern ensures the closure fires exactly once (obligation.rs:113-116). Drop fires only if take() hasn't been called. Move semantics prevent double-reply at compile time.
> - **Destruction sequence ordering**: fail_connection (S4) -&gt; clear (S4 step 2) -&gt; handler Drop (I9) -&gt; exit_tx. Tested in `destruction_sequence_ordering` test.
> - **catch_unwind boundary**: AssertUnwindSafe is sound because the handler is never re-used after panic (exited flag, looper_core.rs:146-148).
> - **Tag byte validation (S8)**: Both `make_service_receiver` and `make_request_receiver` check the tag before deserialization. Mismatch in notification = silent drop. Mismatch in request = send Failed (no orphaned dispatch entries).
> - **Protocol tag confusion across sessions**: Session_id is monotonic per connection. No recycling within a live connection. ABA prevention is correct-by-construction (Gap 5 resolution from `eact_analysis_gaps`).
> 
> ### For Agda Formalization
> 
> The natural starting points for formal verification are:
> 
> 1. **ReplyPort exactly-once property** — this is the simplest linear resource to formalize. The state space is {Pending, Replied, Dropped}, transitions are {reply: Pending -&gt; Replied, drop: Pending -&gt; Dropped}. Both terminal states produce exactly one output. Formalize as a linear type in Agda using ternary-relations separating conjunction.
> 
> 2. **Dispatch one-shot property** — insert creates an entry, fire_reply/fire_failed/cancel consumes it. The HashMap remove gives one-shot semantics. Formalize as a separation logic resource: token ownership is exclusive, consumption is irrevocable.
> 
> 3. **Destruction sequence ordering** — the ordering invariant (fail_connection before clear before handler Drop) is a sequential composition of resource cleanup operations. Each operation has a precondition (dispatch is populated) and postcondition (specific entries are removed/compensated). Formalize as a Hoare triple sequence.
> 
> 4. **Install-before-wire** — this is a happens-before relationship: entry installation happens-before wire send, which happens-before reply arrival, which happens-before fire_reply. Formalize as a causal ordering on events.
> 
> The inter-session deadlock freedom argument (Progress/Global Progress) is significantly harder to formalize because it requires reasoning about the global configuration of all actors. I'd recommend deferring this until the per-actor properties above are verified.</result>

> </task-notification>

>

Without monitors, zapper threads are immediately garbage-collected via structural congruence `(nu a)(zap(a)) || C === C` instead of first invoking a callback. ServiceTeardown covers session-scoped notification.

> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a4d47c9f8404c4f48.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Vet EAct divergences (Be perspective)" completed</summary>
> <result>I have all the evidence I need. Let me now write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 1: E-CancelMsg -- Channel Closure vs. Explicit Message Draining
> 
> ### What BeOS/Haiku actually did
> 
> BeOS explicitly drained. The `~BLooper()` destructor at `src/kits/app/Looper.cpp:108-165` shows a two-phase teardown:
> 
> **Phase 1 -- close_port, drain the in-process queue:**
> ```cpp
> // Close the message port and read and reply to the remaining messages.
> if (fMsgPort &gt;= 0 &amp;&amp; fOwnsPort)
>     close_port(fMsgPort);
> ```
> 
> `close_port()` (kernel `port.cpp:1062-1088`) sets capacity to zero, waking all blocked readers/writers with `B_BAD_PORT_ID`. No new messages can be written. But the port still exists and buffered messages can still be read.
> 
> Then Haiku drains the direct-target queue (in-process shortcut path):
> ```cpp
> BMessage* message;
> while ((message = fDirectTarget-&gt;Queue()-&gt;NextMessage()) != NULL) {
>     delete message;  // msg will automagically post generic reply
> }
> ```
> 
> **Phase 2 -- drain the kernel port, then delete it:**
> ```cpp
> if (fOwnsPort) {
>     do {
>         delete ReadMessageFromPort(0);  // msg will automagically post generic reply
>     } while (IsMessageWaiting());
>     delete_port(fMsgPort);
> }
> ```
> 
> The key is the "automagically post generic reply" comment. `BMessage::_Clear()` at `Message.cpp:399-430` does this:
> 
> ```cpp
> if (IsSourceWaiting())
>     SendReply(B_NO_REPLY);
> ```
> 
> If someone sent a synchronous `SendMessage(msg, &amp;reply)`, the sender is blocked on a temporary reply port waiting for a response. When the looper dies and the BMessage is deleted during draining, `_Clear()` sends `B_NO_REPLY` back to the blocked sender, unblocking them. Without this drain, the sender would block forever.
> 
> **Was this necessary? Was there ever a bug?** Yes, it was absolutely necessary. BeOS had synchronous SendMessage (the `SendMessage(BMessage*, BMessage* reply)` variant), which creates a temporary port and blocks (`Message.cpp:2285-2397`). If the target looper dies without sending a reply, the caller is stuck. The drain-and-reply-B_NO_REPLY pattern was the only thing that prevented permanent hangs.
> 
> ### How pane differs and whether it's safe
> 
> pane doesn't have synchronous send-and-wait-for-reply at the channel level. `Messenger::send_message()` is fire-and-forget. Request/reply uses `Dispatch` with `(on_reply, on_failed)` callback pairs. When the looper exits, `run_destruction()` calls `dispatch.fail_connection()` which fires every pending `on_failed` callback, then `dispatch.clear()`. This is pane's equivalent of the drain -- but it operates on the callback table, not the message queue.
> 
> Messages in the mpsc channel are dropped. This is fine because:
> 
> 1. **No synchronous senders are blocked.** pane's sends are non-blocking. There's no thread sitting on a reply port. Dropping the message is the same as "it arrived but the handler was already gone."
> 
> 2. **Obligation handles compensate.** `ReplyPort&lt;T&gt;::drop()` calls `f(Err(ReplyFailed))`. This is the structural equivalent of `B_NO_REPLY` -- the requester's failure callback fires. Unlike BeOS, this is enforced by the type system, not by the message destructor happening to be called during a drain loop.
> 
> 3. **The drain was only important because of synchronous send.** If BeOS had never had `SendMessage(msg, &amp;reply)`, the drain would have been cosmetic -- messages would simply be lost, same as pane. The drain existed to serve a feature pane deliberately doesn't have.
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE.** The divergence is sound because pane eliminated the root cause that made draining necessary. EAct's E-CancelMsg assumes the Erlang model where a process might be doing a synchronous receive. pane's calloop/mpsc model doesn't have blocking receives in userspace. The obligation handle Drop path (`ReplyPort`, `CompletionReplyPort`) is actually *stronger* than BeOS's drain -- it's type-enforced rather than relying on the message happening to be deleted before the port is destroyed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 2: E-Spawn -- Panes Are OS-Level, Not Spawnable from Handlers
> 
> ### What BeOS/Haiku actually did
> 
> Yes, you could absolutely create a new BLooper from within `MessageReceived()`. There was no restriction. The constructor allocated a port, `Run()` spawned a thread, and the new looper was independent:
> 
> ```cpp
> // Looper.cpp:480
> fThread = spawn_thread(_task0_, Name(), fInitPriority, this);
> ```
> 
> This was a *common* pattern. App servers, viewers, document windows -- you'd create new BWindows (which are BLoopers) from handler code all the time. "User clicks New Window" -&gt; `MessageReceived(B_NEW_WINDOW)` -&gt; `new BWindow(...)` -&gt; `window-&gt;Show()`. That's the standard Be application lifecycle.
> 
> **Lifecycle and supervision:** There was *no implicit supervision*. The new looper's lifecycle was entirely independent of the parent's. There was no parent-child relationship, no crash propagation, no automatic cleanup. If you created a looper in a handler and the parent died, the child kept running. If the child died, the parent had no way to know unless it was explicitly watching via `BMessenger::IsValid()` (which is poll-based -- `Messenger.cpp:263-267`, just calls `get_port_info()`).
> 
> `BApplication` was the exception -- it maintained a list of windows and would quit them on shutdown. But that was BApplication-specific logic, not a general looper-to-looper supervision mechanism.
> 
> **What went wrong:** The lack of supervision was a real source of bugs in Be applications:
> 
> - Orphaned windows after the main app loop exited
> - No way to detect child looper death except polling `IsValid()`
> - No cascading failure -- if a critical sub-looper crashed, the parent kept running in a broken state
> - Memory leaks from loopers that were created but never properly quit
> 
> ### How pane differs and whether it's safe
> 
> pane's `Pane::new()` is external construction only. You can't call it from within a handler's dispatch context because the handler receives `&amp;mut self` and `&amp;Messenger`, neither of which provides a factory for new panes.
> 
> This is *more restrictive* than BeOS. But:
> 
> 1. **The use case still exists** -- applications that need multiple panes (like a multi-window editor) create them from their outer control flow, not from within a handler. The pattern is: outer supervisor creates panes, panes communicate via messages, supervisor monitors lifecycle.
> 
> 2. **Avoiding Be's mistake.** Be's lack of supervision was a genuine design weakness. Spawning a looper from within a handler with no automatic lifecycle binding created orphan problems that Be never solved. By making pane creation external, pane forces the lifecycle relationship to be explicit.
> 
> 3. **EAct's E-Spawn** assumes Erlang-style lightweight processes where spawn-from-handler is cheap and supervision trees manage lifecycle. pane doesn't have a supervision tree yet (see Divergence 4), but the restriction at least prevents the *unsupervised spawn* problem that made Be apps fragile.
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE, with a caveat.** The restriction is correct for Phase 1. The caveat: as pane matures, there will be legitimate use cases for handler-initiated pane creation (a chat app spawning a new conversation pane). The right solution is a supervised factory -- `Messenger::request_pane()` or similar -- that creates the pane through the server with an explicit lifecycle relationship. The current restriction prevents the unsupervised spawn anti-pattern; it should evolve into supervised spawn, not be relaxed into the Be free-for-all.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 3: ibecome -- No Session Switching
> 
> ### What BeOS/Haiku actually did
> 
> There was *never* a yield-mid-MessageReceived pattern in BeOS. `MessageReceived()` was synchronous and expected to return promptly. The design commandment was: **don't block the looper thread.**
> 
> The specific case EAct's `ibecome` addresses -- needing to wait for a reply from another handler while processing a message -- was handled in BeOS in three ways, all of them awkward:
> 
> **1. Synchronous SendMessage (the bad way):**
> ```cpp
> // Sends message and blocks until reply arrives
> BMessenger(target).SendMessage(request, &amp;reply);
> ```
> This worked for *cross-looper* communication. But if you sent synchronously to a handler on the *same* looper, you deadlocked. The message goes into the looper's port queue, but the looper thread is blocked waiting for the reply, so the message is never dispatched. This was a well-known Be pitfall. The newsletter (`Issue4-30.html:517-519`) explicitly warns about it:
> 
> &gt; "It's important to make sure a reply gets sent, in case somebody is waiting synchronously for a reply."
> 
> **2. Async send with state machine (the right way):**
> Post the request asynchronously, return from `MessageReceived()`, and handle the reply in a subsequent `MessageReceived()` call. This required the handler to maintain state across messages -- essentially a manual state machine. It was the recommended pattern but verbose and error-prone.
> 
> **3. DetachCurrentMessage (the escape hatch):**
> Pull the current message out of the looper's dispatch cycle, stash it, do async work, and handle it later. This was used for long operations but was tricky to get right -- the newsletter warns that if you `DetachCurrentMessage()` without eventually deleting it or sending a reply, the sender hangs forever.
> 
> The Haiku implementation confirms this at `Looper.cpp:2147-2158` -- for local targets, `_SendMessage` bypasses the port entirely and enqueues directly into the BDirectMessageTarget queue, specifically to avoid "deadlocks when the queue is full." But it doesn't solve the same-looper synchronous deadlock.
> 
> ### How pane differs and whether it's safe
> 
> pane has non-blocking sends. `Messenger::send_message()` returns immediately. The request/reply pattern uses `Dispatch` callbacks (`on_reply`, `on_failed`), which fire in a future dispatch cycle. There is never a case where a handler blocks mid-dispatch waiting for another message.
> 
> This makes `ibecome` unnecessary. `ibecome` is a formalization of "put down this conversation, go do another one, come back." pane's event loop does this implicitly -- each dispatch cycle handles one message, and the loop processes whatever is next.
> 
> The state that `ibecome` would track is carried by the `Dispatch` table entries. When you `send_request`, the callback pair is stored in `Dispatch`. When the reply arrives in a later dispatch cycle, the callback fires with the handler as context. This is the async state machine pattern (approach #2 from BeOS) but made type-safe and less error-prone by the `Dispatch`/`ReplyPort` machinery.
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE.** This is the cleanest of the four divergences. `ibecome` solves a problem pane doesn't have. BeOS's same-looper deadlock was a direct consequence of supporting synchronous send, which pane deliberately avoids. The `Dispatch` callback mechanism is the correct async alternative, and it's structurally the same as what good BeOS code did manually -- but with type-enforced reply obligations instead of hoping the programmer remembers to delete the message.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 4: E-Monitor/E-InvokeM -- No Supervision Hierarchy
> 
> ### What BeOS/Haiku actually did
> 
> BeOS had monitoring at the *application* level but not at the looper level.
> 
> **BRoster::StartWatching** (`Roster.h:40-51`, `Roster.cpp:836-880`) let you watch for app-level events:
> - `B_REQUEST_LAUNCHED` / `B_SOME_APP_LAUNCHED` -- an app started
> - `B_REQUEST_QUIT` / `B_SOME_APP_QUIT` -- an app exited
> - `B_REQUEST_ACTIVATED` / `B_SOME_APP_ACTIVATED` -- an app was activated
> 
> This was registrar-mediated. You told the registrar "notify me at this BMessenger when apps come and go." The registrar sent you `B_SOME_APP_QUIT` messages. But this was *only for whole applications*, not individual loopers or handlers.
> 
> **BMessenger::IsValid()** (`Messenger.cpp:263-267`) was the only way to check if a specific looper was alive:
> ```cpp
> bool BMessenger::IsValid() const {
>     port_info info;
>     return fPort &gt;= 0 &amp;&amp; get_port_info(fPort, &amp;info) == B_OK;
> }
> ```
> This is a *poll* -- no push notification. You had to actively check. And it only told you if the port existed, not if the looper was healthy.
> 
> **SendMessage failure** was the other detection mechanism. If you sent to a dead looper, `write_port()` returned `B_BAD_PORT_ID`. But by the time you detected this, any messages you'd sent between the looper's death and your detection were silently lost.
> 
> **What Be lacked:** There was no Erlang-style `monitor(Pid)` that would deliver a `DOWN` message when the target died. No supervision tree. No crash propagation. No restart policy. If a sub-looper died, the parent had to notice on its own, and there was no standard mechanism for doing so.
> 
> ### How pane differs and whether it's safe
> 
> pane currently has:
> 
> - `ExitReason` enum (`Graceful`, `Failed`, `Disconnected`) -- the server knows *why* a pane exited
> - `exit_tx` channel -- the looper sends its exit reason to the server on teardown
> - Connection loss detection -- the server detects when a pane's transport drops
> - Obligation handle compensation -- `ReplyPort::drop()` fires `ReplyFailed` to pending requesters
> 
> What pane *doesn't* have:
> 
> - Pane-watches-pane monitoring (one pane notified when another dies)
> - Supervisor/restart policy
> - Death notification via the messaging system
> 
> ### Assessment
> 
> This is the divergence I'm least comfortable with. Let me explain why.
> 
> In practice, BeOS applications *needed* supervision and didn't have it. The result was fragile multi-window apps, orphaned server-side state when clients crashed, and no standard way to build resilient multi-pane applications. Be's roster watching was too coarse (app-level only) and `IsValid()` was too weak (poll-only, no reason).
> 
> pane already has better infrastructure than BeOS did -- the server knows the exit reason, and obligation handles provide per-request failure notification. But there's a gap: pane A cannot say "tell me when pane B dies." If pane A has a meaningful relationship with pane B (e.g., a toolbar pane and its document pane), pane A has no way to learn that pane B has crashed except by sending it a message and seeing the send fail.
> 
> That said, this is a **phasing** issue, not a **design** issue. The architecture has the right building blocks:
> 
> 1. The server already tracks exit reasons
> 2. The messaging system can deliver notifications
> 3. `ServiceHandle` already has best-effort cleanup on drop
> 
> A future `ExitBroadcaster` (mentioned in the eact_analysis_gaps memory as part of Gap 4's resolution) can deliver `pane_exited(PaneId, ExitReason)` notifications to watchers through normal message dispatch.
> 
> **Verdict: RISKY for the current phase, but architecturally recoverable.** The risk is real: any multi-pane application (which is most non-trivial apps) will need death notification. Deferring it is reasonable for Phase 1, but this should be the *first* thing added in Phase 2. The design should be push-based (`watch_pane(id, messenger)` at the server level), not poll-based like BeOS's `IsValid()`, and should carry the exit reason, which BeOS's roster notifications did not.
> 
> Do not adopt Erlang-style restart policies or supervision trees -- that's overengineering for a desktop compositor. The right model is closer to BRoster::StartWatching but scoped to individual panes and with rich failure reasons. A watcher receives a message; what it does with that information is its business.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary Table
> 
> | Divergence | EAct Rule | pane Approach | Verdict | Key Evidence |
> |---|---|---|---|---|
> | Channel closure | E-CancelMsg | Drop channel, obligation handles compensate | **SAFE** | BeOS drain existed for sync send; pane has no sync send |
> | No handler-spawned panes | E-Spawn | External `Pane::new()` only | **SAFE** | BeOS had unsupervised spawn, caused orphan bugs |
> | No session switching | ibecome | Non-blocking sends + Dispatch callbacks | **SAFE** | BeOS had same-looper deadlock from sync send; pane avoids it |
> | No supervision | E-Monitor | Server tracks exits, no pane-to-pane notification yet | **RISKY** | BeOS lacked this too and it hurt; pane needs it for Phase 2 |
> 
> The first three divergences are not just safe -- they're *improvements*. Each eliminates a class of bug that existed in BeOS by removing the mechanism that caused the bug (synchronous send, unsupervised spawn). The fourth is a genuine gap that should be addressed soon, before multi-pane applications are built on pane.</result>

> 
> ## Divergence Audit: Categorical Soundness of pane's EAct Departures
> 
> ### 1. E-CancelMsg: Channel Closure vs. Explicit Draining
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE — with a structural precondition that pane already enforces.**
> 
> **Categorical framing.** In EAct, E-CancelMsg is a *coinductive observation* on the session queue: given a zapper thread `⚡(s,q)` and a queue entry `⟨s, p→q, ℓ, v⟩ · Q`, the rule removes the head entry and continues to the tail. This is the counit ε : F∘U → Id of the free/forgetful adjunction on the queue — it extracts each element, observes it (triggering failure effects on nested capabilities), and continues until the queue is empty. The process is structurally inductive on the queue length.
> 
> pane's channel closure is instead the *terminal morphism* — it maps the entire queue to the terminal object (⊤). No observation occurs; the queue is discarded as a unit. In categorical terms: E-CancelMsg computes `ε ∘ ε ∘ ... ∘ ε` (iterated counit), while channel closure computes the unique morphism to ⊤.
> 
> **When these diverge.** The only case where they produce different observable behavior is when a pending message *carries a nested capability* — something that requires observation to discharge. In EAct, a message can carry a session endpoint (a role identifier `r(s,p)` that is itself a linear resource). Draining observes each such endpoint and triggers its cancellation cascade. Total destruction skips this, leaving the nested capability in limbo — the session it belongs to would lose progress.
> 
> **Why pane is safe.** pane's `Message` trait requires `Serialize + DeserializeOwned + Clone + Send + 'static`. This is a *positive-only* constraint — all four bounds exclude linear resources:
> - `Clone` excludes move-only types (obligation handles, session endpoints)
> - `Serialize` excludes closure-bearing types (ReplyPort, ServiceHandle)
> - The blanket impl makes this structural, not opt-in
> 
> Obligation handles (`ReplyPort&lt;T&gt;`, `CompletionReplyPort`, `CancelHandle`, `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`) are explicitly *not* Messages (documented in obligation.rs:1-7 and message.rs:6-7). They live in handler state (`&amp;mut H`) and the Dispatch table, never in the channel's message queue. Rule R1 ("No Attribute in Message") and R3 ("Obligations are !Message") from the session/optic boundary rules enforce this at the type level.
> 
> Therefore: pane's message queues contain only positive (Kleisli) data. The counit on positive data is the identity — there is nothing to observe, no capability to discharge, no failure cascade to trigger. The morphism to ⊤ and the iterated counit produce the same observable effect: none.
> 
> The destruction sequence in `LooperCore::run_destruction` handles the *handler-side* resources: `fail_connection` fires on_failed for every pending Dispatch entry (conversation-level failure), `clear()` drops the rest, and then handler Drop fires obligation compensation. This is pane's equivalent of E-CancelH (failure continuation invocation), applied to the handler store rather than the queue. The queue itself is safely discardable because it contains only values.
> 
> **Condition for continued safety:** If pane ever introduces a Message type that carries a linear resource or capability (e.g., a file descriptor, a service handle passed by value), this divergence becomes WRONG. The Clone + Serialize bounds currently prevent this. If those bounds are ever relaxed for any message type, draining must be reconsidered.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. E-Spawn: OS-Level Panes
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE — pane's configuration space is a *different* category, not a smaller one.**
> 
> **Categorical framing.** In EAct, E-Spawn creates a new object in the *actor configuration category* — a category whose objects are actor configurations `⟨a | t | σ | ι | ω⟩` and whose morphisms are reduction steps. The configuration category is closed under spawn: for any actor `a` with thread `E[spawn M]`, E-Spawn produces a fresh actor `b` with thread `M` alongside `a` with thread `E[return b]`, all within a single configuration. The category's tensor (parallel composition `∥`) is internal.
> 
> pane delegates to the OS. Each pane is a separate OS process (or, more precisely, a separate thread within the server process, but with OS-level isolation for the transport). The tensor is *external* — it's the OS's process/thread composition, not a calculus-internal parallel operator.
> 
> **Compositional structure.** The question is whether this affects the compositional structure — whether every EAct configuration is expressible as a pane configuration.
> 
> The answer is that pane's configuration space is *not strictly smaller* — it's categorically distinct in a way that doesn't lose expressiveness for the target domain. The key differences:
> 
> 1. **Spawn is not synchronous return.** EAct's `spawn M` returns the PID synchronously to the spawning actor. pane's spawn is mediated through the server — the spawner sends a message and eventually receives confirmation. This changes the morphism type (synchronous return vs. asynchronous acknowledgment) but not the reachable configurations. Any configuration reachable via synchronous spawn is also reachable via asynchronous spawn + wait, just through a different path in the reduction graph.
> 
> 2. **Fresh name generation.** EAct's `(ν b)` creates a name-restricted scope. pane uses UUIDs (Address) and kernel-assigned identifiers (PID, socket fd). The name generation mechanism is different but both produce globally unique identifiers with scope-restricted visibility (pane achieves restriction via PeerAuth and the server's routing table rather than lexical scoping).
> 
> 3. **Empty initial state.** EAct spawns with `⟨b | M | ε | ε | ∅⟩` (empty handler store, empty init state, empty monitor set). pane's PaneBuilder constructs the handler before entering the looper. This is a phase distinction (construction vs. execution), not a restriction — the handler is fully initialized before it processes any messages.
> 
> **What pane cannot express.** There is one genuine restriction: EAct's spawn is an *internal* reduction — one actor can spawn another within the same computation step. pane's OS-level spawn crosses an IPC boundary. This means pane cannot express *spawn-and-immediately-interact-in-the-same-step* patterns. But pane's single-threaded actor model already prevents within-step interaction (everything is sequential dispatch), so this restriction has no practical effect. The independence theorem (EAct Theorem 4.5) already proves that spawn commutes with all other thread reductions — the ordering doesn't matter.
> 
> **Configuration space comparison.** Modulo the phase distinction (construction/execution) and the name generation mechanism, the reachable configurations are isomorphic. Every EAct multi-actor system with `n` actors maps to a pane system with `n` panes connected through a ProtocolServer. The ProtocolServer's routing table is the external representation of EAct's session queues.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. ibecome: Not Adopted
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE — with a precise characterization of the residual gap.**
> 
> **Categorical framing.** `ibecome` in EAct is a *session context switch*: given an actor with handler store `σ[r(s₁,p₁) ↦ (H₁, v₁)]` currently evaluating in session `s₂`, `ibecome` freezes `s₂`'s state type instance, installs a handler for `s₁`, and resumes evaluation in `s₁`'s context. In duploid terms, this is a polarity switch within the active phase — the actor shifts from being negative (waiting in `s₂`) to being negative (waiting in `s₁`), passing through a positive intermediate (the frozen state type instance, `LOption[S]`).
> 
> More precisely: `ibecome` exists because EAct's sends are *blocking* in the session state machine sense — after `send`, the actor must `suspend` (yield to the event loop) to wait for the next input event. If the actor is in session `s₂` and needs to act on session `s₁`, it cannot do so without first yielding `s₂`. `ibecome` is the mechanism for "I want to stop waiting on `s₂` and start waiting on `s₁` instead, within a single handler invocation."
> 
> **pane's avoidance.** pane's sends are non-blocking (`SyncSender::try_send` in ServiceHandle, mpsc channel sends in the writer thread). A handler processing a message in session `s₂` can immediately send to session `s₁` via `ServiceHandle&lt;P1&gt;::send_notification()` or `send_request()` — no yield required. The send completes (or fails) synchronously from the handler's perspective.
> 
> **Is the avoidance categorically complete?** No, but the residual gap is narrow and well-characterized:
> 
> 1. **Blocked sends → non-blocking sends.** Complete. This is the primary use case for `ibecome` (see EAct Fig. 11, the Shop restocking example). pane's non-blocking sends eliminate it entirely.
> 
> 2. **Session-dependent control flow.** `ibecome` also allows an actor to *branch on the current state of another session* within a handler. Example: "While handling a Customer checkout in session `s₁`, check whether the Restock session `s₂` has made progress, and if so, use its result." In pane, this is handled by handler state — the Restock session's progress updates arrive as messages that mutate `&amp;mut self` state. When the Customer checkout handler runs, it reads `self.restock_status` (a field updated by a previous Restock message). The information flow is equivalent; the timing model differs (EAct: synchronous inspection of frozen session state; pane: asynchronous update of handler state). The observational semantics are the same because pane's single-threaded dispatch linearizes all state access.
> 
> 3. **Yielding for CPU fairness.** `ibecome` implicitly yields to the event loop. If an actor has long-running computation in one session, `ibecome` to another session gives other sessions a chance to make progress. pane's handlers must be non-blocking (I2) — long-running work should be offloaded to a worker thread. This is a different mitigation for the same problem, and it's strictly stronger (it doesn't just yield to other sessions on the same actor, it frees the entire looper).
> 
> 4. **Multi-session atomic operations.** The one genuinely inexpressible pattern: an actor that needs to atomically inspect the state of sessions `s₁` and `s₂` and make a decision based on both. `ibecome` achieves this by freezing `s₁` and evaluating `s₂` in the same handler invocation. In pane, the sequential dispatch achieves this naturally — between two consecutive handler dispatches, the handler state is consistent across all sessions. This is actually *stronger* than `ibecome` because pane's `&amp;mut self` encompasses all sessions simultaneously, while `ibecome` can only hold one session's state type at a time.
> 
> **The residual gap.** The one thing `ibecome` provides that pane's architecture does not is *proactive session switching* — the ability to say "I'm done waiting on `s₂`, start waiting on `s₁` instead" without processing an intervening event. In pane, the handler returns `Flow::Continue` and the looper processes whatever arrives next — the handler cannot direct which session's event arrives next. This is a scheduling concern, not a correctness concern. The progress theorem (EAct Theorem 5.5) holds without `ibecome` — the paper itself notes (line 4143): "the system without `ibecome` will enjoy global progress provided every handler is terminating."
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. E-Monitor: No Actor-Level Supervision
> 
> **Verdict: RISKY — session-scoped monitoring is strictly weaker. The gap is characterized; assess whether it's load-bearing for pane's target domain.**
> 
> **Categorical framing.** In EAct, `monitor a v` is a comonadic observation on the actor configuration. The monitor state `ω` pairs actor names with callbacks: `ω = {(b, v)}`. When actor `b` crashes, E-InvokeM evaluates `v b` — the callback receives the crashed actor's PID and can take recovery action (spawn a replacement, clean up resources, notify other actors).
> 
> Formally, monitoring is an *indexed comonad*: the actor configuration carries an observer structure (the `ω` component) that extracts failure information from any actor in the configuration without modifying the observed actor's reduction. The key property is that monitoring is *orthogonal to sessions* — actor `a` can monitor actor `b` regardless of whether they share any sessions. E-Monitor installs the observation; E-InvokeM fires the extraction.
> 
> **pane's proxy.** ServiceTeardown is a *session-scoped* signal: when a peer's service binding is broken (either by RevokeInterest or connection death), the ProtocolServer sends `ServiceTeardown { session_id, reason }` to the other party. LooperCore dispatches it via `fail_session(session_id)`, which fires on_failed for all Dispatch entries on that session.
> 
> **What session-scoped monitoring provides:**
> - Notification that a specific service relationship has ended
> - Reason for teardown (peer revoked, peer disconnected, server decided)
> - Per-conversation failure callbacks via Dispatch entries
> - Handler lifecycle notification via `disconnected()` if the *connection* dies
> 
> **What actor-scoped monitoring provides that session-scoped does not:**
> 
> 1. **Crash detection without shared sessions.** If actor `a` monitors actor `b`, and `b` crashes without ever establishing a session with `a`, the monitor still fires. In pane, if pane A has no service relationship with pane B, A has no way to detect B's death. ServiceTeardown only flows through established bindings.
> 
> 2. **Reason discrimination.** EAct's monitor callback receives the crashed actor's PID, allowing the supervisor to distinguish *which* monitored actor failed. pane's `disconnected()` fires when the connection dies — it doesn't identify which specific peer caused the problem (though ServiceTeardown does carry session_id, so within a connection, the source is identifiable).
> 
> 3. **Restart semantics.** EAct's supervisor pattern (`shopSup`) monitors, detects crash, re-spawns, re-monitors — a clean cycle. pane has no equivalent first-class construct. A pane that loses its connection can reconnect, but the "monitor → detect crash → re-spawn → re-register" cycle requires manual orchestration. The ProtocolServer knows when connections die but doesn't expose a supervision API.
> 
> 4. **Cross-session failure correlation.** If actor `b` participates in sessions `s₁, s₂, ..., sₙ` and crashes, the monitor fires once (actor-level). In pane, ServiceTeardown fires N times (once per session). This is actually *more informative* at the session level but *less informative* at the actor level — pane can see each session die individually but cannot correlate them into "pane B is dead."
> 
> **Assessment.** The information gap is real. Session-scoped monitoring is a faithful image of E-CancelH (per-session failure), but it does not capture E-Monitor + E-InvokeM (per-actor failure observation from an external vantage point).
> 
> However, there's a structural argument for why this may be acceptable for pane:
> 
> - pane's architecture has the ProtocolServer as the central routing authority. The server already knows when connections die (the reader thread exits, the transport closes). Adding actor-level monitoring is a *server feature*, not a calculus extension — it requires the server to maintain a monitoring table and send notifications, which is infrastructure work but not a categorical change.
> 
> - The progress theorem with zapper threads (EAct Theorem 5.11) requires E-CancelMsg and E-CancelH for progress, not E-Monitor. Monitoring is explicitly described as "orthogonal" (EAct §5.2, line 3805). Absence of monitoring does not break safety or progress — it only limits *recovery* (the system detects failure but cannot automatically restart failed components).
> 
> - Plan 9's model has no supervision either. Process death is detected by the reader of its file descriptors (Twalk to a dead fid returns error; read on a dead pipe returns EOF). Recovery is external. pane follows this heritage.
> 
> **The risk:** Without actor-level monitoring, pane cannot implement Erlang/OTP-style supervision trees. If a pane crashes, its peers learn about it *only through their service relationships*. Any pane that doesn't have a direct service binding to the crashed pane learns nothing. For a windowing system this is likely fine — supervision is the compositor's job. For a distributed system, this gap becomes load-bearing. Flag it for Phase 3 (distribution) even if it's acceptable for Phase 1.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary Table
> 
> | Divergence | Verdict | Key Condition |
> |---|---|---|
> | E-CancelMsg → channel closure | **SAFE** | Message trait's Clone+Serialize bounds exclude capabilities from queues |
> | E-Spawn → OS-level | **SAFE** | Independence theorem (EAct 4.5): spawn order doesn't matter |
> | ibecome → not adopted | **SAFE** | Non-blocking sends eliminate primary need; residual gap is scheduling, not correctness |
> | E-Monitor → no supervision | **RISKY** | Session-scoped is strictly weaker; acceptable for N=1 windowing, load-bearing for distribution |</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>61665</total_tokens><tool_uses>35</tool_uses><duration_ms>242943</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> <summary>Agent "Vet EAct divergences (Plan 9 perspective)" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have everything. Let me write the full assessment.
