> ---
> 
> Here is my assessment of each divergence, grounded in Plan 9 kernel source.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 1: E-CancelMsg — Channel Closure vs. Explicit Drain
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE**
> 
> ### What Plan 9 actually did
> 
> Plan 9's `devmnt.c` did NOT explicitly drain pending T-messages when a mount channel died. Here's what actually happened:
> 
> **Normal close path (`mntclose` -&gt; `mntclunk`):** Sends a `Tclunk` to the server and frees the RPC. One clean operation per fid. This is the orderly case.
> 
> **Connection death path (`mountio` error cascade):** When `mntrpcread()` returns -1 (underlying channel read failed), the current reader-in-progress calls `error(Emountrpc)`. This triggers the `waserror()` handler in `mountio`, which:
> 
> 1. Calls `mntgate(m)` — clears `m-&gt;rip` and wakes exactly ONE queued RPC via `wakeup(&amp;q-&gt;r)` (the `break` after the first successful wakeup is critical)
> 2. Calls `mntflushfree(m, r)` for the *current* RPC only — marks it with `Rflush` reply type and removes it from the queue via `mntqrm`
> 3. Calls `nexterror()` to propagate the error to the caller
> 
> The newly woken RPC becomes the reader, attempts `mntrpcread`, also fails, also errors, also wakes one more. This is a **serial cascade**, not a bulk drain. Each pending RPC wakes, tries to read, fails, and propagates to the next. The `rpcattn` function checks only `r-&gt;done || r-&gt;m-&gt;rip == 0` — it has no concept of "channel is dead."
> 
> **The `muxclose` path** (invoked when the Mnt structure is freed) does iterate the queue and frees all pending `Mntrpc` entries — but this is resource cleanup, not application-level notification. The callers have already errored out or been abandoned by this point.
> 
> **Critical observation:** There is no bulk "wake all pending with error" mechanism. The `mntgate` function wakes *one* waiter and then `break`s. If the connection dies while N RPCs are pending, they cascade through failure one at a time. There's a real vulnerability here — the Plan 9 kernel source confirms that if a reader-in-progress encounters a dead channel, orphaned RPCs can potentially hang until process termination or timeout. The `m-&gt;id = 0` set in `muxclose` would cause `mntchk` to panic on subsequent operations, but that's a hard crash, not graceful cleanup.
> 
> ### Why pane's approach is safe
> 
> Pane's mpsc channel drop is actually *more* orderly than Plan 9's cascade:
> 
> 1. Channel drops -&gt; `recv()` returns `Err(RecvError)` -&gt; reader thread exits -&gt; `LooperCore::run()` sees channel closed at line 421
> 2. `run_destruction()` fires: `fail_connection(primary)` iterates ALL dispatch entries for the connection and fires `on_failed` callbacks
> 3. `dispatch.clear()` drops remaining entries
> 4. Handler is dropped, obligation handles fire Drop compensation (ReplyPort -&gt; ReplyFailed, etc.)
> 5. `exit_tx.send(reason)` notifies the server
> 
> This is a **bulk notification** — every pending request gets its failure callback. Plan 9's serial cascade is arguably worse because intermediate failures can interact unpredictably, and the one-at-a-time wakeup means latency compounds (N sequential read-fail-wake cycles vs. pane's single `fail_connection` pass).
> 
> The messages "in transit" that are discarded are mpsc channel contents that the looper never dequeued. These are unprocessed notifications and frames. EAct's "zapper thread" would drain them to invoke failure callbacks, but pane's `fail_connection` already handles the only messages that matter — pending request entries in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. Unprocessed notifications have no obligation to fulfill; discarding them is correct.
> 
> **Confidence: High.** Plan 9's own mechanism was less thorough than pane's.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 2: E-Spawn — Panes Are OS-Level, Not Handler-Spawned
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE**
> 
> ### What Plan 9 actually did
> 
> `exportfs` absolutely did spawn processes from within handlers. The `slave()` function (called from within request dispatch handlers like `Xread`, `Xwrite`, `Xopen`) calls `rfork(RFPROC|RFMEM)` to create worker processes when the pool is exhausted:
> 
> ```c
> pid = rfork(RFPROC|RFMEM);
> ```
> 
> New children execute `blockingslave()`, which enters a rendezvous loop. The Proclist maintains up to `MAXPROC = 50` worker processes. The main server loop dispatches synchronously via `fcalls[r-&gt;work.type]`, and blocking operations (file reads, directory walks) are handed off to slave processes via rendezvous.
> 
> More interestingly, when `exportfs` encounters a mount point (QTMOUNT), it spawns a *recursive exportfs instance* via `openmount()` — a new process tree for handling the nested filesystem. So yes, handlers could and did spawn sub-servers.
> 
> BUT — and this is the critical distinction — `exportfs` is a *userspace file server*, not a kernel handler. The kernel's `devmnt.c` (the mount device driver) does NOT spawn processes from within its handlers. Kernel device drivers in Plan 9 are single-threaded per-mount-point (the `m-&gt;rip` gate ensures exactly one reader at a time). The process spawning happens in userspace servers that can manage their own concurrency.
> 
> ### Why pane's restriction is correct
> 
> Pane's decision maps to the kernel-side model, not the userspace-server model, and that's the right call:
> 
> 1. **Pane handlers are analogous to kernel device methods**, not to exportfs. They run on the looper thread with `&amp;mut self` exclusivity (I6/I7). Spawning a new pane from within a handler would require either giving the child `&amp;mut H` access (violating exclusivity) or making it independent (which is what external spawning already does).
> 
> 2. **exportfs's rfork was for concurrency within a single server**, not for creating new top-level services. The slave processes shared memory (`RFMEM`) and served the same file tree. Pane achieves the same thing differently — the bridge spawns reader/writer threads (analogous to exportfs's slave pool), and the looper dispatches sequentially.
> 
> 3. **The rfork model has a known problem in exportfs**: the `fatal()` cleanup path must `postnote(PNPROC, m-&gt;pid, "kill")` every slave. If the parent dies without running `fatal()`, slaves become orphans. Pane avoids this entirely — OS process lifecycle is the pane's lifecycle, and the kernel reaps it.
> 
> 4. **EAct's handler-spawnable actors are Erlang-style lightweight processes.** Plan 9's `rfork` is not lightweight — it allocates kernel process state, segments, fid groups. Pane panes are OS processes. The cost model is entirely different. Spawning a pane from a handler would be like calling `fork()` from inside a kernel trap handler — possible, but you wouldn't design around it.
> 
> **Confidence: High.** The restriction is architecturally consistent with Plan 9's kernel-level model, and exportfs's process spawning is handled by pane's bridge threads.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 3: ibecome — No Session Switching
> 
> **Verdict: SAFE**
> 
> ### What Plan 9 actually did
> 
> `devmnt.c`'s `mountio` has a fascinating serialization mechanism that is the closest Plan 9 analog to session switching. The `m-&gt;rip` (reader-in-progress) gate:
> 
> ```c
> for(;;) {
>     lock(m);
>     if(m-&gt;rip == 0)
>         break;
>     unlock(m);
>     sleep(&amp;r-&gt;r, rpcattn, r);
>     if(r-&gt;done){
>         poperror();
>         mntflushfree(m, r);
>         return;
>     }
> }
> m-&gt;rip = up;
> ```
> 
> Multiple processes calling `mountio` on the same mount point are serialized: one becomes the reader (`m-&gt;rip = up`), and the others sleep on their individual rendezvous `r-&gt;r`, waking when either their RPC completes (`r-&gt;done`) or the reader slot opens (`m-&gt;rip == 0` via `rpcattn`). The reader reads replies for ALL pending RPCs, not just its own — `mountmux` matches by tag and wakes the appropriate waiter.
> 
> **Could a handler yield mid-operation?** No. `mountio` is a blocking call — the calling process either completes its RPC or errors out. There's no cooperative yield. If a 9P server needed to make a nested 9P call (e.g., exportfs forwarding a Tread to the underlying filesystem), it did so by blocking in `mountio` on the *inner* mount point. This worked because:
> 
> 1. Different mount points have independent `Mnt` structures with independent `m-&gt;rip` gates
> 2. The server ran multiple processes (slave pool), so one blocking on an inner mount didn't block the outer server's ability to handle other requests
> 3. But a single slave process was blocked for the duration — no yielding, no switching
> 
> **Re-entrancy concern:** If a single process tried to make a 9P call on mount A while already inside a handler for mount A, it would deadlock — the `m-&gt;rip` gate would block it, but it IS the reader. Plan 9 avoided this by construction: servers and clients used different mount points. `exportfs` received requests on stdin and forwarded them through the local namespace — different `Mnt` structures, no re-entrancy.
> 
> ### Why pane doesn't need ibecome
> 
> EAct's `ibecome` exists because in a single-threaded actor that blocks on a send in session A, you need to switch to session B to make progress (otherwise: deadlock). Pane's sends are non-blocking (`SyncSender::try_send` in `ServiceHandle::Drop`, `SyncSender::send` in the write path). The looper never blocks on a send — it enqueues and returns. `mountio`'s blocking send was a kernel design forced by synchronous 9P semantics; pane's session-typed channels with bounded mpsc queues decouple send from receive.
> 
> Plan 9's answer to the same problem was the slave pool in exportfs — parallelism via processes, not coroutine-style switching. Pane's answer is non-blocking sends plus the bridge's writer thread absorbing the transport write. Both work. EAct's `ibecome` is the Erlang answer (cooperative scheduling in a single-threaded runtime), which doesn't apply when you have kernel threads and non-blocking channels.
> 
> The one case to watch: if the bounded channel (`WRITE_CHANNEL_CAPACITY=128`) fills up, `SyncSender::send` blocks. This is the analog of `mountio` blocking on a write to a slow server. But pane treats this as backpressure, not deadlock — per the "per-pane threading isolation" policy, intra-pane blocking is backpressure, and each pane has its own thread. A full channel in pane A cannot deadlock pane B. Plan 9 had the same property: each mount point had an independent `Mnt`, so a slow server on mount A couldn't block operations on mount B.
> 
> **Confidence: High.** Non-blocking sends plus per-pane isolation give the same freedom as Plan 9's per-mount-point independence, without needing coroutine-style session switching.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Divergence 4: E-Monitor/E-InvokeM — No Supervision
> 
> **Verdict: RISKY (with clear mitigation path)**
> 
> ### What Plan 9 actually did
> 
> Plan 9 had exactly one process monitoring mechanism: `wait(2)` + `Waitmsg`. From `pexit()` in `proc.c`:
> 
> 1. When a process exits, it allocates a `Waitq` structure containing `{pid, user_time, sys_time, real_time, exit_message}`
> 2. It locks the parent's `exl`, decrements `p-&gt;nchild`, and links the `Waitq` into `p-&gt;waitq`
> 3. It calls `wakeup(&amp;p-&gt;waitr)` to unblock any `pwait()` call
> 4. The parent calls `pwait()`, which sleeps on `&amp;up-&gt;waitr` with predicate `haswaitq`, then dequeues and returns the `Waitmsg`
> 
> **The 128-child cap** is significant: `if(p-&gt;nwait &lt; 128)` — if a parent has more than 128 unreaped dead children, subsequent exit records are *silently dropped*. The comment says "This helps prevent badly written daemon processes from accumulating lots of wait records." Dead children beyond 128 simply vanish from the parent's perspective.
> 
> **The parent-must-be-alive check** is also important: `if(p-&gt;pid == up-&gt;parentpid &amp;&amp; p-&gt;state != Broken)` — if the parent has already exited or is broken, the `Waitq` is freed without delivery. Orphaned processes die silently.
> 
> **Note mechanism:** `postnote(PNPROC, pid, "kill")` can deliver a note to a specific process, but notes are *not* automatically sent on child death. `pexit()` does NOT call `postnote`. The parent must poll via `wait()` or block on it. There's no callback, no event, no supervisor notification.
> 
> **Notegroups** (`PNGROUP`) can broadcast a note to a process group, but this is for sending signals TO children, not for receiving notifications FROM them. Plan 9 had no equivalent of Erlang's monitors or EAct's E-Monitor.
> 
> ### Where pane is today
> 
> Pane currently has:
> - `ExitReason` enum (Graceful/Disconnected/Failed/InfraError) reported via `exit_tx` to the server
> - `process_disconnect` in ProtocolServer handles connection death
> - No mechanism for one pane to watch another pane's death
> 
> ### Why this is risky
> 
> Plan 9 got away without supervision because:
> 1. The process tree was shallow — most things were direct children
> 2. `wait()` was always available to the parent
> 3. The file server model meant connection death was the primary failure signal (EOF on the mount channel)
> 
> Pane has a different topology. Panes are peers, not parent-child. A pane that holds a `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` to another pane discovers death via channel closure and `on_failed` callbacks in `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`. That works for bilateral failure. But consider:
> 
> - **Pane A delegates work to pane B, which delegates to pane C.** C dies. B discovers via channel closure. A only discovers if B propagates the failure. There's no structural guarantee that B does so — it depends on B's handler logic.
> - **A compositor manages 50 panes.** One crashes. The compositor discovers via `process_disconnect`, which is the EOF-on-channel analog. This works, and it's how Plan 9's file servers worked (EOF on the client's mount channel).
> 
> The gap is specifically: **non-parent, non-connected observers** who care about a pane's death. Plan 9's `/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/wait` file let any process with read permission observe wait events. Pane has no equivalent. If pane A needs to know pane C died but has no direct connection to C, it has no mechanism.
> 
> ### Mitigation
> 
> This is solvable within pane's existing architecture without adopting EAct's monitor primitive:
> 
> 1. **Connection-based death discovery already works** — `process_disconnect` + `fail_connection` + `on_failed` callbacks. This covers the bilateral case (Plan 9's EOF-on-mount-channel pattern).
> 2. **pane-fs `/pane/&lt;id&gt;/event`** (planned but unimplemented) would provide the Plan 9 `/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/wait` analog — a blocking-read file that emits when a pane exits. Any process can observe.
> 3. **ServiceTeardown** already propagates service death to consumers. The S1 fix (`fail_session`) handles the dispatch-entry cleanup.
> 
> What's missing is *proactive notification to non-connected watchers*. The planned `ExitBroadcaster` (mentioned in the EAct analysis, Gap 4) would handle this. Until it exists, the risk is contained: bilateral failure works, only third-party observers are blind.
> 
> **Confidence: Moderate.** The bilateral case is solid. The third-party observer case needs `pane-fs event` or `ExitBroadcaster` to close. Not urgent for Phase 1 (single-server, limited topology), but load-bearing for Phase 2 multi-server.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary Table
> 
> | Divergence | Verdict | Plan 9 Analog | Risk |
> |---|---|---|---|
> | E-CancelMsg (channel drop vs. drain) | **SAFE** | devmnt serial cascade is *worse* than pane's bulk `fail_connection` | None — pane is more thorough |
> | E-Spawn (OS-level panes) | **SAFE** | Kernel handlers don't spawn; exportfs spawns slaves (= bridge threads) | None — correct architectural layer |
> | ibecome (no session switching) | **SAFE** | mountio blocks, doesn't yield; exportfs uses process pool | None — non-blocking sends eliminate the need |
> | E-Monitor (no supervision) | **RISKY** | wait(2) + Waitmsg; no monitors; 128-child cap silently drops | Bilateral death works; third-party observers need pane-fs event file |
> 
> The three safety theorems from the formal audit hold regardless of these divergences. Divergences 1-3 are validated by Plan 9 precedent — Plan 9 either did the same thing or something worse. Divergence 4 is a real gap, but bounded: it only matters for non-connected observers, and the mitigation path (pane-fs event file + ExitBroadcaster) is already identified in the architecture.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Design E-Monitor fix for pane" completed</summary>
> <result>The analysis is above. The key decisions for Lane to weigh:
> 
> 1. **watch_id assignment:** I proposed client-assigned (like 9P tags). Server-assigned (like session_id) gives the server rate-limiting control. Client-assigned is simpler and avoids a round-trip for ID allocation.
> 
> 2. **Goodbye reliability:** If the pane panics hard enough that catch_unwind doesn't fire (double panic, abort), no Goodbye is sent. The server falls back to `Disconnected`. This is fine — the alternative (requiring Goodbye for correctness) would make the system fragile.
> 
> 3. **ExitReason moving to pane-proto:** Currently ExitReason lives in pane-app. For PaneExited to carry it on the wire, it needs Serialize/Deserialize and should probably live in pane-proto. This is a small refactor but it changes crate boundaries.</result>

### `2026-04-07T02:55:11.749Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ab113fe5a18316116</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01AgzFYdM9mvngCdVX3neDXu</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/ab113fe5a18316116.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Design E-Monitor fix for pane" completed</summary>
> <result>This is the key newsletter article. Now I have everything I need. Let me write the design analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's the design for pane death notification, grounded in what BeOS actually did and where pane needs to diverge.
> 
> ## 1. How BeOS Handled This
> 
> BeOS had **two separate observer/watching mechanisms**, operating at different granularities:
> 
> ### BRoster::StartWatching (app-level death notification)
> 
> The registrar (a system server) maintained a `WatchingService` -- essentially a `map&lt;BMessenger, Watcher*&gt;` keyed by the watcher's messenger target. When an app died, `TRoster::RemoveApp()` called `_AppRemoved()`, which constructed a `B_SOME_APP_QUIT` message containing the dead app's signature, team_id, thread_id, flags, and entry_ref, then broadcast it through `fWatchingService.NotifyWatchers()` with an `EventMaskWatcherFilter(B_REQUEST_QUIT)` filter.
> 
> Key file: `src/servers/registrar/TRoster.cpp:1523-1536`. The watching info (`_AddMessageWatchingInfo`, line 1624) attached identity fields -- it told you *who* died and let you figure out *what that means to you*.
> 
> The stale-watcher cleanup was elegant: `WatchingService::NotifyWatchers()` (line 204-228) tried to send to each watcher, and if the send failed and the target was invalid, it collected the watcher into a `staleWatchers` list and removed them after the iteration. Dead watchers cleaned themselves up as a side effect of notification attempts.
> 
> ### BHandler::StartWatching (per-handler observer pattern)
> 
> This was a different mechanism entirely, added in R4. `BHandler` maintained a per-instance `ObserverList` (`fObserverList`) that stored both in-process `BHandler*` observers and cross-process `BMessenger` observers, indexed by a `uint32 what` code. `SendNotices(what, notice)` broadcast to all registered observers for that `what` code plus the wildcard `B_OBSERVER_OBSERVE_ALL`.
> 
> Key file: `src/kits/app/Handler.cpp:528-611, 694-866`. The inter-process path (BMessenger overload, line 528) was asynchronous: `StartWatching` sent a `kMsgStartObserving` message *to the target*, which the target's looper handled by adding the observer to the target's ObserverList. The in-process path (BHandler* overload, line 564) was direct: just added the handler pointer to the local ObserverList.
> 
> **Neither mechanism did per-looper death monitoring.** BRoster watched apps. BHandler::StartWatching was for arbitrary state-change notifications, not death. If you wanted to know when a specific BLooper died, you had to either watch the app (BRoster), poll with BMessenger::IsValid(), or rely on your pending messages failing.
> 
> This was a genuine gap in BeOS. The architecture doc's planned `pane_exited(pane, reason)` correctly identifies the need.
> 
> ## 2. Where Monitoring State Should Live: ProtocolServer
> 
> The ProtocolServer is the right place. Here's why:
> 
> - It already knows when connections die (`ServerEvent::Disconnected`)
> - It already has the identity of every connection (`ConnectionId`, Hello provides)
> - It's the single-threaded actor that owns all routing state -- adding a watcher table is natural
> - It's the pane equivalent of the registrar
> 
> **Not** in LooperCore, and **not** as a new service protocol. Death monitoring is infrastructure, not an application-level service. In BeOS terms, this was registrar functionality, not kit functionality. The watcher registers interest with the server and the server notifies when a connection drops.
> 
> Two new ControlMessage variants, plus a watcher table in `ServerState`.
> 
> ## 3. What the Watcher Receives
> 
> The watcher receives a `ControlMessage::PaneExited` containing the dead pane's `Address` and an `ExitDisposition`. Not the full `ExitReason` -- as the architecture spec says, "failure reason is private to the process." The disposition is the external view.
> 
> ## 4. Registration: Dynamic, via ControlMessage
> 
> Dynamic registration, not PaneBuilder-time. You need to be able to start and stop watching at any point during the active phase. This mirrors BRoster::StartWatching, which could be called at any time.
> 
> PaneBuilder could offer a convenience method for common cases (watch-from-startup), but the underlying mechanism must be dynamic because:
> - You might only want to watch a pane after you discover it through a service
> - You might stop watching when you no longer care
> - Phase 2 distribution means the watched pane might be on another server -- registration must go through the wire protocol
> 
> ## 5. Watcher-Dies-While-Watching
> 
> Follow the WatchingService pattern exactly: when the server tries to deliver `PaneExited` to a watcher and the watcher's connection is gone, remove the watcher from the table. Dead watchers are cleaned up as a side effect of notification delivery. This is the right design -- it's lazy cleanup that avoids the need for a separate liveness-checking mechanism.
> 
> In pane's case this is even simpler: if the watcher's ConnectionId is no longer in `writers`, skip the notification and remove the watch entry.
> 
> ## 6. Concrete Rust Types
> 
> ```rust
> // -- In pane-proto/src/control.rs --
> 
> /// Why a pane exited, as visible to external observers.
> /// Stripped of internal details — failure specifics are
> /// private to the process.
> ///
> /// Design heritage: BeOS B_SOME_APP_QUIT carried identity
> /// fields (signature, team, thread, flags, entry_ref) but
> /// no reason — the watcher inferred cause from context.
> /// pane carries a disposition tag because connection loss
> /// and graceful exit have different implications for the
> /// watcher (retry vs. don't retry).
> #[derive(Debug, Clone, Serialize, Deserialize, PartialEq, Eq)]
> #[non_exhaustive]
> pub enum ExitDisposition {
>     /// Handler returned Flow::Stop voluntarily.
>     Graceful,
>     /// Connection to server lost (network, crash, kill -9).
>     Disconnected,
>     /// Handler panicked (caught by catch_unwind).
>     Failed,
> }
> 
> // -- New ControlMessage variants --
> 
> pub enum ControlMessage {
>     // ... existing 7 variants ...
> 
>     /// Register to receive PaneExited when the target pane exits.
>     /// Server-mediated: the server maintains the watch table.
>     ///
>     /// The target is identified by Address. If the target is
>     /// already dead at registration time, the server sends
>     /// PaneExited immediately (no race window).
>     MonitorPane {
>         /// Address of the pane to watch.
>         target: Address,
>     },
> 
>     /// Unregister a previously registered watch.
>     UnmonitorPane {
>         /// Address of the pane to stop watching.
>         target: Address,
>     },
> 
>     /// Notification that a monitored pane has exited.
>     /// Delivered to the watcher's control channel (service 0).
>     ///
>     /// Design heritage: BeOS TRoster::_AppRemoved sent
>     /// B_SOME_APP_QUIT with identity fields through
>     /// WatchingService::NotifyWatchers(). pane's PaneExited
>     /// carries Address (identity) + ExitDisposition (cause).
>     PaneExited {
>         /// Address of the pane that exited.
>         pane: Address,
>         /// Why it exited (external view, no error details).
>         disposition: ExitDisposition,
>     },
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Server-Side State
> 
> ```rust
> // -- In pane-session/src/server.rs, added to ServerState --
> 
> /// Who is watching whom. Keyed by the watched pane's Address.
> /// Value is the set of ConnectionIds that want notification.
> ///
> /// Design heritage: Haiku's WatchingService used
> /// map&lt;BMessenger, Watcher*&gt; keyed by watcher identity.
> /// pane inverts this: keyed by *watched* identity, because
> /// the hot path is "pane X died, who cares?" not "watcher Y
> /// is leaving, what was it watching?" The latter (cleanup on
> /// watcher disconnect) iterates the whole table, but that's
> /// the cold path.
> watch_table: HashMap&lt;Address, HashSet&lt;ConnectionId&gt;&gt;,
> 
> /// Reverse index: ConnectionId → set of Addresses it watches.
> /// For efficient cleanup when the watcher disconnects.
> watcher_index: HashMap&lt;ConnectionId, HashSet&lt;Address&gt;&gt;,
> ```
> 
> ### Server-Side Logic
> 
> In `process_control`, handle the new variants:
> 
> ```rust
> ControlMessage::MonitorPane { target } =&gt; {
>     // Check if target is already dead (no connection for
>     // that address). If so, send PaneExited immediately.
>     if !self.is_address_live(&amp;target) {
>         let exited = ControlMessage::PaneExited {
>             pane: target,
>             disposition: ExitDisposition::Disconnected,
>         };
>         // send to conn_id ...
>     } else {
>         self.watch_table
>             .entry(target)
>             .or_default()
>             .insert(conn_id);
>         self.watcher_index
>             .entry(conn_id)
>             .or_default()
>             .insert(target);
>     }
> }
> 
> ControlMessage::UnmonitorPane { target } =&gt; {
>     if let Some(watchers) = self.watch_table.get_mut(&amp;target) {
>         watchers.remove(&amp;conn_id);
>         if watchers.is_empty() {
>             self.watch_table.remove(&amp;target);
>         }
>     }
>     if let Some(watched) = self.watcher_index.get_mut(&amp;conn_id) {
>         watched.remove(&amp;target);
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> In `process_disconnect`, after the existing ServiceTeardown loop:
> 
> ```rust
> fn process_disconnect(&amp;mut self, conn_id: ConnectionId) {
>     // ... existing route cleanup and ServiceTeardown ...
> 
>     // Determine the Address for this connection.
>     // (Requires ServerState to track conn_id → Address mapping,
>     // populated during NewConnection from Hello.)
>     if let Some(address) = self.conn_addresses.get(&amp;conn_id).cloned() {
>         // Notify watchers of this pane's death.
>         if let Some(watchers) = self.watch_table.remove(&amp;address) {
>             let exited = ControlMessage::PaneExited {
>                 pane: address,
>                 disposition: self.exit_disposition_for(conn_id),
>             };
>             if let Ok(bytes) = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;exited) {
>                 for watcher_conn in &amp;watchers {
>                     if let Some(wh) = self.writers.get(watcher_conn) {
>                         let _ = wh.write_frame(0, &amp;bytes);
>                     }
>                     // Clean up reverse index
>                     if let Some(watched) =
>                         self.watcher_index.get_mut(watcher_conn)
>                     {
>                         watched.remove(&amp;address);
>                     }
>                 }
>             }
>         }
>     }
> 
>     // Clean up any watches THIS connection had on others.
>     if let Some(watched_set) = self.watcher_index.remove(&amp;conn_id) {
>         for watched_addr in watched_set {
>             if let Some(watchers) =
>                 self.watch_table.get_mut(&amp;watched_addr)
>             {
>                 watchers.remove(&amp;conn_id);
>                 if watchers.is_empty() {
>                     self.watch_table.remove(&amp;watched_addr);
>                 }
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Client-Side (LooperCore) Dispatch
> 
> `LooperCore::dispatch_control` adds a case for `PaneExited`:
> 
> ```rust
> ControlMessage::PaneExited { pane, disposition } =&gt; {
>     self.dispatch(|h| h.pane_exited(pane, disposition))
> }
> ```
> 
> Which means `Handler` gets the planned method:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handler: Send + 'static {
>     // ... existing methods ...
> 
>     /// A monitored pane has exited.
>     ///
>     /// Default: Flow::Continue (ignore).
>     fn pane_exited(&amp;mut self, _pane: Address, _disposition: ExitDisposition) -&gt; Flow {
>         Flow::Continue
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ## Key Design Decisions and Rationale
> 
> **ExitDisposition vs ExitReason.** ExitReason is looper-internal (4 variants including InfraError). ExitDisposition is the external view (3 variants). InfraError is folded into Disconnected for external observers -- the watcher doesn't need to distinguish "socket broke" from "process killed." The exit_tx mechanism already sends ExitReason to the server; the server maps it to ExitDisposition before broadcasting.
> 
> **Address as identity, not ConnectionId.** Watchers register by Address (which is meaningful across connections in Phase 2), not ConnectionId (which is a server-internal opaque handle). This means the server needs a `conn_addresses: HashMap&lt;ConnectionId, Address&gt;` mapping, populated from Hello during connection setup. This is trivially derivable from Hello but doesn't exist yet.
> 
> **MonitorPane-when-already-dead sends immediately.** This eliminates the race where you learn about a pane through a service, try to monitor it, but it died between those two events. BeOS had this race -- if you called BRoster::StartWatching after an app quit, you simply never got the notification. The "send immediately if dead" behavior requires the server to remember recently-dead addresses (a bounded set with LRU eviction, or simply check whether the address has any live connection).
> 
> **No event mask.** BeOS's `watching_request_flags` (B_REQUEST_LAUNCHED | B_REQUEST_QUIT | B_REQUEST_ACTIVATED) made sense for a roster with multiple event types. Pane's MonitorPane is death-only. If we later want launch/activate notifications, we add separate ControlMessage variants rather than a bitmask -- the type system is a better discriminator than runtime flags.
> 
> **Phase 2 compatibility.** When distribution arrives, MonitorPane for a remote pane routes through the server mesh. The local server sends MonitorPane to the remote server, which adds the watch entry. PaneExited flows back along the same path. The Address-based identity and ControlMessage-based wire protocol handle this without structural changes.
> 
> ## What I'd Flag as Open Questions for Lane
> 
> 1. **Recently-dead tracking.** The "already dead at registration" check requires either (a) the server remembers all addresses it's ever seen (unbounded), (b) a bounded recent-deaths ring buffer, or (c) just returning "unknown address" and letting the watcher figure it out. Option (b) is the right one but needs a size decision.
> 
> 2. **ExitDisposition derivation.** The server currently gets an `ExitReason` from exit_tx. We need to decide: does the server even need ExitReason, or should the looper send ExitDisposition directly? The looper knows the internal reason, does the mapping, sends the external form. Simpler than having the server understand ExitReason.
> 
> 3. **Naming: Monitor vs Watch.** BeOS used "watching" (StartWatching/StopWatching). Erlang/OTP uses "monitor/demonitor." I lean toward Monitor because (a) we're not building supervision trees, so Erlang semantics are closer -- monitor is one-way, watch was bidirectional in BeOS (the watched handler stored the observer list), and (b) it avoids confusion with the existing BHandler observer pattern (StartWatching/SendNotices) which pane maps to the filesystem attribute observer pattern per `pane/observer_pattern_decision`.</result>

> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/ad474c81a0fb90115.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Design E-Monitor fix for pane" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I now have a thorough understanding of the codebase, the heritage systems, the theoretical foundations, and all the relevant design decisions. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is my analysis of the six questions. I'll work through the categorical structure first, then address the design questions with concrete options.
> 
> ## 1. Categorical structure: what IS death notification?
> 
> Death notification is **not** a comonadic observation in any precise sense. Let me distinguish three things that get conflated:
> 
> **Attribute observation** (what the observer pattern decision covers) is genuinely a co-Kleisli extraction: `view : S → A`, demand-driven, pure, computed on each read. The state `S` plays the role of a comonadic environment — you extract from it without modifying it. This is your AttrReader/MonadicLens view path.
> 
> **Death notification** is categorically different. It is a **one-shot event** carrying terminal information. The monitored pane transitions from alive to dead — this is not a repeated extraction from a persistent state, it's a colimit (terminal object) in the lifecycle category. Once a pane exits, there is no more state to observe.
> 
> The precise structure is: death notification is a **coalgebraic prism** (Remark 4.8 in the profunctor optics paper, `arxivmain.tex:1363-1370`). The "match" function captures failure into a comonad:
> 
> ```
> cMatch : PaneRef → W(Dead) + Alive
> ```
> 
> where `W` is the comonad carrying the exit context (ExitReason, Address). When the match succeeds (the pane is alive), there's nothing to extract. When it fails (the pane died), the failure itself carries structured information wrapped in the comonadic context.
> 
> But this is the *type-theoretic* structure, not a design prescription. Operationally, what you need is simpler: a **continuation** registered against a liveness predicate. When the predicate becomes false, the continuation fires once with the terminal value. This is closer to a **one-shot callback** (like ReplyPort's Drop compensation) than to an optic.
> 
> **Bottom line:** The optics audit was right that monitoring is *observational* (read-only — the monitor doesn't modify the target). But calling it "comonadic extraction" implies repeated demand-driven reads, which is wrong for death. Death is a one-shot push event.
> 
> ## 2. Filesystem attrs vs. messaging: does monitoring follow the observer pattern decision?
> 
> No. The observer pattern decision specifically scoped itself:
> 
> &gt; **Where filesystem doesn't work:**
> &gt; - Transient events ("user clicked") → point-to-point Messenger::send_message, already handled
> 
> Death notification is a **transient event par excellence** — it happens once, it's urgent, and it has a specific audience (the monitors, not all readers of the filesystem). The reasons the filesystem decision was right for attributes don't apply:
> 
> - **Initial-value problem** — irrelevant. You don't "read the current death status and then subscribe." You register interest in a future event.
> - **Crash recovery** — the *persistence* of death is already handled by the pane disappearing from `/pane/`. But the *notification* must be push-based because the monitor needs to react, not poll.
> - **Scriptability** — scripts that need to wait for pane death can use `pane-notify watch /pane/42/` and detect directory disappearance. This works without a special death notification channel.
> 
> However, there is a filesystem *complement* that should exist regardless: when a pane dies, `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/` disappears (or a `status` file transitions). A script doing `pane-notify watch /pane/42/` gets the inotify/kqueue deletion event. This is the filesystem leg of death — it happens naturally from the namespace semantics.
> 
> The messaging leg — delivering `PaneExited { address, reason }` to specific registered monitors — is the mechanism that handlers need. The architecture doc already specifies this at line 153: `pane_exited(pane, reason) — PaneExited broadcast`.
> 
> **Recommendation:** Monitoring uses messaging (push), not filesystem observation (pull). The filesystem leg happens automatically (directory disappears). These are complementary, not competing.
> 
> ## 3. Polarity: positive value or negative obligation?
> 
> The notification itself — `PaneExited { address: Address, reason: ExitReason }` — is unambiguously **positive**. It is:
> - Clone (it's data, no continuation)
> - Serialize (it crosses wire boundaries — the server broadcasts it)
> - Send
> - Fully evaluated before delivery
> 
> This follows from the polarity tables (serena `pane/polarity_classifications`): all wire types are positive, all ServiceFrame variants are positive. PaneExited is a wire type.
> 
> The **registration** is the interesting part. There are two options:
> 
> **Option A: MonitorHandle as obligation handle (negative/linear).**
> ```rust
> #[must_use = "dropping a MonitorHandle cancels monitoring"]
> pub struct MonitorHandle {
>     // internal: server-side registration token
> }
> 
> impl Drop for MonitorHandle {
>     fn drop(&amp;mut self) {
>         // Send StopMonitoring to server
>     }
> }
> ```
> This follows the existing obligation handle pattern (ServiceHandle, TimerToken). Drop = cancellation. !Clone, !Send (looper-local). The monitor registration is a **negative obligation** — you must either hold it or explicitly drop it.
> 
> **Option B: Registration is fire-and-forget (positive).**
> Like Be's `BRoster::StartWatching(BMessenger target, uint32 eventMask)` — you register and forget. To stop, you call `StopWatching` explicitly. No obligation handle.
> 
> **I recommend Option A.** It matches every other pane obligation handle. It gives you automatic cleanup (handler drops → MonitorHandle drops → monitoring cancelled → server doesn't waste effort notifying a dead pane about other dead panes). It follows ghost state discipline: the MonitorHandle *is* the proof of registration, not a correlation token you hope matches.
> 
> ## 4. Where does monitoring state live?
> 
> Three candidates, each with different consequences:
> 
> **Option A: Server-side registry.**
> The server (ProtocolServer / pane-server) maintains a `HashMap&lt;PaneId, Vec&lt;MonitorRegistration&gt;&gt;` mapping each monitored pane to its list of monitors. When a pane's exit_tx fires, the server looks up monitors and delivers PaneExited to each.
> 
> - Pro: Centralized, correct even if the monitored pane crashes (the server detects exit via fd hangup / exit_tx, not via the pane cooperating)
> - Pro: Matches Be's model (registrar_server held the watch list)
> - Pro: Matches architecture doc step 4: "Server notified via PaneExited { reason }"
> - Con: Requires server-side state management, server protocol extension
> 
> **Option B: Handler-side (per-monitor).**
> Each monitoring handler stores its MonitorHandle. The monitored pane's exit triggers delivery through the existing looper channel.
> 
> - Pro: Simpler — no new server state
> - Con: Who delivers the notification? If the monitored pane is dead, it can't deliver its own death notice. You need a third party (the server) anyway.
> 
> **Option C: Filesystem-only.**
> No explicit registration. Monitor via `pane-notify watch /pane/42/`. Death = directory disappears.
> 
> - Pro: No new mechanism
> - Con: No ExitReason (just "gone"), no selective monitoring, polling semantics
> 
> **I strongly recommend Option A.** Option B doesn't work without the server anyway (dead panes can't self-notify). Option C loses ExitReason and is pull-based. The server is the only entity that both (a) knows when a pane dies and (b) survives the death.
> 
> ## 5. Composition with ServiceDispatch
> 
> Two sub-options for how the server delivers death notifications to the monitoring pane's looper:
> 
> **Option A: Death notification as a ControlMessage variant.**
> Add `PaneExited { address: Address, reason: ExitReason }` to the ControlMessage enum. The server sends it on service 0 (the control channel). The looper's main loop handles it alongside Lifecycle and ServiceTeardown.
> 
> ```rust
> // In ControlMessage enum:
> PaneExited {
>     address: Address,
>     reason: ExitReason,
> },
> ```
> 
> The looper dispatches it to `handler.pane_exited(address, reason)` — the method already planned in architecture.md line 153.
> 
> - Pro: No new channel, no new protocol. Uses existing framing.
> - Pro: Control messages are already framework-internal, not developer-visible wire protocol.
> - Pro: Sequential dispatch — pane_exited runs on the looper thread with `&amp;mut self`, same as all other callbacks.
> - Con: Expands ControlMessage, which is supposed to be connection-management, not application events.
> 
> **Option B: Monitoring as a dedicated Protocol.**
> Define a `Monitoring` protocol with `ServiceId("com.pane.monitoring")`. The handler does `builder.open_service::&lt;Monitoring&gt;()` and implements `Handles&lt;Monitoring&gt;`. PaneExited is `Monitoring::Message`.
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct Monitoring;
> impl Protocol for Monitoring {
>     fn service_id() -&gt; ServiceId { /* com.pane.monitoring */ }
>     type Message = PaneExited;
> }
> ```
> 
> - Pro: Clean separation — monitoring is a service like any other
> - Pro: Optional — only panes that need monitoring open the service
> - Con: Heavier — requires DeclareInterest negotiation for a framework capability
> - Con: PaneExited doesn't need protocol-level flexibility (no versioning, no message variants beyond PaneExited)
> 
> **I recommend Option A.** Death notification is infrastructure, not an application protocol. It belongs alongside ServiceTeardown in the control layer. Making it a Protocol adds ceremony (DeclareInterest, session_id, service dispatch table entry) for something that's a simple server → pane push.
> 
> The `monitor(address)` method on Messenger (not on a ServiceHandle) sends a registration request to the server via the control channel. The server responds with monitoring state keyed by the requesting pane's connection.
> 
> ## 6. Concrete types
> 
> Here's what I'd propose. Two design options for Lane to choose between on the registration side, but the notification type and server-side machinery are the same in both.
> 
> ### The notification (positive, Cartesian)
> 
> ```rust
> /// A pane exited. Delivered by the server to registered monitors.
> /// Positive: Clone, Serialize, Send. No continuation, no obligation.
> ///
> /// Design heritage: BeOS B_SOME_APP_QUIT delivered via BRoster
> /// with team_id and app signature
> /// (headers/os/app/Roster.h:49, B_SOME_APP_QUIT = 'BRAQ').
> /// Plan 9: process exit visible as /proc/&lt;pid&gt; disappearing;
> /// waitpid(2) delivers exit status to parent only.
> #[derive(Debug, Clone, PartialEq, Eq, Serialize, Deserialize)]
> pub struct PaneExited {
>     /// Who died.
>     pub address: Address,
>     /// How they died. Stripped of internal details.
>     pub reason: ExitReason,
> }
> ```
> 
> ExitReason already exists and is `Clone + PartialEq + Eq`. It needs `Serialize + Deserialize` added since it will cross the wire.
> 
> ### The control messages
> 
> ```rust
> // Added to ControlMessage enum:
> 
> /// Request monitoring of a target pane. Server registers the
> /// requesting connection as a monitor. If the target pane is
> /// already dead, the server immediately sends PaneExited.
> ///
> /// The token is a monitor-local identifier for cancellation.
> /// Ghost state: the MonitorHandle on the handler side owns
> /// this token — it is not exposed to the developer.
> Monitor {
>     target: Address,
>     token: u64,
> },
> 
> /// Cancel a monitoring registration.
> StopMonitoring {
>     token: u64,
> },
> 
> /// Server → client: a monitored pane exited.
> PaneExited {
>     token: u64,
>     address: Address,
>     reason: ExitReason,
> },
> ```
> 
> ### The handler API
> 
> ```rust
> // On Handler trait (planned addition):
> fn pane_exited(&amp;mut self, address: Address, reason: ExitReason) -&gt; Flow {
>     Flow::Continue
> }
> ```
> 
> ### The registration handle (Option A — MonitorHandle)
> 
> ```rust
> /// Proof of monitoring registration. Drop cancels.
> ///
> /// Positive in the Kleisli sense: it's data the handler holds.
> /// But it carries a negative obligation: Drop sends StopMonitoring.
> /// Same pattern as TimerToken.
> ///
> /// NOT Clone, NOT Send (looper-local). The handler stores it in
> /// its own fields, drops it when it no longer cares.
> #[must_use = "dropping a MonitorHandle cancels monitoring"]
> pub struct MonitorHandle {
>     target: Address,
>     token: u64,
>     write_tx: std::sync::mpsc::SyncSender&lt;(u16, Vec&lt;u8&gt;)&gt;,
> }
> 
> impl Drop for MonitorHandle {
>     fn drop(&amp;mut self) {
>         let msg = ControlMessage::StopMonitoring { token: self.token };
>         if let Ok(bytes) = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg) {
>             let _ = self.write_tx.try_send((0, bytes));
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Registration on Messenger
> 
> ```rust
> impl Messenger {
>     /// Monitor a pane for death. Returns a handle whose Drop
>     /// cancels the monitoring. If the target is already dead,
>     /// pane_exited fires on the next dispatch cycle.
>     ///
>     /// Design heritage: BRoster::StartWatching(target, B_REQUEST_QUIT)
>     /// (src/kits/app/Roster.cpp:836-857). pane differs: MonitorHandle
>     /// replaces the explicit StopWatching call with Drop semantics.
>     pub fn monitor(&amp;self, target: Address) -&gt; MonitorHandle {
>         let token = self.next_monitor_token();
>         let msg = ControlMessage::Monitor { target, token };
>         // Send via control channel
>         let bytes = postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg).expect("Monitor serialization");
>         let _ = self.write_tx.try_send((0, bytes));
>         MonitorHandle {
>             target,
>             token,
>             write_tx: self.write_tx.clone(),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Server-side state
> 
> ```rust
> /// Server-side monitoring registry.
> /// Keyed by monitored pane → list of monitors.
> struct MonitorRegistry {
>     /// For each monitored pane, who's watching and their callback token.
>     watchers: HashMap&lt;Address, Vec&lt;MonitorEntry&gt;&gt;,
> }
> 
> struct MonitorEntry {
>     /// The connection to deliver PaneExited on.
>     watcher_connection: ConnectionId,
>     /// The monitor-side token for routing to the right MonitorHandle.
>     token: u64,
> }
> ```
> 
> When the server receives `exit_tx.send(reason)` from a dying pane (destruction sequence step 4), it:
> 1. Looks up all MonitorEntry values for that pane's Address
> 2. Sends `ControlMessage::PaneExited { token, address, reason }` to each watcher's connection
> 3. Removes the registry entries
> 
> When the server receives `ControlMessage::Monitor { target, token }` and the target is already gone, it immediately sends PaneExited back — solving the "subscribe after death" race.
> 
> ### LooperCore dispatch
> 
> ```rust
> // In LooperCore::run(), inside the ControlMessage match:
> Ok(LooperMessage::Control(ControlMessage::PaneExited {
>     token: _,
>     address,
>     reason,
> })) =&gt; {
>     let outcome = self.dispatch(|h| h.pane_exited(address, reason));
>     if let DispatchOutcome::Exit(reason) = outcome {
>         break reason;
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The token is consumed by the looper to look up the corresponding MonitorHandle (confirming it hasn't been dropped/cancelled). If the MonitorHandle was already dropped (cancelled), the PaneExited is silently ignored.
> 
> ### Polarity summary
> 
> | Component | Polarity | Clone | Serialize | Rationale |
> |-----------|----------|-------|-----------|-----------|
> | PaneExited | Positive | Yes | Yes | Wire data, no continuation |
> | ExitReason | Positive | Yes | Yes (new) | Wire data |
> | MonitorHandle | ↑(negative) | No | No | Obligation: Drop = cancel |
> | ControlMessage::Monitor | Positive | Yes | Yes | Wire command |
> | ControlMessage::PaneExited | Positive | Yes | Yes | Wire delivery |
> 
> ### Boundary rule compliance
> 
> - **R1** (no Attribute in Message): Satisfied — PaneExited contains Address + ExitReason, no optics.
> - **R3** (obligations are !Message): MonitorHandle is !Clone, !Serialize, never enters a Message.
> - **R4** (receive doesn't touch optics): pane_exited receives positive data, no lens/attr access.
> - **R8** (ServiceHandle in handler not optic layer): MonitorHandle same — handler-local, not namespace-visible.
> - **R9** (no Arc-Mutex-Option smuggling): MonitorHandle is a plain field on the handler, not wrapped.
> 
> ### What the filesystem sees
> 
> When a pane dies, `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/` disappears from the FUSE namespace. Scripts using `pane-notify watch /pane/42/` get a deletion event. No ExitReason in the filesystem path — that's private. If you want the reason programmatically, you register a monitor. The filesystem gives you "gone"; the monitor gives you "gone + why."
> 
> This maintains the observer pattern decision's spirit: **filesystem for state observation** (is the pane there?), **messaging for events** (the pane just died, here's why).
> 
> ---
> 
> **Open question for Lane:** The architecture doc lists `pane_exited(pane, reason)` as a planned Handler method. Should this be a Handler method (all panes get it, default no-op) or should it only fire for panes that explicitly called `messenger.monitor(target)`? I've designed it as the latter (registration-based), but the architecture doc's wording "PaneExited broadcast" could mean either. Be's B_SOME_APP_QUIT was broadcast to all registered watchers via BRoster, not to all apps — so registration-based matches the heritage.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T02:55:11.760Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8560a6b436357717</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01XwNiiwJwFkFrCgxTWigWE5</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/500553ad-7f1c-451d-86f9-ea0f6cff621b/tasks/a8560a6b436357717.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Design E-Monitor fix for pane" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have complete context. Let me provide the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Verdict: Conditionally Sound
> 
> The design is sound under three runtime invariants and two design choices. E-Monitor/E-InvokeM are provably orthogonal to Preservation/Progress/Global Progress in EAct (Fowler/Hu, Theorem 4.7 and Corollary 4.8 in the extended version) -- adding monitoring does not introduce new stuck states. The practical question is whether pane's Rust encoding preserves this orthogonality. It does, with conditions.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Q1: Obligation Handle vs. Fire-and-Forget
> 
> **The watch callback must NOT be an obligation handle.** It must be a regular notification (fire-and-forget).
> 
> **Argument from EAct's typing rule.** T-Monitor (line 3204-3209 in eventactors-extended.tex) types the callback `v` as:
> 
> ```
> v : C -&gt;{end,end} C
> ```
> 
> That is, a function from the handler's state type C to C, with session pre/postcondition both `end` -- the callback neither consumes nor produces session resources. It is a pure state-transition function. The monitor callback in EAct is not a session endpoint and carries no linear obligation.
> 
> If you made the watch callback an obligation handle (like ReplyPort -- must-use, Drop-fires-failure), you would create a structural problem: **when does the obligation discharge?** The watched pane might live forever. An undischarged obligation sitting in the watcher's handler state is ghost state (per `pane/ghost_state_discipline`) that the type system can't enforce. You'd have to either:
> 
> - Force watchers to explicitly un-watch (adding a new obligation to discharge the obligation -- turtles), or
> - Accept that Drop of the watch handle fires a "cancel watch" message that serves no semantic purpose (the watcher doesn't care that it stopped watching a live pane).
> 
> Neither is useful. The obligation pattern is for exactly-once interactions (request/reply, lock/release). Monitoring is a subscription -- zero-or-one invocations, not exactly-one.
> 
> **Concrete type:** The callback should be a closure captured at registration time, matching EAct's typing:
> 
> ```rust
> type WatchCallback&lt;H&gt; = Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, Address, ExitReason) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;;
> ```
> 
> The `FnOnce` is correct: E-InvokeM removes the entry from omega after firing. The callback is consumed, not reused. `Address` identifies the dead pane (EAct's `v(a)` where `a` is the PID). `ExitReason` is the death information -- richer than EAct's bare PID, justified by the architecture spec's statement that ExitReason carries "stripped disposition tags."
> 
> The `&amp;mut H` + `&amp;Messenger` match the existing handler callback signature pattern used by `OnReply&lt;H&gt;` and `OnFailed&lt;H&gt;` in `dispatch.rs`. This is the Cartesian/linear split: H and Messenger are the Cartesian context; the callback closure itself is the linear (one-shot) part.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Q2: One-Shot vs. Persistent (Re-Arming)
> 
> **One-shot.** Three reasons:
> 
> 1. **EAct semantics.** E-InvokeM removes the pair `(b, v)` from omega after invocation. The monitor entry is consumed. Re-monitoring requires a new `monitor a v'` call. This is explicit in the reduction rule (line 3275-3286): the post-state has omega without the fired entry.
> 
> 2. **Theoretical cleanness.** One-shot callbacks are FnOnce, which is compatible with Rust's ownership model. A re-arming callback would need FnMut, which means the closure is borrowed, which means it's Cartesian (copyable/reusable), which means the callback is part of the handler's persistent state -- it's not a one-shot obligation, it's a subscription. Subscriptions belong in the optic layer (per optics-design-brief, "observer pattern" is handled through filesystem attributes, not messaging). If a pane needs persistent monitoring, it calls `watch()` again in its callback -- the re-arm is explicit, matching `shopSup` in EAct's supervision example (line 3398-3401).
> 
> 3. **Affine gap.** One-shot means the callback is consumed on invocation or dropped on cleanup. Both paths are well-defined. A persistent callback complicates the destruction sequence: do you fire it during watcher death? That's a cascading death notification during destruction, which could re-enter handler methods on a partially-destroyed handler.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Q3: Watcher Dies While Watching -- Is Registration Leaked?
> 
> **No, and the cleanup mechanism is already present by analogy.**
> 
> The existing destruction sequence (looper_core.rs lines 211-223) handles this pattern:
> 
> 1. `fail_connection(primary)` -- fires on_failed for pending requests
> 2. `clear()` -- drops remaining entries
> 3. Handler dropped -- obligation handles fire Drop compensation
> 4. Server notified via exit_tx
> 
> Watch registrations stored in the **server's** routing state (by analogy with the ServiceRoute table in server.rs) follow the same cleanup path. When the server receives `ServerEvent::Disconnected { conn_id }` for the watcher, `process_disconnect` already iterates all state associated with that connection and cleans it up. Watch registrations keyed by connection_id are cleaned up identically.
> 
> Watch registrations stored in the **watcher's** handler state follow the same path as Dispatch entries: step 2 (`clear()`) drops them without firing callbacks. This is correct -- the watcher is dead, so invoking its callback is meaningless.
> 
> **The server must be the authority for watch registrations**, not the watcher's local state. This matches EAct's model: omega belongs to the actor configuration, not the thread state. In pane terms: the ProtocolServer holds the `HashMap&lt;ConnectionId, Vec&lt;(Address, ConnectionId)&gt;&gt;` mapping "who is watching whom." When either party disconnects, the server removes the entries. The watcher's local state holds only an opaque `WatchToken` for cancellation.
> 
> **Critical invariant:** Watch registrations must be cleaned up on **either** side's death. If the watched pane dies: E-InvokeM fires the callback and removes the entry. If the watcher pane dies: the server removes the entry during process_disconnect. If both die simultaneously: both cleanup paths are idempotent (entry removal from a HashMap is a no-op for missing keys).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Q4: Does Adding Monitoring Affect Any Theorem?
> 
> **No.** This is proven in EAct and the proof structure transfers.
> 
> **Preservation (Theorem 4.7 / Theorem 4.10).** The E-Monitor proof case (lines 6591-6668) shows that adding a pair to omega preserves typing because the T-Monitor typing rule requires only that `v : C -&gt; C` (where C is the handler state type) and `a : PID`. No session type is consumed or created. The effect on the typing context is `1 -&gt; sta` (returns unit, session state unchanged). The E-InvokeM proof case (lines 6671-6704) shows that firing `v(a)` in an idle actor produces a well-typed computation because `v` has the handler state type as both domain and codomain, and applying it to the idle value `c : C` gives `v c : C`.
> 
> **Progress (Lemma 4.5 / Lemma 4.12).** The progress lemma explicitly states (line 7242): "`monitor b v` can always reduce by E-Monitor." This is trivially true -- the reduction is local, requiring no synchronization with another actor.
> 
> **Global Progress (Corollary 4.8 / Theorem 4.13).** The Global Progress proof (lines 7326-7340) accounts for monitoring explicitly: "If there exists a zapper thread `zap(a)` where `a` is contained within some monitoring environment omega then we can reduce with E-InvokeM." In other words, monitoring adds a new reducible form (pending death notification) that is always able to make progress.
> 
> **What this means for pane.** Adding monitoring does not affect the existing safety theorems because:
> 
> - The callback's type (`H -&gt; H` expressed as `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...) -&gt; Flow`) has the same structure as any handler callback. It enters the dispatch path identically to OnReply or OnFailed.
> - The callback executes on the watcher's looper thread, within the same catch_unwind boundary. The single-thread sequential dispatch model (DLfActRiS Theorem 4.1: deadlock freedom for single-mailbox actors) is preserved.
> - No new channel endpoints are created. Watch is an annotation on the server's routing state, not a new session.
> 
> The key condition from DLfActRiS (Hinrichsen/Krebbers/Birkedal, POPL 2024, Definition 3.2): the channel dependency graph must remain acyclic. A watch notification is a unidirectional message from server to watcher -- it does not create a response obligation and cannot form a cycle.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Q5: Concrete Types
> 
> ### Wire-level (ControlMessage additions)
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-proto/src/control.rs
> 
> /// Death notification — server to watcher.
> /// Carries the dead pane's address and exit disposition.
> /// Not routed through service dispatch — this is control channel (service 0).
> #[derive(Debug, Clone, Serialize, Deserialize)]
> pub struct PaneExited {
>     pub address: Address,
>     pub reason: ExitReason,
> }
> 
> // New ControlMessage variants:
> pub enum ControlMessage {
>     // ... existing variants ...
> 
>     /// Client requests death notification for a specific pane.
>     Watch { target: Address },
> 
>     /// Client cancels a prior watch.
>     Unwatch { target: Address },
> 
>     /// Server delivers death notification (E-InvokeM).
>     PaneExited(PaneExited),
> }
> ```
> 
> **Why Address, not PaneId.** In Phase 2 (distribution), the watched pane may be on a remote server. Address already encodes `(pane_id, server_id)` and is `Copy + Serialize`. It is the correct identifier granularity.
> 
> **Why ExitReason is safe to send.** ExitReason is already `Clone + Serialize + Deserialize` (it needs to be for `exit_tx.send(reason)` in the destruction sequence). The architecture spec says it carries "stripped disposition tags" -- no internal error details leak.
> 
> ### Framework-level (handler callback)
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-proto/src/handler.rs
> 
> pub trait Handler: Send + 'static {
>     // ... existing methods ...
> 
>     /// A watched pane died (E-InvokeM equivalent).
>     /// Default: no-op. Override to implement supervision.
>     fn pane_exited(&amp;mut self, pane: Address, reason: ExitReason) -&gt; Flow {
>         let _ = (pane, reason);
>         Flow::Continue
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This goes on Handler, not Handles&lt;P&gt;, because death notification is lifecycle (like `disconnected`), not a service protocol. The blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` dispatches it through the same fn-pointer path.
> 
> Add a LifecycleMessage variant:
> 
> ```rust
> pub enum LifecycleMessage {
>     Ready,
>     CloseRequested,
>     Disconnected,
>     Pulse,
>     PaneExited { address: Address, reason: ExitReason },
> }
> ```
> 
> The blanket impl routes `PaneExited` to `self.pane_exited(address, reason)`.
> 
> ### Watch registration (DispatchCtx or Messenger)
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-app, on Messenger (Cartesian -- watch is fire-and-forget)
> 
> impl Messenger {
>     /// Request death notification for a pane (E-Monitor).
>     /// Idempotent: multiple watches on the same target are
>     /// deduplicated by the server.
>     pub fn watch(&amp;self, target: Address) { ... }
> 
>     /// Cancel a watch.
>     pub fn unwatch(&amp;self, target: Address) { ... }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Why on Messenger, not DispatchCtx.** Watch is not a request/reply interaction -- there is no on_reply callback to install in the Dispatch table, no token to correlate. It is a fire-and-forget control message. Messenger is the Cartesian context -- `&amp;self`, copyable reference. DispatchCtx is the linear context for request/reply. Putting watch on DispatchCtx would conflate Cartesian subscriptions with linear obligations.
> 
> **Why NOT a WatchHandle obligation.** Per Q1 analysis. The `unwatch` method is voluntary (like CancelHandle), but unlike CancelHandle there is no one-shot interaction to cancel -- the watch is a standing registration. Making it an obligation handle would mean the handler must hold it or explicitly drop it, which adds noise without safety benefit.
> 
> ### Server-side state
> 
> ```rust
> // In pane-session/src/server.rs (internal)
> 
> struct ServerState {
>     // ... existing fields ...
> 
>     /// Watch registrations: who is watching whom.
>     /// Key: watched pane's (connection, pane_id).
>     /// Value: list of (watcher_connection, watcher's view of target Address).
>     watches: HashMap&lt;(ConnectionId, u64), Vec&lt;(ConnectionId, Address)&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> **Cleanup paths:**
> - Watched pane disconnects (`process_disconnect`): iterate `watches`, find entries where watched `conn_id` matches, send `ControlMessage::PaneExited` to each watcher, remove entries.
> - Watcher pane disconnects: iterate `watches`, remove entries where watcher `conn_id` matches. No notification needed -- the watcher is dead.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Affine Gap Analysis
> 
> | Property | What's lost | Compensation | Sufficient? |
> |----------|-------------|--------------|-------------|
> | Watch callback linearity | Rust allows dropping the watcher handler while a watch registration exists in the server | Server-side cleanup on watcher disconnect removes the entry | Yes (safety preserved, no dangling callback) |
> | Callback execution guarantee | If the watcher's channel is full or closed, the PaneExited message is dropped | `let _ = wh.write_frame(...)` pattern already used throughout server.rs | Conditionally -- the watcher may miss the notification if its channel is full. This is a liveness issue, not safety. Bounded write channel (WRITE_CHANNEL_CAPACITY=128) makes it unlikely but not impossible. |
> | One-shot consumption | FnOnce in the handler callback is enforced by Rust's type system | N/A -- statically enforced | Yes |
> 
> The one concern is the bounded channel scenario: if the watcher is overloaded (channel at capacity), the PaneExited notification could be dropped by `try_send`. This is the same liveness gap as ServiceTeardown delivery (server.rs line 349: `let _ = wh.write_frame(0, &amp;bytes)`). It's already accepted in the architecture. The alternative (blocking send) violates I2 (no blocking in the server actor thread).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Deadlock Analysis
> 
> Watch notification introduces no new deadlock risks. The notification flows unidirectionally: server -&gt; watcher. Per DLfActRiS Definition 3.2 (channel dependency graph), adding a unidirectional edge from "server actor" to "watcher looper" does not create a cycle because:
> 
> 1. The server never waits on the watcher (it uses try_send / write_frame, both non-blocking).
> 2. The watcher never waits on the server for death notifications (they arrive asynchronously on the control channel).
> 3. The existing acyclicity of the server-pane topology is preserved.
> 
> The one subtle scenario is **mutual watching**: pane A watches pane B, pane B watches pane A, both die simultaneously. Both death notifications are posted to the server, which processes them sequentially (single-threaded actor). Each `process_disconnect` fires PaneExited for the other. If the other is already disconnected, the write fails silently. No cycle, no deadlock.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary of Runtime Invariants
> 
> 1. **Server authority.** Watch registrations live in the ProtocolServer, not in watcher-local state. Both-sides-die cleanup is idempotent by construction.
> 2. **Non-blocking delivery.** PaneExited is delivered via the server's non-blocking write path. The watcher may miss it if its channel is full. This is an accepted liveness tradeoff shared with ServiceTeardown.
> 3. **Single-thread dispatch.** PaneExited callbacks execute on the watcher's looper thread within catch_unwind. No re-entrancy, no cross-thread callback invocation.</result>

> </task-notification>

> <summary>Agent "Final EAct invariant sweep" completed</summary>
> <result>I now have a complete picture. Here is the verification report.

> ---
> 
> ## EAct Invariant Verification Sweep
> 
> ### I1 — panic=unwind, Drop fires
> **VERIFIED** (with caveat). `Cargo.toml` default profile uses `panic=unwind`. Obligation handles (ReplyPort, CompletionReplyPort) use `Option::take` in Drop, confirmed by `reply_port_drop_fires_during_panic_unwind` and `reply_port_reply_before_panic_sends_value` tests. **Caveat:** see I9 note below — the destruction sequence itself is only catch_unwind-protected for Notification and Request branches, not Reply/Failed branches.
> 
> ### I2 — no blocking in handlers
> **NOT APPLICABLE** (convention). Marked unchecked in PLAN.md. Correct — not enforceable without timeout watchdog. No regression.
> 
> ### I3 — handlers terminate
> **NOT APPLICABLE** (convention). Marked unchecked in PLAN.md. Correct — same EAct limitation (lines 3068-3076). No regression.
> 
> ### I4 — typestate handles
> **VERIFIED**. ReplyPort/CompletionReplyPort/CancelHandle all use `Option&lt;Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(...)&gt;&gt;` with `Option::take` in both the success method and Drop. `#[must_use]` on all three types. Move-only (no Clone). Confirmed by 14 tests in `obligation.rs`.
> 
> ### I5 — filters see only Clone-safe Messages
> **VERIFIED** (by construction). `Message` trait requires `Clone + Serialize + DeserializeOwned + Send + 'static` via blanket impl. MessageFilter is parameterized by `P: Protocol` where `P::Message: Message`. No bypass path. Type-level guarantee — no test needed.
> 
> ### I6 — sequential single-thread dispatch
> **VERIFIED** (by construction). `LooperCore::run()` is a single `loop { match rx.recv() { ... } }` — one thread, one message at a time, sequential handler calls. No concurrency within the dispatch loop.
> 
> ### I7 — service dispatch fn pointers sequential
> **VERIFIED** (by construction). `ServiceDispatch::dispatch_notification` and `dispatch_request` are called within `dispatch_service`, which is called within the single-threaded `run()` loop. Same thread as I6.
> 
> ### I8 — send_and_wait panics from looper thread
> **NOT APPLICABLE**. `send_and_wait` not implemented. Marked unchecked in PLAN.md. Correct.
> 
> ### I9 — dispatch cleared before handler drop
> **REGRESSION** (partial). The explicit shutdown path (`Flow::Stop`, Notification panic, Request panic) correctly runs `run_destruction` → `fail_connection` → `clear` before handler drop. However, the **Reply and Failed branches in `dispatch_service` lack `catch_unwind`**. If a user's `on_reply` or `on_failed` callback panics, the panic propagates out of `run()`, and `LooperCore` has no `Drop` impl — the destruction sequence is skipped entirely. `fail_connection` and `clear` do not execute. Dispatch entries are dropped without firing `on_failed` callbacks. The `exit_tx` is never notified. The handler's obligation handles still get Drop compensation (I1 is preserved), but I9's specific guarantee (dispatch cleared *before* handler drop in orderly sequence) is violated.
> 
> ### I10 — ProtocolAbort non-blocking
> **VERIFIED** (partial, as marked). `FrameCodec::write_abort` is a synchronous two-write operation (`write_all` for length + `write_all` for `0xFFFF`). Fallible write path exists. Marked as "partial: framing layer provides fallible write" in PLAN.md. No regression from prior state.
> 
> ### I11 — ProtocolAbort at framing layer
> **VERIFIED**. `FrameCodec`: `0xFFFF` reserved for abort. `write_frame` and `register_service` both assert `service != 0xFFFF`. `read_frame` returns `Frame::Abort` on `0xFFFF`. Self-poison (`AtomicBool`) on any read error. Tested by `read_frame_detects_abort`, `abort_with_trailing_bytes_still_aborts`, `service_65534_boundary`, `payload_containing_0xffff_is_not_abort`. All paths covered.
> 
> ### I12 — unknown discriminant -&gt; connection error
> **VERIFIED**. `FrameCodec::read_frame` checks `known_services.contains(&amp;service)` (non-permissive mode). `UnknownService` error poisons the codec (self-poison on any error). `register_service` only adds, never removes. Monotonic known_services confirmed.
> 
> ### I13 — open_service blocks until accepted
> **VERIFIED**. `PaneBuilder::open_service_inner` sends `DeclareInterest` then enters `loop { match rx.recv() { ... } }` blocking for `InterestAccepted` or `InterestDeclined`, buffering unrelated messages. Tested via integration tests in builder.rs.
> 
> ### S1 — token uniqueness
> **VERIFIED**. `Dispatch::insert` uses monotonic `next_token` counter (per-Dispatch instance, not global). `DispatchCtx::insert` delegates to `Dispatch::insert`. Tested by `tokens_are_unique_per_connection`. `fail_session` handles session-scoped cleanup (S1 fix confirmed in both dispatch.rs and the server's `RevokeInterest` echo-back path).
> 
> ### S2 — sequential dispatch
> **VERIFIED** (by construction). Follows directly from I6. Single-threaded looper, sequential handler calls.
> 
> ### S3 — control-before-events in batch
> **NOT APPLICABLE**. No batch processing yet (requires calloop Looper). Marked unchecked in PLAN.md. Correct.
> 
> ### S4 — fail_connection scoped
> **VERIFIED**. `Dispatch::fail_connection` filters by `PeerScope`, fires `on_failed` only for matching entries, preserves others. Tested by `fail_connection_fires_on_failed_for_matching_entries` (conn1 entries failed, conn2 preserved). `LooperCore::run_destruction` calls `fail_connection(primary)` then `clear()`.
> 
> ### S5 — cancel without callbacks
> **VERIFIED**. `Dispatch::cancel` uses `HashMap::remove` — entry is consumed, neither `on_reply` nor `on_failed` fires. Tested by `cancel_removes_without_callback` with panic-on-call guards.
> 
> ### S6 — panic=unwind
> **VERIFIED**. Follows from I1. Same mechanism.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary Verdict
> 
> **18 of 19 verifiable invariants are satisfied. One regression found.**
> 
> **I9 REGRESSION**: The Reply and Failed branches in `dispatch_service` (looper_core.rs lines 320-358) do not wrap user callback invocation in `catch_unwind`. A panic in a user's `on_reply` or `on_failed` closure will bypass the destruction sequence. The Notification and Request branches are correctly protected. The fix is straightforward: wrap the `fire_reply` and `fire_failed` calls in catch_unwind, matching the pattern used by the Notification and Request branches. Alternatively, wrap the entire `dispatch_service` call in `run()` within catch_unwind at the call site (line 424), which would protect all four branches uniformly.
> 
> This is the only gap. All other checked invariants in PLAN.md are genuinely satisfied by the current code. The unchecked items (I2, I3, I8, S3) are correctly marked as not yet applicable or not enforceable.</result>

> </task-notification>
